“THE BULLETIN” BOOK EXCHANGE.
On receipt at The Bulletin Office, Sydney, of Postal Order, Postal Notes, or Stamps (of any province) equal to the price stated (which is published price with
postage added), Books and Periodicals in the subjoined lists will be forwarded to any Australasian address.
J. M. Barrie’s new novel, “The Story of Senti-
mental Tommy,” which commences in Jan.
Ss'iiiisNER S, does not promise well. It is not
sp nit me. m<. and in the over-elaboration of trivial
incident t a- desired haven of humor is missed
Notes.
One hardly looks to Pinero for originality of
thought, but he frequently compasses originality
of expression. Here are some bits from “The
Notorious Mrs. Ebbsmith ’’
How many “coming men ” has one known? Where on
earth do they all go to ?
Oh, Agnes, the agony of success to a nervous, sensitive
man ; the dismal apprehension that fills his life and gives
each victory a voice to cry out “Hear, hear! Bravo,
bravo, bravo! But this is to be your last —you’ll never
overtop it! ’’
Oh, these modern nurses, in their greys, or browns, and
snowy bibs! They have much to answer for, dear Lucas.
Ah, Duke, when you all carry out your repressive
legislation against women of evil lives, don’t fail to in-
clude in your schedule the Unsympathetic Wives. They
are the women whose victims show the sorriest scars;
they are the really “ bad women ’’ of the world : all the
others are snow-white in comparison !
Men don’t relish, I suppose, being cast off by women*
It’s an inversion of the picturesque ; the male aban-
doned is not a pathetic figure.
Dear Bulletin, —“The Old Ma’m’selle’sSdcret’’
is a German novel, the title in the original being
“Das Geheimniss der Alten Mamsell ” (“The
Old Maid’s Secret ”). It is written by a woman
named Marlitt, who is, in the delightful native
phrase, a highly-successful-and-by-the-public-
deservedly-much-beloved. authoress. The story is
chiefly concerned with the adopted daughter of a
respectable shopkeeper, who inconsiderately dies
and leaves her to the tender mercies of.hjj widow.
The girl is badly treated by the family, but ulti-
mately turns out to be a sprig of nobility under a
cloud, and marries the inevitable German pro-
fessor amid a hailstorm of Du and Dieh, there-
after bearing children with true Teutonic rapidity.
In point of literary excellence, Marlitt is about
on a par with Miss Braddon, whom she somewhat
resembles, as far as a traditional German style
can resemble anything. However, she affords a
relief from the eternal “philosophy” of many
German novelists. — W.
“ Back from the- Jaws of Death,” hy “ Yarro-
wee” (Is. 3d.), is a-sensational Sydney publica-
tion which has some ingenuity of construction and
a liberal allowance of blood. The Domainer’s
method of going to bed may be compared with
that of Louis XIV. :—
A pile of papers stood by his side as he sat in the grass,
pipe in mouth. Then Carefully, but deftly, he commenced
operations on one leg ; wrapping around it sheet, after
sheet of paper, commencing at, the foot upwards, in much
the same way that a doctor binds up a broken limb with
lint after the splints have been fixed in their place. This
done, his other leg underwent a similar operation. Then
each having been dialt with separately, both were
encased together by more sheets of paper on the
same principle. The body next received attention. By
a dexterous wriggling of the arms, quite astonishing
to one uninitiated in the process, in a few moments the
task was complete. Many of these paper parcels, with
heads showing up at one end, can be seen in the Domain
during any summer night. Their only pillow is a friendly
elevation of the ground, or the sack in which they have
carried their papers. From the head of each of these
figures in their paper couches, little rings of white smoke
curl up as, at regular intervals, they puff away at the
soothing weed.
Anarchist Andrews issues, at Melbourne, the
first number of Reason, “ a monthly magazine of
social and general philosophy ” (3s. 6d. per year),
which contains more hard thought to the square-
inch than any other periodical on the continent.
Andrews is a purist in style as well as in ethics,
and his reasoning has lucidity and pith.
Sydney Telegraph of Jan. 25 notes in its
“ Books up to Date ” column that—
MacMillan’s [sic] introduce us to the first volume of the
“ History of the English People,” now being written by
John Richard Green, M.A., Fellow of Oxford ; of which
the style is so attractive that the remaining seven will be
looked f<'r, even by the general reader, with genuine
pleasure.
Poor Green ! His “ Short History ” was pub-
lished in ’74 ; his “History ” in ’77-’80. He is a
text-book; a classic ; reprinted a score of times ;
surfeited with the applause of the multitude, the
esteem of critics. And a “great Australian daily
newspaper ” pats him approvingly as a rising
author with an attractive style! If only free
Govt, trains carried the Telegraph across the
Styx, how his ghost (over since ’83) would
chuckle !

Roll Boldrevvood’s next story will have Western
Victoria as a background. Browne used to know
the country very well indeed in his younger days,
but has not been there for many years. He is
now refreshing his memories. Western Vic.
has not hitherto cut much of a figure in literature.
Gordon used t<> ride in steeple-chases there : “ On
the fields of C tier line there’ll be labor in vain
before the Great Western is ended ’’—and so on ;
but the fiction wf -the-distrfct is, so far, limited to
ex-Premier Shields famous Casterton speech with
its unrealised programme.
Henry Lauson's Poems.
Henry Lawson joins “The Banjo” in Angus
and Robertson’s admirable Australian series. In
these two writers, with all their imperfections, we
see something like the beginnings of a national
school of poetry. In them, for the first time,
Australia has found audible voice and characteris-
tic expression.
* * * *
Passing a few verses scattered over our litera-
ture’s single century, the name of Kendall is the
only one to be cited in opposition to this view.
Domett and Gordon were aliens in birth and
thought. The same is true of Stephens, happily
still with us. Their poems are not Australian
poems, but poems about Australia —or Austral-
asia, if it be objected that Domett’s epic deals
wholly with Maoriland (though the literary limi-
tation of “ Australia ” to the continent has doubt-
ful validity). Naturally, they reflect their acci-
dental environment. Domett, feeling the poetic
impulse, apostrophises the Maoris just as he would
have apostrophised Red Indians had his lot been
cast in America. Gordon’s “ Sick Stockrider ’’
might easily have been a “Sick Gaucho ” ; he
could have ridden “From the Wreck” without
leaving England, and with a few verbal changes
he could have written it there. Take away the
title “Convict Once” and the constellations, and
Stephens’s masterpiece is cosmopolitan.
*■ * * *
But Kendall ? Kendall comes closer to the
heart of Australian things, yet does not quite
reach it. For he preserves always his indivi-
duality : not Australia, but Kendall, strikes the
dominant note in Kendall’s verses. So susceptible
a soul could not, indeed, fail to be influenced by
the voices sweet and stern which called him on
every hand. But though these thrilled, they did
not fill him. Australia colored, beautified, some-
times inspired his poems, but never swayed,
mastered, wholly possessed the poet.
* * » *
With Paterson and Lawson it is different. The
men are subordinate to their themes. In Ken-
dall’s best verses —the sonnets: “ To a Mountain ; ”
the personal lyrics—the interest is subjective, it is
brought by Kendall himself. Even in bis descrip-
tive pieces— “ Moohi,” “ < >rara,” “Coogee”—we
are less concerned with tlie scenery than with the
poet’s relation to the scenery. But Paterson’s
and Lawson’s best verses are objective, imper-
sonal. The interest is in the poem, not in the
poet. Kendall, Paterson and Lawson have each
held a mirror to their native land. Kendall’s
reflects his own image and Australia’s ; Paterson’s
and Lawson’s reflect Australia’s.
* * * *
This is true of both, but it is more true of
Paterson. Yet, though Lawson’s later verses are
largely colored by bis own thoughts and emotions,
the bulk of his book is Australian, and it is
when he is most Australian that he is most happy.
This amounts to saying—what is perfectly true—
that his earlier poems are among his best. It is
curious to note the contrast between Lawson’s
optimism of seven years ago and his pessimism of
to-day. In 1889 “ The Ghost ” vanished
. , . as a purer brighter presence trained my side—
“ Heed him not 1 there’s truth and friendship in this
wondrous world,” she cried,
“ And of those who cleave to virtue in their climbing for
renown,
Only they who faint or falter from the height are shaken
down.
At a cynic’s baneful teaching let your lip in scorn be
curled!
‘ Brotherhood and Love and Honor ! ’ is the motto for the
world! ”
In 1895, “ The Ghost” has returned—to stay.
And is it for this damned life (1) vve praise the god-like
spirit that died
At Eureka Stockade in the Roaring Days with the days
when the world was wide?
* * * •
The change of note from Hope to Discontent,
the change of view from impersonal to personal,
have not improved Lawson’s poetry. The less he
broods the better he writes. It was before he
soured that he wrote “ Andy’s Gone with Cattle”
(1888), “The Glass on the Bar” (1890), “When
the Children Come Home ” (1890), and “ The
Drover’s Sweetheart ” (1890), and there is nothing
in his book superior to these. “ The Vagabond,”
which may be taken as typical of Lawson’s later
attitude, is a fine piece, with some splendidly
sonorous stanzas, but—there are so many sucking
Byrons busy at monotonous variations of the
bitter old theme. And the Lawson who wrote
“ The Drover’s Sweetheart ” is unique.
* * * *
Lawson’s shortcomings are obvious enough.
His mental scope is narrow ; he is comparatively
uncultured ; he iterates the same notes, and rarely
improves his thought by elaboration; he wants
harmony and variety of metre; his work is bur-
dened with many weak lines and careless tags.
But how graphic be is, how natural, how true,
how strong ! How he feels, and makes his readers
feel, in poems like “Out Back ! ” when phrase by
phrase is painted an imperishable bush picture.
Lawson’s shortcomings are obvious; but, for
Australians, they are as nothing beside his merits.
And it must be remembered that his verse repre-
sents only half of his work, and perhaps not the
finer half. His prose, brimming with humor and
pathos, is of the best produced in Australia.
* * * *
Lawson’s keen sympathy, his knack of observa-
tion, are characteristically feminine. His sense of
humor, his talent for vivid portrayal, are as
characteristically masculine. Yet it is the woman
in him, as in others, that makes his talent glow
to the white heat of genius. Intell et is great ;
emolion is great; but, for a poet, the greater of
them is emotion. This it is which fires dull
words, turns ore into gold, and, Pygmalion-like,
draw's .with passionate ardor from cold stone
warm, pulsating life. This
adds the gleam,
The light that never was, on sea or land,
The consecration, and the Poet s dream.
His <j .plenty for emotion is Lawaop’s best gift.
It i- because he feels so deeply that he writes so
strongly. His life has been a struggle and it is a
struggle still; but, mayhap, had fortune been
easier, his work would have failed so to move
us. For the oases of literature are watered with
tears and blood, and a people preys upon its poets’
vitals.

THE
Angelus Symphony.
fPHE Symphony is a keyboard instrument for general use, and
is also an automatio instrument reproducing mechanically
the notes of any music supplied. Thousands of pieces have been
prepared for the instrument and new selections continually
added, embracing the writings of the celebrated composers of
the past and composers of the present day, combining classical,
operatic, saored, dat.ce and popular music, all of which can be
peiformed upon the Symphony by a person having no know-
ledge whatever of music. While this is a wonderful advance-
ment over any m< chanical instrument heretofore made, therejs
in connection with the Symphony additional possibilities which
commend it to the educated musician.
The Symphony is fast becoming a factor in the conservatory
of music. The correct reading of a difficult passage in a vocal
c r instrumental piece can be taught more readily to the pupil
from a demonstration upon the instrument than by any other
method of instruction. Practice has proved this.
Every Set of Reeds in the Symphony is operated by the Key-
board ; also every set is brought into use by the Automatio
attachment, whereby the full power of the instrument is
obtained if used either way.

Bards of the Backblocks.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
AT THE BACK O’ BOURKE.
Where the mulga paddocks are wild and wide,
That’s where the pick of the stockmen ride,
At the Back o’ Bourke,
Under the dust-clouds dense and brown,
Moving Southward by tank and town,
That’s where the Queensland mobs come down,
Out at the Back o’ Bourke.
Over the Border to and fro,
That’s where the footsore swagmen go,
At the Back o’ Bourke ;
Sick and tired of the endless strife,
Nursing the bones of a wasted life
Where all the sorrows of Earth are rife,
Out at the Back o’ Bourke.
Whether the plains are deep or dry,
That’s where the struggling teams go by
At the Back o’ Bourke,
North and Southward, in twos and threes,
Bullocks and horses down to the knees,
Waggons dipped to the axle-trees,
Out at the Back o’ Bourke.
That’s the land of the lying light
And the cruel mirage dancing bright—
At the Back o’ Bourke ;
That’s where the shambling camel tram
Crosses the Western ridge and plain,
Loading the Paroo clips again,
Out at the Back o’ Bourke.
That’s the land of the wildest nights,
The longest sprees and the fiercest fights,
At the Back o’ Bourke ;
That’s where the skies are brightest blue,
That’s where the heaviest work’s to do,
That’s where the fires of Hell burn through,
Out at the Back o’ Bourke.
That’s where the widest floods have birth
Out of the nakedest ends of Earth,
At the Back o’ Bourke,
Where poor men lend and the rich ones borrow;
It’s the bitterest land of sweat and sorrow,
But if I were free I’d be off to-morrow
Out at the Back o’ Bourke.
GLENROWAN.
A REFLECTION ON BRERETON’S “RE-
FLECTION ON LAWSON’S POEMS.”
“ Seasons bloom and seasons wither; dark or
bright, they cannot last; ”
I suppose, my noble Brereton, you think that
patter “ fast; ”
But it’s stale as hash-house “resurrection”remnants
of the past.
Let the seasons slide ? Of course, sir; ’cos you’ve
got to and they will 1
Not the seasons are the matter, but the chances
that they kill;
He’s a fool who dreams past chances are the pre-
sent chances still!
Go it, merry, Brerry gallant, go it rattling while
you’re young,
Tell the girls their kisses bellows-up the love-songs
on your tongue,
Tell your mates, and fool yourself, too, that “we
never can go bung.” >
Look around you, Brerry songster, where the
friends are of your past;
Men whose gifts of brilliant promise limned a
future ne’er outclassed;
Read that future in their present. Don’t it make
you stand aghast ?
Dux, who should have been a Potentate, by future
then read clear,
Lurks at corners on the roadway of the daily path
you steer,
Tugs your elbow softly, humbly, as he tries to
“ bite your ear. ”. ...
Same with Vox and Jus and others, who were
leaders in their prime,
And who held, like you, my Brerry, that the pre-
sent is a chime,
That the flow’rs and girls and rainbows are a
merry pantomime.
’Cos their hearts were light and open, opened they
their purses, too,
’Cos they whooped the whoop of “ Now’s-the-
time,” they paid the reck’ning due,
Let us hope, my Brerry buster, such a fate won’t
happen you.
“Seasons bloom arid seasons wither ! ” Yes, but
Man has only one
Set of seasons in his lifetime— Spring and Summer
—then he’s don^j
Then comes harvesting and winter ! Where’s his
chance of future fun ?
If he’s grabbed his pile and kept it, shut his ears
to woes of peasant;
Made his joys from others’ sorrows, shut his eyes
to things unpleasant;
Then he may (if glut permit it in the bloated !)
“ sing the present. ”
Men don’t bloom again when once they start to
wither; Winter’s blast
Never starts the Spring’s soft zephyr in a life once
overcast —
And the wrecks—that’s all the Poets—thus can
only “ sing the past.”
Blood is ichor ! marrow’s callous! present out-
looks make us freeze—
Sing us Wine and sing us Women —time has
always room for these ;
Let our sinned joys boil to thrill us. Sing the
Past; its Memories !

NOTICE.
By sending direct to The Bulletin Office Twenty
Shillings sterling—with postage added where required—
which you may' pay by Post Ofice Order, One Pound
Note, or stamps of any Australian province, you can
have The Bulletin posted regularly to you every week for
a year.
This offer is not open to residents of Sydney. In that
city Tiie Bulletin is not delivered by mail, but is entirely
in the hands of our carriers, with whom we do not wish to
interfere
Cheques, Drafts, etc., and all Business Communications
to be addressed to William Macleod, Managing Director.
Literary Communications to be addressed J. F.
Archibald.
Special Notice.
The whole contents of this newspaper are copyright by
the publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
article, tale, sketch, poem, paragraph, illustration, or
any other part of the contents of this number, is hereby
expressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
‘ Correspondence ” column all contributions submitted -
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other A rticles,
Verse, Short tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or, say, 3000 words, are specially acceptable),
Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper-Clippings. All
communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unavailable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sending MS. to distinctly understand that
we are not responsible for its preservation or transmission.
To Amateurs in Hlach and White.
Anyone supplying to The Bulletin original humorous or
other topical drawings in line or wash, of sufficient merit
and, interest and adapted for direct reproduction, or any
interesting photograph illustrative of a current event, will
be paid for same on acceptance. Any picture containing
a good or adaptable idea, but unfitted for direct reproduc-
tion— i,e,, any picture which has to be re-drawn—will be
paid for as a suggestion only. The conditions under
which such artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.
Copies of The Bulletin can be obtained in London,
after the arrival of each mail, at the office of the British
Australasian Co., Limited, 31 Elect. Street, E.C.
Also in the Transvaal -.—The Standard and Diggers
News Co., Limited, Johannesburg, South Africa.
Also :— R. A. Thompson and Co., 3 Church Street,
Capetown, South Africa.

SATURDAY, FEBRUARY 15, 189 G.
“Socialism in Our Time.”
The feud which has arisen between the
Queensland Labor-party and the nominal
Opposition, and which seems likely to ensure
the Boodle brigade another long lease of
power and plunder, is a regrettable evidence
of the want of practical common-sense among
the men who preach “Socialism in our
Time.” Concerning their sincerity, their
patriotism, their honesty of purpose, and
their courage and enthusiasm there seems
little doubt ; at least, so far as all the avail-
able evidence goes, the Queensland Labor-
party holds, in these matters, the best and
cleanest record in Australia. But it is
seriously lacking in generalship, and, unless
things in the North are very different from
what they seem, it will find, after the
preaching elections, that it has fooled away
a great opportunity by a too uncompromising
adherence to all its principles at once. This
is, of course, only a probability, not a
certainty ; the Labor-party, despite the fact
that it has virtually thrown overboard its
only ally, and decided to play a lone
hand against the most powerful and
unscrupulous Government in the seven pro-
vinces, may come out victorious, for nothing
is so unexpected that it is dead sure not to
come to pass. A-lso, it might have lost even
with the assistance of the Opposition, for
there is hardly anything so probable that it
may not fail to arrive just when it is most
expected. But if the Labor-party wins its
victory will be much less decisive, and if it
loses its defeat will be more overwhelming
by reason of its present attitude ; and there
is just a fair probability that its lamentable
want of generalship may turn what might
have, been a narrow victory into a decisive
overthrow. The present is not the time
when the Queensland democrats can afford to
quarrel over small—or even moderately large
—differences of opinion, and it is the very
worst time in all the history of the province
to demand “ all or nothing.”
» * * *
The odds in favor of the Government are
very heavy. Its party bosses the biggest
financial institutions of the province, and if
the man who opposes them is indebted to
any of these institutions —which have a large
proportion of Queensland in pawn—he is
liable to be sold up if he doesn’t promptly
cease his opposition. All the people whose
souls and assets are mortgaged to the mone-
tary concerns which the Ministerial party
controls understand that they must vote for
the Government candidate, and work for
him, and boycott, as far as possible, all the
people who work against him. The sugar-
planting interest is bound to the Govern-
ment, for on it depends the supply of cheap
nigger-labor. The pastoral interest is also
with the Government, because the Boodle
Ministry is always ready to put down with
the strong hand real and imaginary labor
“insurrections,” and if it promotes these
alleged insurrections in the first place, in
order that it may put them down afterwards,
and thus win the gratitude of the pastoral
class, that fact generally escapes notice. ■'A
long list of “ proposed ” railways has been
dangled before the electors, and the country
knows its Boodle Government well enough
to understand that while the electorate
which votes for it may get its line,
the one which votes against it certainly
won’t. The Queensland Political Association,
of which Premier Nelson is the head, has
been at work for years past, objecting to the
vote of every Democrat who could possibly
be removed from the roll on any pretext
whatever, and the Revision Courts, which
are merely the instruments of the Boodle
Government, have wiped off nearly every
man whom the Political Association objected
to. Public attention has not been much drawn
to these matters in Parliament because Speaker
Cowley —a Government nomi nee, by the way
—appears to promptly silence, or suspend,
or eject any member who too violently calls
attention to the shady doings of the Ministry.
The Government manages the elections on a
mysterious plan of its own, which somehow
seems to give the Boodle candidate a much
better show than the other man ; and among
all these devices the Democratic Party in the
North has a remarkably hard row to hoe.
* * * *
If the Democratic Party can show that it
has a reasonable chance of winning, it will
gain many adherents who won’t throw in
their lot with it while it is merely a forlorn
hope. In any other of the Australias,
save perhaps W.A., the man or the con-
stituency which adheres to the losing
cause seldom comes to serious grief
thereby, except perhaps that the man
who votes with the Ministry has a better
chance of being appointed a policeman than
the other man. In Queensland the man
who openly supports the Labor cause, or is
suspected of supporting it in secret, is
promptly struck off the roll on the ground
that he is dead, or something of the sort; if
he has a billet he probably loses it ; if he
has an overdraft in an institution which can
be “ influenced ” he is probably sold up ; if
he is a merchant or tradesman the loyal
crowd are very likely warned not to support
him in future; if he runs a paper the
Government advertising is apt to leave him
very suddenly. Therefore, a large number
of people, who would gladly support
the good cause if it were managed in
a way which gave it even a reasonable
show of success, will otherwise stand out.
And the conduct of the Queensland Labor-
party, in breaking up its alliance with the
Opposition over some small points of differ-
ence, reduces its chance of success so much
that the people who have something to lose
by offending the Boodle Ministry will at
least give it no open assistance. The aver-
age man doesn’t want to risk his life in a
forlorn hope, and the Labor cause, as now
managed, practically is one. A party which
throws away its chances so recklessly doesn’t
inspire confidence in its own future.
* * * *
The proposed coalition between the Labor
men and the Oppositionists would have pre-
vented them running candidates against each
other, and would have concentrated the
voting power of the two pai’ties everywhere
upon one candidate. Also, it would have
meant that they would return to Par-
liament to present a solid front to the com-
mon enemy ; and as the Labor element
would constitute probably two-thirds of
the coalition it would have much the
better end of the bargain. Now there will
be no mutual support, and the two
parties will fight against each other
at random, and in this miserable divi-
sion of interests the Boodle Government
will almost certainly score a great success.
The cause of the division was that the
Opposition would not give in its adherence
to the whole policy of “ Socialism in our
Time.” It was ready, however, to give its
solid support to the principle of “ One-Man-
One-Vote ” and to the abolition of nigger
and Kanaka labor. Therefore, if the coali-
tion returned with a majority, it could have
abolished the property vote, given the
suffrage to every male white adult in Queens-
land, and sent a bunch of Democrats to the
Upper House to break up the solid Tory
phalanx there. And then, when the next
general election came round, if the country
wanted “ Socialism in our Time,” every man
would have had a chance to vote for it, and
bring it to pass. The Labor-party holds that
the masses in Queensland want its programme
in its entirety, and if so, the only thing which
prevents that programme becoming law is
the plural vote, and the property-qualifica-
tion, and the wholesale disfranchisement of
the people who want to vote in its favor.
The Labor men cannot remove these dis-
qualifications themselves because so many of
their adherents are disfranchised ; therefore,
to secure their removal they should coalesce
with anybody or everybody who can be ie
upon to support One-man-one-vote, and th
give their party a fair chance to assert itse
Their utter inability to see this obvious fact
is such a pitiable instance of stupic l y ’
the cause of the Queensland Labor-party—
unless it gets a new set of leadeis may e
fairly set down as hopeless.
* * *
If the party takes up its present uncom-
promising attitude because it has pledge
itself to insist on its whole policy and on a
its policy at once, as the price of its alliance
with any other political section, then 1
may as well give up the ghost no poli-
tical body ever got all its policy at once, it
it desires simply to show its incorruptible
honesty, then it would be much more useful
if it was less honest and more politic. If its
reason is that its bosses won’t share the
leadership with anybody else, then it is sacri-
ficing the country to its own selfishness
as well as to its own crass stupidity.
And if, as seems probable, it is travel-
ling on the maxim that the truth is mighty
and must prevail, and that because its cause
is a good one it is bound to come out
uppermost sooner or later, and that a year
or two, or a lost chance or two, don’t matter
while time and Providence are on its side, it
is throwing its priceless opportunity away for
the sake of a threadbare moral wheeze
taken out of a copy-book. The truth is
reasonably mighty and it prevails now and
then. The good cause, if it is well-managed,
comes out on top about as often as the bad
one, but the world is covered with the
wreckage of good causes which, not being
well-managed, didn’t come out on top, and
look as if they never would. If the bad
cause has the best generalship behind it, it
has just the same chance of victory as any
other. If the Queensland Labor-party fools
away this opportunity of upsetting the
Boodle Government, its time may come again
in six months or three years or a century—
no one knows. Possibly in the interval of
waiting the Ministry may find some means
of suppressing the Worker, and may
enact a new franchise which will wipe out
most of what remains of the Labor-vote, or the
nigger-laborer may starve most of the balance
of the white workers out of the country, or
the Japanese invasion may begin in real
earnest, and a big Japanese vote may arise
to down the party which wants Queensland
for the white Australians. Or, again, the
cry of “Socialism in our Time” may die
out, as so many other political watchwords
have done ; and the Labor-party may go to
pieces for no visible cause, as other parties
have done, when they had lost their oppor-
tunity. If the country believes in the Labor
programme, then the first, last and only
thing the party wants is manhood suffrage
pure and simple—that gained, the com-
munity will do the rest at next election.
* * * *
Therefore the Labor-party, if there was
even a fraction of ability among its leaders,
should be willing to sink everything else—
even “ Socialism in our Time a thousand
fathoms deep and out of sight for the present,
and go for manhood suffrage alone, with the
help of anybody who will coalesce with it
on that basis. If it fails to do this the
inevitable public conviction must be that
the party doesn’t want to succeed because
success is the worst thing that could happen
to it. In other words, the public idea will
be that it has no constructive ability and is
aware of the fact; that while it is out of power
it can live on the tale of what its leaders
would do if they were in power, whereas if
they were in power their incapacity to do
anything would inevitably break up their
following, and, therefore, they consider it
better to stay where they are. Anyhow
unless some kind of an agreement can be
arrived at with the moderate men of the
Opposition, the present prospects are bad
A great part of the public has learned from
the Government and its lying organs to
regard Socialism, either in our time or in
any other, as synonymous with blood and
butchery, arson, riot, disorder, and whole-
sale destruction of property. If the Labor-
party announced its willingness for the
present to go for manhood suffrage only, and
to leave the rest to the electors afterwards
it would gam many adherents and make a
good beginning at reform. Its insistence on
its whole programme or nothing only scares
the dull, middle-class element into alliance
with the present Government, for fear that
the only alternative thereto is a GovernnS
ral 6 imaginary gory shearer from
the vv est with a leavening of the incendiary
swagman and rouseabout thrown in. J

on the University so that it might offer

cipal source of revenue, viz., the Govern-

To the common intellect, Peacock’s proposal
was clear e.,0ug1,-but the cultured brahi o
Brownless found it mystifying of
The Chancellor sa d the terms of inf+
what ambiguous, a* it did not state whethei'ih'Oo^rn.'
ment would pay the fees of scholars, or whether the Uni-
versity was to forego them.
At present, the Government provides for 12
scholarships for State-school children each
year, and if Peacock simply wanted an exten-
sion of this practice, he would have said so.
Brownless further went on to say that State-
school scholarships were class scholarships, and
by that fact against the policy of the University
—but lie did not say why such abominations
were already tolerated. As the State-schools
are open to every child in the province, and as
every grade of alleged society uses the State-
schools, it is obvious that the use of the word
“class” with the meaning of exclusiveness or
differentiation is a gratuitous misapplication of
terms. Judge a’Beckett concurred in the mis-
use of the word “ class.” He said :
It would be impolitic to send an illiberal response, but
he thought they might shunt it altogether by pointing out
that it was opposed to the policy of the University to
offer prize money to a limited class.
The precious Council concurred, and carried a
motion accordingly. It is to he seen whether
Peacock will coolly endure such a trumpery
piece of special pleading on behalf of the chief
tool of close corporations.
* * . __ * *
The Chancellor was aide to show that con-
siderable sums of money had already been
declined in the same terms :
It was against the policy of the University to grant
class scholarships. For that reason the council had
refused an offer of £30,000 from Dr. Graves to endow a
scholarship for Oddfellows and their sons, and an offer
by Mr. Cameron of £20,000 to endow a scholarship for
pupils from the Western district.
It was not stated that the Government was
consulted before this ,£50,000 was rejected, and
the presumption is that Chancellor Brownless
and the council did it “on their own.” I have
no special sympathy with Oddfellows, and my
soul does not yearn for the “ pupils of the
Western District,” but if some benevolent mono-
polist offered my university twenty or thirty
thousand pounds to educationally benefit girls
with strawberry-marks on their left elbows, or
to provide intelligence for bald old gentlemen,
I would have to find some very special reason
that I don’t know at present for refusing.
The University did not refuse to be the go-
between for Salmon Wilson and his knight-
hood—it took the Wilson .£30,000 and built
the Wilson Hall as a standing monument to a
greedy grabber for class distinction. It lays
itself open to grave and frequent accusations of
being nothing more or less than a tool for
accentuating class distinctions with its degrees
and diplomas. For such an institution to inter-
pose pettifogging obstruction to proposals made
by a duly-authorised officer of the public, is to
abuse a trust it is supposed to hold solely and
wholly in the public interest. For persistence
in such a course of action there is only one
remedy.
» * * *
The official University Calendar is very
considerably a record of class distinctions
beginning with the theological colleges and
ending with the University Masonic Lodge.
Such institutions are paraded and advertised
out of the University grant—but the admission
of certain picked pupils from the State schools
to the existing lectures (a matter that would
not entail extra expenditure of one penny) is
something to be fought tooth and nail as savor-
ing of “class.” To what tricks would Melb.
University treat itself if Parliament did not
hold the purse-strings ?-

PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Raid on the Transvaal.
Australians are aware that the messages sent
from this country to London by the cable-
agencies are frequently misleading—partly be-
cause few people have the genius to compress a
great situation into a few words, partly because
the despatchers generally take their cue from
the dominant party, or the party which shouts
the loudest. South African cables are more
misleading still. It is a part of the policy of
Rhodes, the would-be dictator, to deliberately
influence public opinion in favor of his projects
by means of the press, and he has systematically
brought under his control, or partial control,
the leading newspapers and telegraphic agencies
of South Africa. The result is that it is
extremely difficult for facts prejudicial to his
policy to filter out at all, while facts favorable
to his policy receive extraordinary prominence.
In connection with the recent attack of Rhodes’s
lieutenant, Jameson, on the Transvaal, for
example, the cables gave the impression
that Johannesburg was seriously threatened
by the Boers; that the grievances under
which its citizens smarted were intoler-
able ; that women and children were
m danger, and that Jameson marched only to
protect life and property. But democratic
outh African papers like the Johannesburg
Diggers News, now to hand, put quite
another face on the story. So far from Jame-
son s incursion being the result of a sudden
impulse, it was arranged weeks previously and
meditated long before. Johannesburg feel-
'nS~as the decision of the Australian meeting
to take no part in hostilities hinted—was
strongly adverse to the raid. Jameson relied
on ie promised assistance of the gang of specu-
lators— the leading spirits are Jews-Iwho rule
the Johannesburg Stock Exchange. These
grudge the taxation of their dividends by the

cipheis in their own country by means of repre-

Boer population swooped suddenly down on
Australia, Australia would do the same. The

The Same Rag Again.
Melbourne Beacon, the Foreign-trade and
Single-tax organ, still raves about its alleged
fact that the people of New South Wales are
much better fed than those of Victoria owing to
the blessings of Foreign-trade in the former
province. This paper showed from Coghlan’s
Statistics (page 300, volume I. for 1894) that the
statement in question was a hollow invention,
and the Beacon now returns to the charge and
says The Bulletin’s figures are not there.
Picking out one or two items it says :
Coghlan, on whose statistics The Bulletin scribe pro-
fesses to rely, gives it (the wheat consumption) at 3001bs.
and 3901b5. respectively. . . . Similarly Coghlan gives
the consumption of sugar for 1892 at 93’51bs. for New
South Wales and 90‘7511)3. for Victoria, thus again stamp-
ing as a pure invention the statement alleged to be made
on his authority that “the balance on the sugar item is in
favor of Victoria.”
Now, on the page to which this paper referred
the Beacon’s garbled statistician, and which he
refuses to look at, the wheat consumption is
given as 3601 b. for N.S. W. and 3381 b. for
Victoria, which is a great difference, and the
sugar consumption is returned at 94Jib. per
inhabitant per annum in Victoria against
91 Jib. in N.S. W., which is a distinct balance
in favor of Vic., though the Beacon says it
isn’t. If the Beacon professes to deny the
existence of these figures it makes no differ-
ence ; any one can find them on page 300
of Coghlan’s 1894 Statistics, and to deny the
existence of figures that are open to everybody
is mere drivel. The same table shows, to any-
one who cares to add it up, that the general
assertion about the Cornstalk being better fed
is merely an attempt to play upon the assumed
ignorance of the public. The old table for 1892,
to which the Beacon now refers, if added up,
shows the same thing. All the same, this
fiaper lends its tribute of admiration to the sub-
ime audacity of the Melbourne Foreign-trade
rag. A paper which can flatly refuse to admit
the existence of figures that are open to the
inspection of everybody who is within reach of
the local Public Library, and can denounce the
statement that they do exist as “pure inven-
tion,” and which represents N.S. W. as being a
Freetrade province in ’92 when everybody
knows it was nothing of the kind, has a great
future before it.
A Tory’s Dilemma.
Melb. Argus is fairly “stuck” for a policy.
It would like to be Freetrade, but absolutely
dare not face the abolition of Customs revenue,
and the consequent necessity for direct taxa-
tion. It would like to be Protectionist and
champion the cause of Protected Fat Men, but
to do this it would have to throw over the Free-
trade Fat Men of Flinders-lane. In its dilemma
it flounders from one extreme to another. For
instance, one Bedggood, a boot-manufacturer,
made a complaint against Factories’ Bills—the
Argus at once slavers Bedggood as a heavenly
person. Two days afterwards it says factories
legislation is necessary and Bedggood’s class
are sweaters :
Argus, Feb. 3, 1896.
Praise of Protected Manu-
facturers.
Manufacturers who spend
capital in erecting and
equipping factories become
suspected persons when
they seek to obtain men and
women to work for them.
Tlie harassing espionage to
which they are subjected
might be borne by long-suf-
fering persons (oonscious of
their own desire to do wrong
to no one), but when the
Chief - Secretary wants to
regulate their businesses in
vital particulars the time
comes for protest. Patience
and meek submission then
cease, to be virtues. On
Friday last Messrs. Bkdg-
good and Co. told in our
columns how they were
actually prevented from
carrying on their work by a
Minister of the Crown. . . .
What can the Chief-Secre-
tary possibly know of the
secret intentions or policy of
a trading firm ? They are
the best judges of what is
required in their own busi-
ness, and a Minister who
interferes runs the risk of
looking like a fool.
Argus, Feb. 5,1896.
Attack on Protection.
Factories legislation of
some kind has been admit-
tedly rendered necessary by
the utter and deplorable
failure of the High Tariff to
give the promised abundant
work and good wages in the
trades. The rule has
worked out that the heavier
the duty the greater the
sweating,
As a further complication in its tangled policy,
the Argus adds the 25 per cent, reformed
tariff. It occasionally tells the Freetraders
that this is only a halfway-house to Freetrade,
but it also tells Protectionists that it is the ideal
Protection, because it allows competitive goods
to come in and produce revenue. The Argus
faces North by South, and blushingly confesses
to being an ardent Protectionist, who believes
that a Factories’ Act is required to prevent
sweaters from being interfered with. The
Argus cannot see that the establish-
ment of Protection is something more than
barring the foreigner from our markets : it is
a measure that is incomplete till it provides a
fair wage for work done under fair condi-
tions. Protection implies a bargain with the
manufacturer for equitable conditions of work
and wages —if the bargain be broken by one
party to it, the other has a reasonable grievance.
The Vic. Sweating Board’s report proved con-
clusively enough that inequitable trade-condi-
tions existed in the Protected tailoring and
boot-making trades—though not as badly as in
the non-Protected baking and underclothing
trades. The Assembly passed a Bill designed
to meet all these cases, and the first person
to squeal is the Protected person Bedggood,
with the effect of seriously upsetting the Argus
policy for the current week.
Party Government.
The Tory papers in Victoria and Maoriland
are just now busy abusing their respective
Ministries because each one is accused of being
what the other is not. Premier Seddon runs
his Government, as far as possible, on strict
party lines. These lines are, briefly, that if a
man belongs to the Ministerial party he must
always vote with the Ministry whether it is
right or wrong, and if he belongs to the Opposi-
tion he must always vote against the Ministry
whether it is right or wrong. Any private
member caught thinking for himself is a traitor
and a turncoat. When the Government in-
troduces a Bill it is its duty to insist
on its party voting for it blindly and
consistently, else the Government will resign
and hand over the job to the Opposition,
which will be quite as unmanageable and quite
as autocratic. The private member doesn’t
legislate, has no control over the State finances,
has no opinions except the opinion that he
should say ditto to his leader, and when he
gives up saying ditto to him he simply goes
over and says the same blind, sheep-like ditto
to another man. He isn’t allowed to amend any-
thing, or reject the bad features of a measure and
retain the good ones, or throw out the fool of
the Ministry and keep the other men ;he isn’t
a man—only a ditto. This is Party Govern-
ment in its full state of development, and
Seddon runs his Ministry to a great extent on
party lines. Therefore he is unanimously
denounced by his Tory opponents as an autocrat
leading a “ servile majority,” a tyrant, and a
man who is destroying the principle of Parlia-
mentary Government. Premier Turner., of
Victoria, on the other hand, has practically
abolished the party system. He makes no
demands on the blind allegiance of his fol-
lowers ; he holds that every member has been
sent into the House to express his independent
opinion and act on it ; and he allows his own
party, as well as everybody else, a free hand
to criticise the Government measures and
amend them if they can, and he hardly ever
attempts to oppose an amendment with the
crude argument that he will resign if it is
carried. As long as his party follows him on
general principles he is content ; his measures
are mostly discussed on their merits, not passed
under compulsion with the threat that other-
wise the House will be subjected to the horrors
of a MTntyre Government; and the Minis-
terial majority is made up, as often as not,
with votes from the Opposition corner, or even
with a few from the direct Opposition. There-
fore, Turner, because he doesn’t perpetually
bang down the statement that he will resign if
his party doesn’t support him whether right
or wrong, is described by the Vic. Tory press
as a weak-kneed individual, who is also
destroying the principle of Parliamentary
Government. The party system is really a
howling tyranny, and no politician nowadays
dare carry it out in its entirety, else he would
be overturned in a month. But, still, he is
supposed to carry it out, therefore he is cursed
if he does attempt to enforce it and cursed by
the same people if he doesn’t. And by good
fortune these twin curses are gradually killing
the useless, antiquated system. A thing which
can’t be done and yet can’t be left alone is an
anachronism.
Another Ass Programme.
The S.A. National Ass. has announced its pro-
gramme for the coming elections, and the S.A.
voter is hereby requested to consider it in its
beautiful entirety and see what the Ass has to
offer him :
1. Taxation.—(a) Against the single tax. (6) Against
the progressive and absentee taxes, (c) Against any in-
crease in the land tax.
2. Federation. —In favor of intercolonial Freetrade and
Australian federation.
3. Electoral Law.—(a) Against any reduction in the free-
hold qualification of the electors in the Legislative Coun-
cil. (6) In favor of extending the leasehold qualification
to Crown lessees owning improvements of the value of
£2OO. (c) In favor of the occupying qualification being
extended to joint occupiers whose respective qualification
is over £25 each.
4. Land Settlement. —( a ) In favor of liberal land legis-
lation to promote pastoral or agricultural occupation. (6)
In favor of the sale of Crown lands.
5. Northern Territory.—ln favor of developing the
Northern Territory by permitting the introduction of
suitable labor for sub-tropical cultivation. Encouraging
pastoral settlement and the mining industry, or, in the
alternative, requesting the Home Government to relieve
the colony of its responsibility.
6. Government Economy.—( a ) In favor of reduction in
Government expenditure. (6) Abolition of payment of
members of the Legislative Council, (c) Reduction of
the education vote by the discontinuance of indiscriminate
free education, (d) Reduction in the cost of railway ser-
vice, having due regard to efficiency.
7. State Interference. — (a) Against unnecessary State
interference with production and industry. (6) Against
State interference with the Savings Bank.
8. Open Questions. —Temperance legislation, effective
voting, religious instruction in State schools.
The Ass is definite on some points and lament-
ably indefinite on others. With regard to the
definite ones, it wants to abolish the absentee
tax and the special tax on big landlords, there-
fore the people who live in the province and
the working-classes and the small farmers will
have to pay more to make up the shortage thus
created. It wants to keep the Legislative
Council as close a corporation as possible, and
by abolishing payment of members to ensure
that only ricli men shall be able to sit there—in
other words, it wants to make the Upper House
a strict Tory organisation which will squash the
progressive measures of the People’s Chamber.
Further, it wants to introduce cheap nigger-
labor and Chinamen and leprosy into the
Northern Territory, whence they will slop over
into South Australia proper, as well as into the
adjacent provinces, and gradually oust the
white agriculturist and the white artisan. As
an alternative to this, it desires to make the
Territory a Crown colony, or a colony run by a
British syndicate, which would make the
Federation of all Australia wholly impossible,
and would probably introduce into this continent
a little irresponsible filibustering power of the
African Chartered Co. variety, which might
have one day to be resisted by the other pro-
vinces with armed force. Likewise, it proposes
to abolish free education, and cultivate national
ignorance, and re-establish and consolidate the
fine old banking monopoly and the system of
10 per shent mortgages. It doesn’t promise
to assist the Bible-in-schools party, so there is
no reason why the religious crowd should
rally round it. Also, there is no reason why
the teetotal crowd should rally round it, as it
distinctly refuses to commit itself on the grog
question. And its promises of economy don’t
amount to much, partly because every party
promises economy, so in this respect it offers
nothing that the province hasn’t got already ;
and chiefly because it doesn’t state a single
specific instance in which it is going to econo-
mise, except by saving a miserable ,£4BOO a
year off payment of members and economising
on the spread of knowledge, against which it
proposes to sacrifice a great deal in land
and income tax. Moreover, it doesn’t even
definitely promise to reduce expenditure, it is
merely “in favor of” a reduction, and it is
presumably also in favor of the millennium,
which is quite as definite and quite as much to
the point. It is also “ in favor of ” Federation
and intercolonial Freetrade, but as the Demo-
cratic party and the present Democratic
Government are on that side too, the Ass here
also offers nothing that South Australia doesn’t
already possess. Moreover, as it proposes, by
its Northern Territory policy, to make Federa-
tion practically impossible, this item might just
as well have been left out.
The Bitter Cry of the British Manu-
facturer.
The following pamphlet (written by Bryant
and May) is now interleaved in several English
magazines that come to the Protection-cursed
and Protection-blighted Australia
THE QUESTION OF THE HOUR.
It there be one question of greater importance than any
other, it is
How are Working People to be kept Employed?
Down at Bow, in the East-End of London, is the largest
match-factory in the world, where constant employment
is found for nearly two thousand workers, mostly of the
female sex.
Notwithstanding the best efforts of an energetic staff
to encourage the demand, large numbers of applicants
for work are turned away every day, solely through the
increase of foreign competition.
More than £400,000 worth of foreign matches have been
purchased by inconsiderate consumers in this country
during the last twelve months.
When shall we wake up to the importance of Supporting
Home Industries in matches?
assert that if all consumers in Great Britain and
Ireland would use only their matches, they would be able
to pay one thousandpounds weekly in wages to additional
workers in the East-End of London.
This is the outcry of one of the worst-paid
and most unwholesome of all London’s bad
trades. The firm cries from the deeps of low
wages and low standard of living. A
touch of absurdity lightens the tragedy in
the wretched manufacturers’ howl : “Why
should we not support Home Industries in
matches ?” Just as Reid is Freetrade in every-
thing but sand-shifting and sugar, so these
fellows dare not be heterodox in anything but
matches. But it is significant that in the
country and century of Cobden and Bright
this miserable trade should report the triumph
of foreign competition and the discomfiture of
miserable cro\vds which hang round English
match-factories for the sweater’s dole without
getting it.
The recently-published annual report of N. S. W.
police department upsets several teetotal asser-
tions. In ’95 there were four pubs, less in
Sydney than in ’94, yet there were over 300 more
convictions for drunkenness. This tends to
disprove the case for Local Option, which rests
on the assumption that drinking is in pro-
portion to the number of publicans, or, to use
the pump phrase, “ the consumption of alcohol
varies according to the facilities for obtaining
it.” The hope that, in consequence of depres-
sion and want of employment, drunkenness
would diminish, has proved unfounded, adds
the report. This bears out the anti-teetotal
contention that poverty, instead of being the
result, is oftener the cause of drunkenness.
Lastly, despite increased drunkenness, there
has beeu a diminution in serious crime. The
serious criminal, like the wide-awake gambler,
is generally a water-drinker.
Dear Bulletin, — Be those two similar episodes
in the careers of Mr. Blake, Q.C., and the late
Mr. Aspinall, Q.C., let us charitably suppose
that the miserable little cabal which refused the
use of Melbourne Town Hall to the masterful
Edward Blake did so in ignorance of the man
and his career. Blake was for many years
Canada’s most powei ful political orator. The
rumor, “ Blake is going to speak,” was suffi-
cient to fill every seat in Ottawa House of
Commons. There are no useless frills, there is
no Irishism, on Blake’s rhetoric. Clear, calm,
logical, convincing, he holds his audience from
start to finish. During his Canadian political
eareer he was the one man really feared
by that wily Tory leader, Macdonald.
When the notorious Pacific Railway scan-
dal was unearthed, and Macdonald was
convicted of having drawn on Hugh Allan,
the Montreal steamship man, for large sums
with which to bribe the constituencies, Allan
to be afterwards repaid with the Pacific Rail-
way contracts, it was Edward Blake, with his
powerful denunciations of the corruption of the
Government, and his fervid appeals to the
honor and patriotism of the people, who was
mainly instrumental in thrusting Macdonald
from power. After that chieftain’s overwhelm-
ing defeat, when Blake might have had any
post he chose, from Premier down, he was with
difficulty induced to accept a seat in the Cabinet
without portfolio. Premier, Chief Justice, High
Commissioner for Canadain England—allof these
have been in his grasp, and at his disposal.
The knowledge of this fact has sufficed him.
He has never sought office. He has bestowed
it on others, and probably derived more gratifi-
cation from making premiers and judges and
ambassadors. As for the clap-trap titles that
all the butchers and bakers and candlestick-
makers of Australia are grasping after, a know-
ledge of the number of times that Blake has
brushed contemptuously aside offers of these
“ honors,” so coveted by them, would turn the
whole mongrel pack green with envy. Blake
has never been “ for sale ” either for position or
title, and if for no other reason, he was most
cordially hated by the Tory clique in Canada.
The mouth of Edward Blake, scholar, jurist,
statesman, orator, patriot, padlocked by a
parcel of fat and greasy citizens of Gonebung-
borne !— Kanuck.
Adelaide runs its sanitary department on
much the same basis as Sydney. The accumu-
lated rubbish of the city is mostly taken out to
a piece of waste ground behind the cemetery
and conveniently adjacent to the city, and
there piled up in a mound 150 ft. long and as high
as the man with the shovel can make it. It
has; been gathered there for many years.
When the mound gets too large and offensive
some of it is carted away, no one knows exactly
where, and the process of accumulation starts
again. An unclean hermit has scooped out a
cave for hitnself in the oldest and soundest part
of the awful pile, and lives there gathering
infection in his long hair and his matted beard.
Sydney cultivates a similar mountain of horrors
at Moore Park, and the accumulated rags and
dead cats and meat bones of the city are
shot down there in the hope of a blessed resur-
rection by-and-bye. A few weird outcasts, like
the Eaters-of-Unelean-Things thatdwelt outside
ancient Carthage, live and sleep and dine among
this dreadful accumulation of offal, and gather
infected rags from the heaps to sell to bedding-
factories. Also, pigs root and burrow among
them, and no one knows for certain that the
lank and leprous hog which provides his morn-
ing bacon hasn’t mooned its life away in this
lamentable spot and gnawed the ordinary dead
dog of commerce. Likewise, cows haunt the
same locality and stir up the tuberculous rags
of the dead consumptive and the clothes of the
deceased cancer patient, and bring away
samples of them in the milk. And neither
Adelaide nor Sydney seems to have any definite
idea of abandoning this dirty, primitive system,
and destroying its refuse by fire—the only
reasonable guarantee against the spread of in-
fection. Both cities are only in the second
stage of development. The first stage is that
in which man lets everything drop on the floor.
The second is that in which he throws it out of
the front door to a variable distance and leaves
it there, and after a century of progress a
great part of Australia still throws it out and
leaves it there.
S.M. Herald heavily censures N.S.W. Labor-
party for appealing to the State for “ class
advantages.” “ Already your class is educated
by the State,” it reprovingly observes. What
about the State-paid train which carries the
Morning Herald gratis every day over the
country ? What about the University, largely
supported by the State, and whose hours of
instruction are so distributed that the Herald
class obtain most of its advantages ? What
about High Schools and various other subsi-
dised establishments which, again, the Herald
class take first and longest pull at ? And what
about Government House ? Governor after
Governor has stated that all he gets from the
State he spends in entertaining a class among
whose swallow-tails and inane faces Herald
people are regularly found. “ Class ” advan-
tages, indeed !
“ K.” writes from Tasmania :
Dear Buri/btin, —The writer of “ An Alleged Plan down
South ” evidently has little knowledge of C. H. Grant, of
Tasmania. He says : “They (Vic. Ministers) went to the
most melancholy of Australian railway systems and took
a man front it who, as ministerial head, knew least about
it.” Now, as a fact, Grant has never been connected
with the Tasmanian railwa3' system, the one referred to.
Grant came to Tasmania 20 odd years ago to manage the
Hobart and Launceston railway (known as the “ main
line”) for the English Tasmanian Main Line Railway
Company. From its opening in 1876 until its pur-
chase by Tas. Govt, in ’9O, Grant managed it with
success. For some months previous to its purchase
by Govt., the latter had been engaged in litigation
with the co., during the conduct of which it became
more and more apparent to the Tas. taxpayer that
Grant, a man of many parts, shrewd and business-like,
was, in almost every respect, superior to the successive
Ministerial and Crown Law officers of the province. The
Government lost a hopeless case, and Grant emerged
from the witness-box with his reputation for skill in his
profession and for general outside practical knowledge
enhanced above any other in the island. Then the
Attorney-General sailed for England to conduct an appeal
to the Privy Council; but, meanwhile, the awful
superiority of Grant filled the minds of the rest of the
Cabinet with a fear that he would proceed to score several
more points upon them in unexpected quarters, and a
hurried bargain eventuated in the Government purchasing
the line from Oct. 1, ’9O, at a very profitable figure for the
co. Grant began his Parliamentary career in June, ’92,
when he was elected one of the M.’sL.C. for Hobart. Two
months after his election he became Minister without
portfolio in the Dobson Ministry, and since its downfall in
April, ’94, he has been a private member. The writer of
the article, tabulates the average railway losses of the
various Australasian provinces for ’92-3, showing Tas-
mania’s as the highest, and after pointing out the high
ratesof Tas. railway fares andfreights continues: “Theonly
knowledge that Grant could claim was, therefore, the
knowledge of a bad system for the badness of which he
was in a remote degree responsible.” During the years
’92-3, Grant had no connection with railways in Tasmania
other than that of Minister without portfolio. And that
is the nearest connection that has ever existed between
him and the Tasmanian railway system. On the purchase
of the Main Line by Govt, it fell into the “ system ” under
the management of the Government General Manager of
Railways (Mr. F. Back). It is still worked without loss,
as is also the Western line. It is such white elephants as
the Oatlands, the Derwent Valley, the Chudleigh, the
Sorrell, and the Apsley railways, that more than eat the
profits of other lines. Those are the bad parts of the
system, and it is precisely with them that Grant was
never connected, and for the badness of which he cannot
be even in “ a remote degree responsible.”
Hear Bulletin, — Re rifle-companies as a
means of producing sharpshooters, I doubt the
experiment proving a success, as wherever such
companies are formed, the local solicitor,
bank - clerk or civil - servant will officah
the corps, and social distinctions will keep out
the very men likely to become expert with the
rifle. I refer to the bush-workers. The high
prices at one time obtainable for marsupial
scalps and skins turned thousands,of our bush-
men’s attention to the use of modern firearms,
and the skill then acquired under the most
favorable circumstances for bringing out guerilla
riflemen is still with the men who wander
throughout the interior; for, although the prices
now paid make the trade none too profitable,
it is still followed up in the slack season
after shearing and harvest. Moreover, up-to-
date rifles are to be found in almost every bush
hut now, where, 12 years ago, one might occa-
sionally only see an obsolete muzzle-loader, so
that the younger generation are familiarised
with good weapons as soon as they are strong
enough to hold them straight. In the boom-
times I travelled over most of N.S. Wales for a
skin-buying firm, and visited hundreds of
camps, and often witnessed magnificent shoot-
ing when the inevitable argument as to which
was the best make of rifle cropped up, at 200,
300 and 400 yards. The target, usually an
empty 11b. powder - canister fastened to a
sapling, was literally punctured into sections
as each shooter proved what his rifle could do
in his hands. Misses were not put down to
defectne p iwder, fishtail winds, or the weather

A glance at the just-to-hand M.L. statutes for
’95 session shows that the local democrat is up
and doing. Most noticeable, and probably
most far-reaching in its consequences, is the
Adoption of Children Act drafted on the lines of
Roman law. Besides its ordinary use, it will
probably be widely resorted to as a means of
removing the effects of illegitimacy from chil-
dren. The act is a deliberate slap in the face
of English common law, which has always
fiositively refused to recognise adoption (per-
rnps for good reasons, who can say ?). The
Family Homes Act does for the poor what the
marriage and other settlements already do for
the rich—saves the Home from the mortgagee
and assignee, on an equitable basis. The
Wages Attachment Act keeps small wages from
the garnishee. The Servants Registry Offices
Act should prove a useful leech-killer and might
well be adopted in Australia. The Corrupt
Practices Amendment tries to limit Parlia-
mentary election expenses to £2OO. The
Evidence Amendment contains novel clauses
defining “proper” and “ improper” questions
with reference to the bullying cross-examiner.
It also protects confessions to “ ministers ” and
medical men, but rather injudiciously extends
the admissibility of confessions extracted by
detectives, &c. The Property Law Consolida-
tion Act contains a most useful provision allow-
ing payment of mortgage money to the Treasurer
when the lender is out of the country. The
Reprint of Statutes Act is a preliminary to an
extensive consolidation. But it contains
references,to “obsolete” statutes. Are there
any such ? If so, should there be ? How draw
the line between “ blue ” laws and laws not
blue ? Adulteration, shop assistants, and cre-
mation are also legislated on. Altogether, the
most interesting and progressive set of Aus-
tralasian statutes of the year.
Sir Tit-bits Newnes lately bought London
Weekly Dispatch with a view to converting it
into a “ good ” Sunday paper. English Sabbath
journalism up to now has always been more or
less radical— i.e., evil —in its tone. Newnes, the
glorified pirate, will endeavor to furnish an anti-
dote to the jioison, and earn a peerage.

Hawke’s Bay (M.L.) Herald of
Jan. 14, which doesn’t like The Bulletin
and loses no opportunity of saying nasty
things about it, steals without acknowledg-
ment from this paper five paragraphs,
occupying about half-a-coluinn of space.

A Geraldton correspondent tells The
Bulletin that he has just sold a full-sized
billiard-table, &c., to a party of Germans
who are taking it to Lawler’s, 2fco miles
past Cue goldfield. “ I have never heard,”
he says, “of a billiard-table travelling so
far by road. From Mullewa, the end of
the line, to Cue is 220 miles, from Cue to
Lawler’s 260—in all 480 miles by road, and
not a good one for water. The Germans
deserve to make money after such an un-
dertaking.”
Here is Frank Mahony’s idea of
Henry Lawson—the bard of the Man Who
Walks, as “Banjo” is of the Man Who
Rides. The Bulletin doubts if Lawson
was ever on a horse in his life, but he has
“ humped bluey ” over many a sultry
stretch of country “out back.” Mahony
has caught him in the act, and the presenta-
tion is lifelike —though the dog, this paper
fancies, is a bit of romance ; it doesn’t look
like the dog Lawson writes about. “In the
Days When the World Was Wide and other
Verses,” Lawson’s book of poems (to which
Mahony’s sketch is prefixed), is now ready
(ss. 6d. post free from this office), and Aus-
tralians who like reading some of the best
Australian verses ever written can only do
better than buying a copy by buying two.
The scandal of overcrowded passenger-
steamers has at last increased to such a pitch that
the owner-ridden Australian Marine Boards re-
luctantly take notice of it. Two or three captains
have, therefore, been fined about «£2O apiece—
which, distributed among, say, 200 passengers at
£5 per head, amounts to a risk of exactly 2 per
cent, on the gross returns. Auy intelligent cap-
tain, therefore, who has be a in the habit of
carrying 100 passengers more than his certificate
permits, will in future carry 104—and pay his
over loading insurance premium by further over-
loading. This paper considers that the ‘ ‘ fine ” for a
second offence should be one month without the
option. But probably the Marine Boards won’t
look at it like that.
Sydney, February 11, 1896.
My Dear Moor ybinda—
The gushing old maid who hugs a wheezy pug
to her bony bosom had better take notice of a late
suburban excitement. Two stalwart policemen
unearthed a box of bones in the back-yard of a
lady who had moved away somewhere else, and
who was at once suspected of being a disciple of
Mrs. Makin. The bones were reverently carted
to the proper authorities, who held on to them
with a stern expression until the city coroner
arrived, and solemnly handed them over to a
medico. He was getting ready to deliver a long
Latin opinion, Yvhen a breathless neighbor arrived
with the news that, some time ago, the pet-dog of
the occupant of No. 6017963'021 died, and was
buried in the yard. Even then, the bones were
viewed with the cold eye of legal suspicion until
the doctor discovered a tail among the remains.
The moral is that, if you yvill give your pets Xtian
burial, be sure the tail is included.
Apropos of buried animal pets whose skeletons
engender suspicion in the mind of Policeman X.,
it once fell to the late W. B. Dailey’s lot to prose-
cute a woman for infanticide, based on a myste-
rious collection of bones discovered in a front-
garden. The case was going very badly for the
accused, and symptoms of rope were visibly appa-
rent in the countenance of His Honor, when
zoologist Krefft turned up in an excited state with
an explanation, which, not being able to put it into

everybody’s amusement, until the befogged Bench
saw the application of his gymnastics and let the

So that recent suburban case has historic prece-
dent, and will probably happen yet again.
Nobody who has a pet buried on the premises can
feel really certain that she isn’t on the eve of
being arrested for some Makinesque crime. The
only safe way to bury a pet is to have witnesses to
the ceremony, and signed certificates to the effect
that Grimalkin has been well and truly laid under
the apple-tree; people who haven’t taken these
precautions tremble in their tan hoots. I know
I do. I have the mortal remains of Toby, a pet
wallaby, buried under the rose, and Heaven only
knows what may happen if ever his bones are
spaded up—especially if any of his tail-joints are
missing.
Sydney Anglican cathedral, beautifully deco-
rated for the occasion, was crowded to the doors
on 4th inst., when pretty Miss Maud Roberts
became Mrs. Stanley Meylius. The sacrificial
robes were of cream satin, with trimmings of real
lace and a lower-cut bodice than one usually sees
at a wedding ; while a necklace in floral enamel set
with diamonds, and a handsome bridal posy (the
bridegroom’s gift) made becoming etceteras. Her
four maids wore strawberry-pink and green silk
respectively, the two elder bridesmaids, wearing
Gamsboro’ gowns with large Gainsboro’ hats in
black velvet with pink and black feathers, and
carrying floral crooks. The bridegroom, who is
fair-haired, wore a deeper shade of mauve in his
tie than is usual —English fashion, I suppose. The
going-away gown was an artistic combination of
heliotrope figured crepon, with fixings of cherry-
colored silk and ecru lace ; and a hat of toast-
colored straw with crimson flowers completed a
stylish ensemble.
A Maoriland hen-convention (it calls itself a
“ Women’s Social and Political League ” on its
notepaper) came to one sensible conclusion at the
end of a year’s jaw-jaw, when it rose and hurled
its anathema at “ the great injustice caused by
wealthy ladies working articles for bazaars and
sales of work, thus depriving their needy sisters of
a means of livelihood.” To carry the war into the
enemy’s camp, copies of this anathema were sent
to a baker’s dozen of local clergymen, none of
whom had enough sophistry to invent a plausible
excuse—at any rate, no answers have been
received up to date. The Church in Maoriland is
peculiarly easy-going and rapacious. It doesn’t
object to holding a show in aid of its special brand
of soul-cure on the stage of an ungodly playhouse,
for instance. Most parsons restrict the amateur
high-kickist (who shows a great deal more of her
petticoat than the other sort) to the holy platform
of the Sabbath-school.
Apropos new woman—if the Bloomering cycliste
gets over 14th Feb. without wanting to break
somebody on her wheel it won’t be the fault of the
ugly-valentine artists, who have turned out a par-
ticularly strong sample of invective against the
female Ixion. She is called indecent, insane,
ugly, old and ludicrous in more or less blistering
samples of verse, and I pity the husband of the
wheelswoman when the postman weighs in with
the mail on St. Valentine’s Day.
Sydney is to have a French library under the
auspices of the Alliance Franjaise, and the
Consul-General, M. Biard d’Aunet. It is to
be temporarily housed (from Ist prox.) at
the French Consulate, and will be well stocked
with French fiction, classics, history, travel, and
so forth. For five guineas one becomes a per-
petual member; one guinea per annum is the price
of full membership; and there are no extra charges.
And, talking of French, Madame Juliette Henry’s
Cercle hasn’t lost ground during the short holidays
—her first re-union of the new term last Wednes-
day was largely attended and a great success.
New-chums like to rub the saltpetre in occasion-
ally. A Johnny-come-lately was unusually com-
plimentary, even effusive, over the evidences of
money observable in Sydney. But he carried the
usual sting in his tail. “ Ai’m not a bit surprised,
yer know, at the money some of your trades-
lieople make. Ai quite expected to find your
ocksmiths, for example, in quite a flourishing
state, ’pon me honor. ”
From W. H. Paling and Co. comes a piano solo,
“Memories,” by Florrie Clarks, and dedicated to
Lady Clarke. “ Memories ” is a study in common
or garden arpeggios, but without the bedrock of
melody which separates the “ piece ” from the
“exercise.” As good practice in straddling all
over the gamut “ Memories ” has much to recom-
mend it. ‘ ‘ Sowalde ” waltz, by Helen B. Harding,
is a somewhat insipid and purposeless dance-tune,
running to eleven pp., and more or less in five flats ;
and a song, “Forget Me Not,” sung by Ada
Colley, and composed and written by J. R. Gor-
ing Thomas (not A. Goring Thomas, the English
song-composer), depends for its success on the
amount of passion in the voice of the singer.
“ Forget Me Not ” is rather see-saw, I regret to
say. Why doesn’t a genius come along so that I
may gratify my inherent longing to distribute
nothing but marmalade? I don’t want to find
fault with people’s music—-I want to be kissing
my hand at genius once a week.
Apropos of music, the Triad is a Maoriland
journal which dedicates its little self principally to
the worship of Euterpe, and issues a high-class
musical supplement with each number. In the
two which reach me are included a Mazourka of
Chopin ; Sarabande (Corelli), and a duet from the
Fairy Music of Mendelssohn, besides one or two
classic songs. The Triad evidently has music in
its soul, and I hope it will meet with every
success.
Weigel’s Journal oe Fashion for February
naturally hasn’t much news to impart, for Autumn
isn’t within cooee yet, and summer fashions are a
novelty with the edge off and a coating of dust on
the hem, so to speak. The journal announces in a
depressed kind of way that basques will be smart
for winter—but the greatest horror in the book is
that cardboard fancy-work is coming in again.
Of all the useless tricks in the realm of fancy-
work, cardboard-teasing is the worst. A night-
mare of my infancy is a cardboard “ travelling
purse supposed to be a fitting receptacle for one’s
cash en route. It had cardboard sides feebly
designed with a struck-by-lightning sort of flower
apiece and joined with pink silk veiled in tulle—a
silk card provided the handle—making a most
useful article to put firmly away in a drawer. I
thought the feminine world had got beyond show-
ing such flabby and useless results of so much time,
but it seems that the era of glorified band-boxes is
on us again. How I pity all engaged young men,
who may now expect cardboard-braces and smok-
ing-caps !
Talking of engaged young men, it may interest
a certain tuneful and attractive comic opera singer
now appearing with much “ eclaw ” in Australia,
to learn that the Australian novelist, Guy
Booth by, is now a Benedict with a rich wife.
One time the warbleress above-mentioned was
engaged to Mr. 8., to whom she gave the merci-
less mitten —but that was before his books began
to sell. And I see that Gilbert Parker, the man
who dramatised “Faust ’’and gave the devil all
the limelight, and didn’t write that memorable
S.M. Herald introduction to a Melb. Cup report,
is newly-married to a gilded Yankee girl. All
literary America was at the wedding.
The Sydney Town Hall meeting of old volun
teers brought to a head by ex-Premier Dibbs, tc
which flocks of back-number warriors came dis
playing their medals, reminded me of the crone’s
chorus in “Dorothy,” altered to “ Sogering isn’t
what it used to be.” . . . . “Better than
that we can fight you’ll see.” And on top
of all comes a cold douche of criticism from
“a high military authority.” “ The value of the
proposed volunteer reserve would be practically
nil from a military point of view.” And then the
H.M.A. goes on to blightingly remark in print
that if the late Premier had always been of so
belligerent a humor he mightn’t have found it
necessary to enrol himself and his rusty gun.
Evidently the rough passage the military esti-
mates had through Parliament is neither forgiven
nor forgotten. At the same time, High Military
Authorities are to be received with caution. The
H.M.A. kind of man used to laugh at the Boer
militia, for instance, because its goose-step wasn’t
perfect.

purpose entertaining Major-General and Mrs.
