brilliantspectacle isa foregone conclusion whenever
the picturesque military and naval element organ-
ises a function.
Not a playgoer all over Australia but will feel
an intense desire to dress in sackcloth and ashes
on hearing that the Brough and Boucicault partner
ship dissolves at the fag-end of June next, when,
in all probability, the gifted “ Dot ” hies him tc
Lunnon, which is about the right-sized village foi
an actor of his ability. I have only one fault tc
find with Dion, junr. ; occasionally he pronounces
“Mrs.” as “Mishes” —as if he had a cold in his
head.
I hope that the dissolution of partnership won’t
deprive us of the solace of the B. company. 1
write in a solemn spirit with a crape-wreathed
pen on this most serious subject. The B. and B.
co.—in spite of a dogged preference for one lead-
ing lady, whether the part to be interpreted fits
her like a glove or a No. 10 Wellington boot—is a
charming aggregate, with plays always up to date,
and smartly-dressed into the bargain. Mrs.
Brough, by the way, patronises the same Sydney
frockist whom Maud Hobson overwhelmed with
her hunger for gew-gaws. But when you come to
realise that, if Mrs. B. doesn’t hold the co.
together, we have only W. and M. left between us
and the deep sea of over-painted melodrama,
where the villain evidently goes to bed in full
evening-dress, and individual attention is given to
the groans of the wounded—then you feel that, if
Boucicault is permitted to escape to the Big
Smoke, somebody ought to hobble Brough as a
guarantee of good faith.
The “ wibble-wobble ” is becoming the curse of
our vocalists. Introduced by a lot of ill-taught
singers, under the title of tremolo or vibrato,
it has insidiously destroyed more good voices
than enough. Santley detests it, and says,
“Whenever I hear a singer introducing the
tremolo, I know that voice is on the sure road to
ruin—nothing can save it.” The disease is parti-
cularly bad in Sydney, and is daily getting
worse. In nine cases out of ten it is nothing
but a senseless affectation, which soon becomes an
unconquerable habit, utterly destructive of purity
of tone and voice. How is the awkward fact tc
be got over that Patti, Melba, Yere Sapio,
Antoinette Trebelli, and dozens of the musical
planets hate and abhor it, and seldom, if ever, ust
it, unless to illustrate certain degrees of expres-
sion ? But Sydney vocalists know better than the
greatest of the great of the musical firmament.
The wibble-wobble is practised—and performed—
by sopranos, contraltos, tenors, and basses—they’re
all in it! J
Hobart young men, I am told, are much enraged
over some recent local dances, to which none of
them were bidden ; maledom being represented
by officers off the warships now in port. When
dancing commenced there were hardly any officers
present, and the ladies had to sit and look on —
some even went home again. The lady of the
house went round and apologised for the non-
appearance of the men. And now a number of
the Hobart youths declare they will refuse the
next invitation they get.
Yours affectionately,
SAPPHO SMITH.

Opera House Rickards’ Variety Co.
That dear old friend, the man who called
about a hat, is in evidence this week at Sydney
Criterion. It is years since Bosco Blithers
last called about a nat in Sydney—except when
some casual amateur co. has grievously mauled
Craven’s fine comedy—and “ Mixed ” has become
new again to a great number of modern play-
goers. It also bears the reputation of being a
lucky comedy, and deservedly so ; more than one
season that was fast going to decay has been
restored to prosperity by the arrival of the
astonishing Blithers in search of his hat. Mr.
Wilson has staged the piece remarkably well;
the Cri. is just large enough to hold it, and
not so large that the maundering of the professor
of elocution gets lost among the vasty recesses
of the roof ; and the co. is evidently getting accus-
tomed to play together and works remarkably
well. Ralph Roberts’s Blithers is very nearly as
good as that of the lamented Phil. Day, and his
metallic voice suits the character. Shine, as the
parent who dined out with a midnight female and
afterwards struck a stranger with his eye, is a
good elderly prodigal, and a wild Irish person
with red hair excites a good deal of approval.
Some of the ladies whom The Bulletin com-
plained of earlier in the season have retired, over-
whelmed with this paper’s just disapprobation,
and the new ones are a decided improvement.
Altogether, Blithers should keep on calling about
his hat to good business for the next three weeks
at least.
** * *
“To the West,” the perfectly new melo-
drama which was staged last Saturday at Her
Majesty’s, is not a production which the public is
expected to take seriously. It is a wildly incon-
sequent affair which perpetually breaks out where
it is least expected, and it goes with a furious
whiz from beginning to end, like an escaped
elephant rushing a dynamite factory, with
Coherence running for its life in front and the
mangled remains of Probability trailing behind.
Nobody with any semblance of a sense of humor
could help enjoying it right through its five acts.
It is one long, painted razzle-dazzle, with joy—-
not always the kind of joy that was intended,
perhaps—in every line. It is utterly irrespective
of time and space, and climbs over everything that
comes in its road, except those things that it goes
straight through. It shows Dampier in evening
dress on board an ocean liner; Dampier in gaol
doing 14 years ; Dampier delirious in the desert
finding gold in the vicinity of Coolgardie; Dampier,
later on, as a miner in a caved-in shaft; Dampier
as a sundowner with a mosquito-net round his hat
and a vacant expression in his eye ; and Dampier
as many other things. A big audience welcomes
him with enthusiasm as all these things, and is
especially enthusiastic when Dampier, with a
<£soo reward on his head, holds forth at con-
siderable length, and with an expression of mild
drivel on his open countenance, re the subject of
sheep to the villain who is out looking for him.
There is a lot of genuine comedy in Dampier’s
essay on sheep, and when he isn’t holding forth
on sheep he makes the impossible hero look as
possible as circumstances will admit, which is
saying a good deal. Still that hero might look a
lot better, but on the opening night everything
went awry, curtains came down sideways or else
refused to come down, and the things that didn’t
come down sideways mostly hung fire and wouldn’t
explode.
* * * »
A lot of other people ramp round along
with Dampier in the sandy-blighted land of West-
ralia. Alfred Woods does another excellent
aboriginal. Rolfe, Johnson Weir and Hodson fill
sundry miscellaneous characters creditably, and in
place of the usual funny man, this prodigal drama
turns on three —Tolano, Watson, and the always-
gratifying Leston, besides others who drop into
comedy incidentally. Also, the beautiful Mrs.
Harry Marshall makes her first appearance at Her
Majesty’s, and in addition to playing her part
admirably in a cheerful, butterfly fashion, she
throws in a musical voice which is always audible,
even above the riot and moral combustion. On
Saturday night the shilling place fell in
love with Mrs. Marshall on the spot, and the
eighteenpenny place-held itself back with diffi-
culty. The rest of the co. is mostly swept jtway
in the excited rush of the drama, which is a large
and improbable shriek about everything, with an
explosion in it, and a dreadful tragedy on a cliff,
and a ship, and a row in a gaol, and deserts,
horses, salt lakes, condensers, sheep, and a general
tearing-up of the uni verse by the roots. And when
one gives up trying to find out why everything
happened, which he naturally does, as nothing
seems to be the cause of anything, it is a very
interesting production which everybody should
see, with a wet towel round his head as a pre-
caution against delirium and sunstroke.
*# # *
“Mrs. Ebbsmith” and the 8.-B. Co.’s
season at Sydney Lyceum end together this week.
The co. will re-appear in Sydney some time in
April and will run a season at the Criterion until
June. Then this valued firm dissolves, Boucicault
going to England in search of a wider field for his
talents, while Mr. and Mrs. Brough run a re-
organised co. on their own. Australia will drop a
tear as large as a grand piano over this regretable
severance. Brough and Boucicault have run for
many years the best co. in these parts, and one of
the best to be found in these or any other parts, and
though it will probably still be the best co. in
Australia under the senior partner’s sole manage-
ment, still half the,firm isn’t the whole of it, and
Boucicault’s place will be very hard indeed to fill.
Moreover, if the firm gets into the habit of going
away to England the disease may spread and the
whole concern may go away bodily some day, and
when that happens this country will no longer be
worth living in. Therefore, The Bulletin proposes
organising a society for the forcible detention of
Boucicault, and taking up subscriptions with that
laudable end in view. The society will guarantee
Dion G. a pension of .£1000 a year over and above
his earnings so long as he continues playing in
this country and sticks to comedy, and an 8-ton
granite monument, and a tremendous funeral, and
a most beautiful obituary when he dies. And if
he insists on going, despite this offer, the society’s
masked emissary will have orders to slaughter him
in a dark place oh the night of his farewell, so
that if Australia can’t have its Boucicault no one
else shall. The committee which organises the
farewell benefit wjll secretly make a chalk-mark
over the beneficiary’s vital spot, while it embraces
him on the stage,.so that the emissary, who will
plug him as he goes out, ,ean make no mistake as
to the place to aim at. Australia has lost so many
things of late, including four big banks and J. B.
Patterson and Carbine and its faith in the efficacy
of prayers for raili, that it won’t part with its best
comedian without a struggle.
* * * *
The ever-welcome Comic Opera Co. com-
mences a six weeks’ season at Sydney Lyceum on
Monday with the “ Yeomen of the Guard ; ” the
“ Gondoliers ” and “ Ma Mie Rosette ” to follow.
Also, the Firm promises “Miss Decima,” which is
altogether new to Sydney, and probably “La
Belle Therese.” The co. is unusually strong this
time, including Courtice Pounds, Wallace Brown-
low, Lauri, Howard Vernon, Bracy, Florence
Young, Juliet Wray, the long-lost Flora Graupner,
Marietta Nash, May Pollard, and a host of others.
The “Work Girl”—the drama with the
partially naked Jew in it —is in its fourth week at
Sydney Royal and still doing well. The manage-
ment now advertises it as “the great ladies’
drama,” which is probably its amusing way of
putting things.
* * * *
Sydney Music Hall has laid on a new
ventriloquist, who can represent the ocean moan-
ing on the sands, or a lost dog in the next street,
or a person in an adjacent suburb pounding on a,
Eubhc-house door and trying to get a drink after
ours, or a fat man jammed under a gate, or
almost anything else down to a fried egg in the
distance, or the sound of the editorial gum-pot in
the far West. The other 14 or 165 artistes are all
keeping well and conducting themselves respect-
ably.
* * *- *-
Alice Leamar re-appeared at the Tivoli on
Saturday, and John Coleman—he of the wildly-
improbable legs—is to show there next Monday,
and, in consequence of these things, Sydney now
takes its vittles a good deal more regularly than it
did last week.

Some verses suitable for “Djin-Djin”
were offered to the Firm last week through the
post. The writer suggested that they should be
introduced by Miss Young at the end of the first
act, in which case, as he also suggested, the
management might wipe out the obligation with a
couple of tickets for the show. To this end the
poet wound up with his name and address, taking
care, however, to give the G.P.O. as his address.
Perhaps he was anxious to keep his dwelling-place
a secret lest newspaper interviewers should come
bothering him for his photo. These are the
verses :—
Up to now the devil Djin-Djin hasn’t been assisted
by a page, but presuming that a page were worked
into the libretto, the above ballad set to dreamy
music ought to make a sensation. J. C. William-
son states that literary ability has never been so
marked in Melbourne as now. Every post brings
about half a cwt. of bright ideas in prose and
verse, all for Djin-Djin, and each of them offered
in exchange for two tickets or one private box.
In fact, he has often thought of having an authors’
night at the Princess’s, when the lr use would bo
crowded with people who have forwarded little
things of their own.
* * * *
“The Sixth Commandment,” at Melb.
Alexandra, is about the most lurid and blood-
thirsty outcome of Dan Barry’s management, so
far as The Bulletin can remember. It is rumored
that two or three bishops formed themselves into
a syndicate for writing the modern drama in
question, which probably explains why the plot
revolves on a vicar’s awful villainy. Ordinary
playwrights continue to believe a good deal in
parsons. Sometimes they depict an unhappy
chaplain with a bottle to his lips, or permit an
aged rural dean to refer to his unhallowed early
career, but such cases are rare on the stage, and
when found they are associated with deep peni-
tence. When bishops collaborate in drama-
tic literature, they, being better informed,
have no compunction about placing a mur-
derous apostle in the foreground and leaving him to
work out his own damnation. Augustus Glover is
the soiled sky-pilot of this brisk narrative. A
long-lost will and all unsuspecting child stand
between him and wealth, therefore Rev. Augustus
devotes all the time he can spare from church
business to the task of tracking the coveted papers
and destroying the infant. Frequently the bet-
ting is six to four on Augustus. For instance, ho
flings the prattling babe into a steamship’s
furnace, besides making a prayerful effort to get it
swallowed up by a whale. Either of these mis-
deeds would be crowned with success but for Dan
Barry, who plays the part of evil genius to the
gent in holy orders. Daniel plucks the little
brand from the burning, and hauls it from the
cradle of the deep. Also, he thwarts the shep-
herd in a base endeavor to spoil a prize-fight at
Coolgardie, by administering drugs to the Game
Chicken, and finally the good young man of the
piece comes into his own. Marine disasters, bike-
races, “comic relief,” and scenery of a deserving
nature have been crowded into this epic poem.
The details of the Coolgardie prize-fight are super-
intended by Professor Miller, and a vigorous com-
pany of seasoned Alexandra artists does the best
possible with an entertainment which promises to
fill the Haleck with exceeding joy for some nights
to come.
* * * *
Charles Godfrey, who made his first
Australian appearance at Melb. Opera House
on Saturday, may be written down a tolerably
huge success. The audience received him as a
vocalist and character-sketcher of more than
ordinary ability, which he certainly is, and in
return for this general mark of confidence the
new-arrival gave them three or four good samples
of his quality. The recent cooling down of
that sore-eared monarch, Emperor William,
is unfortunate for Charles Godfrey to some
extent. Just now “Balaclava” is received
on its bare merits as a descriptive song sup-
ported by clever spictacular effects, instead of
being a red-hot iiomtive to jingoism. All the
same, this expensive laborer in the Rickards
vineyard will prove worthy of his hire. Lively
Florence Wilton is filling in some last nights at
the Opera House previous to returning ’Ome, and
the company in a number of unspecified respects
is much the same as usual.
* * * *-
“Djin-Djin” will be reluctantly withdrawn
from the Princess’s on Friday (14th). The Melb.
public feels truly sorry to part with this mascotte
pantomime, so m order to soften the blow of its
leave-taking, the Firm has arranged to adminster
two goes of “Djin-Djin” (afternoon and evening)
on the date aforesaid.

** * *
Rev. Haskett Smith, the parson w T ho has
paid dozens of visits to Jerusalem, and been buy-
ing groceries at Nazareth any time these last
twenty years, finished a second lecturing season
in Melbourne last week, and is now exploring the
holy land of Adelaide. The name of R. S. Smythe
no longer adorns the programme, but under Hugo
Fischer’s management the reverend member for
Palestine prospereth none the less. He still finds
proof of Bible inspiration in the fact that many
places mentioned m the Book have been actually
beheld by Haskett Smith, and the faithful don’t
want anything more convincing. Also, he has in
view a personally - conducted tour through the
countries he knows so well and talks of so fluently.
Therefore, all persons desirous of dabbling in the
Dead Sea and getting to the other side of Jordan,
are advised to keep Rev. Haskett Smith in view,
and on no account to set out for Canaan without
him. He is undoubtedly the first authority in
Christendom on all matters connected with holy
cities. He has studied the fleas and crawling
things of each historical district, and knows which
sort of insecticide is most suitable in every case.
My heart and brain with fire rage
For the love of Iris' fair ;
I’ll brave the Devil and his page
For the sake of winning her.
For she is young and beautiful,
Fairer than the brightest star ;
For stie is good and dutiful
To her royal old Pa-pa.
At Poverty Point .
Melbourne playgoers particularly were beginning to
wonder what had become of NinaTulloch, “IT he Magpie,”
of Frank Clark’s old Silk Stockings co., and news comes
of her death in South Africa. Nina was a Tasmaniarv
girl of good family. Her imitations of the magpie’s
morning call were extraordinary in their fidelity, and
made a startling chorus to Nina’s one small song.
Melba is said to have cleared £3OOO a-week during the
American concert tour recently completed. Not bad for
the diva’s first venture “ on her own.” At Christmas she
joined the opera co. in New York.
Miss Marie Elster, whose engagement with the Carl
Rosa Opera Co. terminated after one appearance in the
“ Bohemian Girl,” has not since been heard of, although
much inferior artists are raved over every night in Lon-
don and the provinces.
Clever Lardelli has not made the headway he expected
in London. At a late date, he had not got anyone to take
up his opera, “ Katherine,” and his fugitive work is not
more than sufficient to keep him going. Lowenfeld made
him an offer of some kind, but, evidently, nothing has
come of it.
Decima Moore (who, by the way, is a cousin of Signor
Lardelli) is quite charmed with Australia, writes Tiie
Bulletin’s London correspondent. Her father keeps a
bicycle-shop in Brighton.
Mrs. Hugo Gorlitz (Amy Sherwin) is now located at a
very pretty house at Hampstead, London, where she dis-
penses hospitality to a host of friends. Her Xmas enter-
tainment was most successful and quite German in style,
with a huge tree and no end of presents, great and small,
for her guests.
Australians try in vain to remember Barnato tumbling
with Wilson’s Circus. The reason is that Barnato never
did tumble with Wilson’s, or any other circus.
Frances Saville is claimed as “ the Californian prima-
donna” because she was born in Cal., and two of her
brothers still live there. A ’Frisco paper comments
severely on the impudence of Australia in annexing her
as an “Australian prima-donna” merely because her
parents brought her over here when she was too young to
protest.
George Coppin forgot to mention something in his
jubilee panto, speech at Melb. Royal the other week.
This is also the jubilee of his first “retirement” from the
stage. In 1846 he and his wife, both of them on the
sunny side of 30, took a farewell benefit in Melbourne.
If Ada Crossley and Belle Cole are singing in the same
concert company, which of them has choice of position on
the programme ?
The theatrical profesh. is very mixed in its recollections
of the laws of Moses. Dan Barry announced “ The Sixth
Commandment ” as his next drama for Melb. Alexandra ;
whereupon his friends and acquaintances came to him,
one after another, saying, “ So you’re going to give them
something spicy?” They took it for granted that the
sixth was the “ adultery” commandment.
Miss Gillespie, a niece of Premier Kingston, is regarded
as a coming sensation in Adelaide musical circles. The
lady is said to have declined a probationary engagement
with W. & M. recently, and intends to shortly swell
the Australian circle in London.
Luscombe Searelle, who has retired from South Africa
with a fortune, and has a strong literary bent (he once
published a volume of respectable verse) has two books
on the stocks—one a gossipy volume about South Africa,
the other his own experiences in various parts of the
globe, with anecdotes of people he has met and known.
He was wise in clearing out from South Africa (writes The
Bulletin’s London correspondent), as there is every pros-
pect of the theatrical business being overdone. Gus.
Harris contemplates sending out companies, and several
other impresarii will do the same, including George
Edwardes, who is to be lessee of the gorgeous new Johan-
nesburg theatre now being built by Barney Barnato’s
Investment Co. —the land upon which it is to stand cost
£40,000. Sass is to be Edwardes’s local representative.
May Fortescue had a tolerably successful tour in South
Africa, under the management of our old friend Searelle.
She did wonderfully well in the various coast towns, hut
was rather a frost in Johannesburg, owing to her
imperious ways. She wound up by coming to logger-
heads with Searelle, who had to remind her of the differ-
ence between running a co. “ on her own ” and being lead-
ing lady simply (call it “ star,” if you will) under an
impresario. She did not capture the expected mil-
lionaire.
Nellie Stewart plays the title role in “ The Shopgirl ”
in America in the place of Ellaline Terriss, who has shifted
to London to replace Ethel Ilaydon. Nellie, so far, has
got poor press notices in the Land of Wooden Hams.
At Richmond (Melb.): “Grand Minstrel and Variety
Show. Admission —a silver coir, i r three bottles.”
The new quartet by Prof. Marshall Hall, which has
lately been performed gmd praised in England, is the work
which nearly got lost in a drain-pipe last year. The Prof,
was going home late at night from a suburban party,
when he fell into a gutter and broke his collar-bone; the
MS. of his quartet, which he was carrying in his hand,
floated down the drain, and was stopped in its course by
the corpse of a cat.
Flo Young is alleged to possess the proper Trilby foot.
Joe Sweeny should make an efficient Svengali, and Alfred
Dampier ought to fill Taffy. George Lauri, with a judi-
cious song or two thrown in, and the necessary bad
French, shoukl shine as the Laird o’ Cockpen, whilst the
difficult part of Little Billie might suit New Boy Roberts.
The question (writes The Bulletin’s London correspon-
dent) was recently asked—“ Has Ada Crossley realised
the hopes and expectations of her friends?” I, for my own
part, must say that she has not. Whether it is that the
friends aforesaid overrated her ability ; whether they
spoilt her by undue adulation ; or whether the rather
chilly lady has really no love or taste for her profession (I
suspect this is at least partly the cause), she has not come
to the front as was expected. Of course she started by
making the mistake of throwing over Santley for Mar-
chesi, and though the great baritone is too chivalrous to
bear malice towards a lady, and raised no cabal against
her, there is little doubt that a kind of adverse prejudice
was created thereby. Anyhow, be the reason what it
may, she has, to put it mildly, been parlously near want
of success all through the piece. It is said that she made
a success with Harrison’s concert co. in the provinces,
but I have not read a single criticism which might not
have been written about any ordinary singer/ I believe
she is to sing at the next Patti concerts at the Albert
Hall, and that means something.”
Another version. “The Brookmans, of Adelaide, who
put Ada Crossley in Great Boulders, have recently pre-
sented her with a parcel of Oroya shares. Ada has
returned to the method of voice-production favored by
her Meib. teacher, Madame Simonsen, and through that
or something else her voice is daily improving. She sang
again at the Queen’s Hall on 4th Jan., when Clara Butt
and Belle Cole were once more pitted against the Aus-
tralian, who more than held her own. Miss Crossley’s
great chance comes in March next, when she sings with
Patti in the Royal Albert Hall.”
Adey Brunei and her husband, pupil and manager,
leave London in April for America. Adey is a niece of
a distinguished gentleman, who, besides being a physician
to Queen Victoria, is also a brother of Her Most Gracious,
so that Miss Brunei is really in the happy position of being
able to call the Queen her aunt.
Vic. Assembly has passed the third reading of the bill to
prevent Sunday performances at the theatres. The bung-
ness of the times has already pretty well effected that
pious purpose, but an amiable desire to give the churches
a show prompts Respectable members to prepare for a
possible revival. Framing the measure so as to exclude
churches was found somewhat awkward, and it is not
quite certain now that the Rev. Deuteronomy Jones will
be justified under the Act in sending round the church
dinner-service.
Mesmerist Kennedy claimed to be the original of Du
Maurier’s Svengali, the hypnotic musician in “ Trilby.”
Kennedy had a circumstantial story about an incident in
a N.Y. drawing-room when he and Du Maurier were pre-
sent, which he worked off on callow interviewers. Du
Maurier, in a recent interview, declares there was no
original for Svengali—the character was purely imaginary.
The rush for seats for the first week of Robusto God-
frey’s season induced Rickards to shove up prices 6d.
Godfrey can rip jingo songs off his chest with more gusto
than any other man living, and throws so much patriotism
into his melodious bellow that his presenoe in Melbourne
during the recent loyal frenzy would probably have led to
the gutting of every 7 German-sausage shop in the city 7.
In the future policemen on duty at Melb. theatres are to
be changed regularly. The peeler who is always there
grows sympathetic towards the House and usually 7 lends a
hand in loading the gallery and leading the applause.
Possibly, too, fewer Melb. ballet-girls will marry police-
men now.
Augustus Harris offered the part of Cinderella in Drury
Lane pantomime to Ethel Hay 7 don, but George Edwardes
would not release her, and is about to send her to America
to play with one of his companies there. The “ woodiness
of ankle,” which The Bulletin noticed when Ethel played
in Melb. Princess’s pantomime, is the only thing that
prevents her from thoroughly setting the Thames on fire.
Henry Stockwell’s wife and family have joined him in
London, and are now settled at Fulham, where Leminone
also lives. Mine. Sherwin, who introduced them both to
Sydney audiences, has lost none of her interest in them,
and often secures engagements for both. T. M. Jackson
is another who owes much to the Tasmanian singer’s kind
intervention. Jackson and George Clutsam both live with
Mme. Minna Fischer and her mother in St. John’s Wood.
Clutsam is said to be turning out music which “ must one
day bring the world to his feet.”
Florence Wilton’s Catherine wheel must be a reminis-
cence of her “ Jack-in-the-Box,” which she played at the
Strand Theatre some years ago. She isn't quite as agile
a Catherine-wheeler as her predecessor in the part,
Fannie Leslie.

tralia, and if you know as much as I do you will be able

Professional play-pirates still exist in N.S.W. and the
other provinces. A correspondent was offered the scrip
of one of Lestocq’s most popular comedies, the other day,
mb rosa, for £l. If “Plain Bill” Holloway could only
cast his eagle eye on that pirate !
“ Plain Bill” Holloway and his co. are doing huge biz
in Johannes urg. His daughters, Theo and Juliet, who

At Hobart recently the girls of the Comic Opera Co
played a cricket-match against the officers of the Orlando.
How the Hobart girls did glare at the poachers on their
preserves ! The Hobart girls think they have a mortgage
on anything 7 wearing brass buttons.
The advt. of Miss Polly Emery’s farewell benefit at
Sydney Royal announced that the performance would be
supported by Brough and Boucicault’s famous co., Alfred
Woods’s talented co., also Westmacott’s co., Rickards’s
co., and the Criterion co. Further, it differentiated
between Mr. C. B. Westmacott, who assisted with his co.,
and C. B. Westmacott, Esq., who lent the Royal for the
occasion. Consider the nice gradations—the famous co.,
the talented co., the three other co.’s, and the man who is
partly Esq. and partly not !
Purves, they say, doesn’t like Champion. The story
goes that they first met behind scenes at a B. and B. per-
formance -both at the invitation of B. the Second. Before
they had been introduced, Purves chanced to make a
remark on the subject of the drama which Champion
thought silly—and said so. Boucicault sought to draw a
herring across the track by introducing them to each
other. Quoth Purves, “So you’re H. H. Champion !
Well, you may know something about the Labor-question,
but you know very little about the drama! ” Re-
joined Champion, “So you’re Purves, are you? Well,
you may know something about law, but you know
very little about manners ! ”
Q. Govt.-party saj sprayers night and morn-
ing for Glassey—“ the best friend Boodlewraith
has. ” Glassev’sdefenders lay continual stress on his
incorruptibility—“ lie’s an ass, we grant you, but
look what a phenomenally honest ass ! ” As the
effect of his honesty is to keep the party of dis-
honesty in power, the country pines for a little
more capacity and a few less scruples.

A
SOCIETY
CAMEO
One of the tragedies of the “heat-wave”
tvas played on the Bourke-Wanaaring track to-
wards Mt. Brown, in one of the hungriest parts of
the far north-west (N.S.W.). J. P. M‘Cann,
partly losing his eyesight, lost also a Gov. billet;
but instead of sitting down while his wife went
washing for a living, or worrying the Minister for
an allowance, he bought a wheelbarrow and a
magic-lantern and started for Bourke, pushing
through the shearing-sheds and way-back towns.
The liberality of the bushmen soon enabled him
to buy a horse and sulky, and with these he
started out to “do ” the N.W. corner of N.S.W
and the Queensland border towns, taking his nine-
year-old son with him. Twelve miles from Tin-
neroo bore, in one of the hottest days, he left the
sulky (the horse was “done”) and made a
desperate struggle to find a tank which he believed
to be somewhere near the track. He failed,
dragged himself to the sulky, and insisted upon
his boy moistening his own lips with the few drops
of water which the latter had saved for his father..
Then they drove the horse on, and reached the
bore; but the man died within a few hours.
POLICY.
“ You have dirtied your jacket. What ever will your mother say? ”
“If mother says anything about it, I'll change the conversation.”
Concerning deaths from heat-apoplexy or heat-
stroke in the bush, it is remarkable how few there
are. When the sun is gone, the average traveller
assumes the danger to be past, and throws himself
on the hot ground, as likely as not without unroll-
ing his blankets, and prepares to sleep. Out in
the far West (N.S.W.) the thermometer has fre-
quently run up to past 100 in the shade at
nights ; and at these times even healthy people,
lying in hammocks, have felt a strong inclination
to “ let themselves go to slide into unconscious-
ness. Add the heat of the ground, and the temp-
tation would be often absolutely irresistible. This
may, perhaps, explain the phenomenon of two
men, who had apparently been suffering from
thirst, being found dead under the one tree on
Nundora, without any trace of a final struggle.
* * * *
During the recent hot weather in the
South the North has been, by comparison, beauti-
fully cool. While Brisbane sweltered, Bourke
broiled, and Thargomindah stewed, Northern
towns, like Townsville, the Towers, and Hughen-
den, smiled a smile of placid peace and thanked
Heaven they were too far North to catch the heat-
wave. Wragge wrath fully predicted Sheol in the
shade, but the North only smiled —the prediction
was out of it.
* * * »
New South Wales prayed for rain and got<
a little sprinkled over those parts of the province
where it was least required, and then the drought
started again. Victoria prayed for rain and got a
little, but not enough to save its grain-crops from
being more than half-ruined. Queensland didn’t
pray for it, and it came down in oceans in quite
an unsolicited way, and washed away a good part
of Townsville, and nearly drowned a Supreme
Court judge, and swamped the local daily paper
to such an extent that it couldn’t publish for two
days, and it partially drowned Rockhampton, and
left half the province with hardly a dry shirt for
twp weeks, and reduced the Central Railway to
such a state that passengers have to be conveyed
long distances in boats over an unexpected inland,
sea. And this is what people call the Efficacy of
Prayer.
* * * *
The Bulletin notices that that little tin
Windeyer, the snappish Judge Docker, has been
sitting from early morning until about midnight
at Bathurst Criminal Sessions. It must be the
Judges themselves who are responsible for such
outrages on justice; for if they objected, the
Crown Law Department would not dare to insist,
at the risk of having the matter publicly dis-
cussed.
» » » »
There will shortly be published by a well-
known managing-clerk “A guide for litigants ab
the Courts of Petty Sessions, Melbourne district.”
This will enable one to know what justices can be
approached and how; also the solicitors who are
friends of certain magistrates, and before whom
they always bring their cases, thereby scoring
wins, and in civil matters getting liberal costs
awarded; also how to pack benches, and the
attorneys keenest at the game ; also what justices
can be approached privately for bail and judiciously
squared by being handed a sov. or fiver (according
to the gravity of the case) to pay cab-hire, and not
be asked for the change. Price 215., of all book-
sellers.

The moral of the big pastoral law-suit, on
which the N.S.W. bar is now feeding fat: Never
dismiss a confidential clerk, if you have any par-
ticular reason why he should continue to respect
your confidence.
Miss POLLY EMERY, the Australian
Soubrette.

* ¥ * *
Gov. Hampden has said, in almost as
many words, that he doesn’t see why he should be
bothered to attend every paltry church-bazaar and
missionary-meeting. All reasonable people will
sympathise with H.E.’s determination. The
system he has found so galling chiefly dates from
the reign of the unspeakable Gov. Carington, who
was so grovellingly anxious to propitiate every-
body that he would attend with all his suite at the
opening even of a box of sardines.
* * * *
Melbourne Herald abuses Prempeh, of
Ashantee:—
Meanwhile, the obstinacy of this petty young savage
has been attended with mischievous consequences, the
ultimate reach of which we do not yet know. Prince
Henry of Battenberg is dead, and the Queen’s favorite
daughter left a widow.
And all because a mischievous nig hoped to save
his country from the clutches of the omnivorous
Bull. The point is that no savage should go to
such a length in defending his possessions as to
afford one of Queen Victoria’s German relatives an
opportunity of getting his feet wet in the service
of the Empire.
* * * *
The antiquated tele-
graph system of Westralia
seems to have definitely
broken down under the
strain of extra business.
Some of the messages lying
in the office at Perth are
now 15 days old, and
could have reached Mel-
bourne or Sydney much
faster by steamer. Perth
officials acknowledge that
they have given up all
hope of ever overtaking
the accumulation, so it
seems that the business is
to remain in arrears until
the Day of Judgment. The
pile of ancient messages
will probably be packed in
a barrel shortly, and sent
East by steamer, or gradual
brig, or started across the
continent per camel, and
then things will be a little
easier for a while, but in a
week or two the trouble
will be in full blast again.
“Urgent” messages are
abolished now in the West,
as it is a mere mockery to
call a message “urgent”
and then take a fortnight
to deliver it. The born
Westralian can’t move fast
enough to get out of his
own road; he is a person
who sleeps in his velocity,
arid doesn’t hear of his own
death till three months
afterwards ; and a tortoise
in ballast would overtake
him any day and bite him
from behind for obstructing the roadway.
-Re Gov. Brassey’s announcement to his
Ministers that he intended to proceed to the
prorogation on horseback. The somewhat simple
old man began by explaining that although he had
bought Hopetoun’s gaudy State'carriage and
trappings, he did not intend to use them. He
trotted them out with six horses at Ballarat Exhi-
bition opening, but felt uncomfortable all the
wlnle. Ihe thing was too much like a circus.
He, therefore, intended to strike opt a new line
for himself, and ride to Parliament House “ On
a bike, sir ! anticipated Peacock, with his wild
hyaena laugh. “No, sir,” continued Brassev
solemnly, upon a horse.” “Yes, yes; with the
staff on bikes, put m the Premier encouragingly
Oh, no. said the Gov., “they can hire a
carnage “ Why not let them have the police
van . chipped in Duffy to Turner, sotto vocc, and
so on. Although Ministers received the old
gentleman’s ultimatum with official courtesy, thev
were greatly disturbed by it. On horseback, wilh
20 troopers behind and 20 before him, Brassey’s
progress was pictured in their minds as some-
thing similar to the entry, under heavy
escort, of a captured bushranger. The change,
which the Akgus described as “important,” is
believed to be the outcome of Brassey’s persistent
abhorrence of conforming to precedent. He only
has to be told how a thing has been done, to
determine to do it differently. This would be
laudable if it only went far enough. For instance,
if H.E. had said : “I don’t want any carriage, or
any escort. I’m going to the prorogation on a
thrippeny tram,” the Democracy would have
howled its great heart out with joy.

Two cable thrills :—
A sensational rumor was current to-day that Viscount
Milton, M.P., for Wakefield, had been killed while hu 1 ting,
his horse having been frightened by a train and thrown
him over a bridge. The rumor, however, proved to be
unfounded.
The Queen kissed Prince Alexander, the eldest son of
the late Prince Henry of Battenberg.
The S.M. Herald’s idea of foreign intelligence
for the enlightenment of Australian readers, is
that tlie Queen kissed somebody that hardly any-
body ever heard of, and that somebody whom no-
body ever heard of isn’t dead. And if that news
hadn’t come this country might have gone on
blindly imagining that the man it never heard of
really was dead, or that he had never been alive :
We offer up a thanksgiving because he isn’t dead.

The news came over many lands,
And under many seas ;
It fled along the storied strands
Of ancient Cy-clad-es.
It hurried down the Red Sea shore,
And ’cross the Arab sands it tore,
And as it came our hearts grew light that had
been dull as lead,
For this man we never heard about —he really
isn’t dead.
It crossed the Shatt-el-arab in
The silent, solemn night,
Went westward to the Carib in
The early morning light.
And north and south across the deep,
To nations moaning in their sleep,
It carried news of gladness as on wings of joy it
sped,
For, after all, it seems, this British viscount isn’t
dead.
’Tis said there was a rumor that
He tumbled off his horse
And landed on his shattered hat,
And thus became a corse ;
We hadn’t heard the story
Of that battered peer and gory,
But the cable comes to say he didn’t fall upon his
head—
We didn’t know he had one—and it seems he isn’t
dead.
His memory we will cherish
Within our souls with care,
We learn he didn’t perish
Because he wasn’t there ;
He didn’t fall off any horse
Because there wasn’t one, of course.
We really don’t know who he is, but from our
little bed


AT PER DOZEN.
The most important; point for the consideration
of all persons who desire to possess photographs
of those nearest and dearest to them is with regard
to permanence rather than price. No photograph
that will turn yellow, break out in spots, or vanish
into nothingness within a few weeks can have its
value expressed in figures. When delivered, it
may shine like the enamelled mystery on a soap-
box, but the seeds of disease are there, and must
come to the surface. This may be regarded as
one of the certainties of cheap photography at per

topper. The noble owner had left his boiled Sun-
day hat on board, and the fact being discovered
at the last moment a breathless messenger sprinted
harbor to recover the lost adornment.
The Sunbeam had got her anchor up and was
about to make sail when the agent was seen fran-
tically waving himself round his head on the
beach, so the vessel was hove to and a boat sent
to fetch him. Then there was a long search for
the box with the desired article in it, and by the
time it was found and delivered the Sunbeam had
drifted so close inshore that she couldn’t get out
again. After she had gone aground the hat
was found to be still on board, the messenger
having departed with an empty box. It is a char-
acteristic yarn; the average Briton would go
back to the gates of perdition for his bell-topper
if he had accidentally left it there. If the Light
Brigade had done its famous charge with a plug
hat on, and had dropped that plug hat in the
midst of the Russian army, The Bulletin verily
believes the survivors would have charged back
again for it.

The “ Annual Convocation of the Supreme
Grand Royal Arch Chapter ” of Maoriland was
held the other day at Wellington. “ M.E. Comp.
Dr. L. G. Boor, Deputy Grand Z.,” presided, and
a lot of routine business was gone through. Then
the meeting proceeded to elect the following
officers :
First Grand Principal Z. M.E. Comp. ; Deputy Grand
Principal, M.E. Comp. ; Second Grand Principal, H. Most
E. Comp.; Third Grand Principal, M.E. Comp. ; Grand
Scribe E., M.E. Comp. ; Grand Scribe N., M.E. Comp. ;
Grand Treasurer, M.E. Comp. ; Grand Recorder, M.E.
Comp. ; Grand Chancellor, M.E. Comp. ; First Grand
Sojourner, M.E. Comp. ; Second Grand Sojourner, M.E.
Comp.; Third Grand Sojourner, M.E. Comp. ; Grand
Superintendent of Works, M.E. Comp.; Grand Sword
Bearer, M.E. Comp. ; Grand Standard Bearer, M.E.
Comp. ; Supreme Committee, the Officers of Grand
Chapter and M.E. Comps., also Z. Excellent Comps.
The Bulletin badly wants to know if all these
people take themselves seriously when they are off
the chain, so to speak ; or does the Z. Grand
Principal Kuzzilbash M.E. Whoop smile over the
whole thing when he meets the Deputy Grand
Arch Flapdoodle Z. Excellent Fryingpan, and
acknowledge in confidence that it is all bosh, and
pretty bad bosh at that ? Men ! They are bigger
asses than even women !

* * * *
The usual girl turned up the other day in
Melb. District Court with the usual story 9f
assault committed by a bold, bad man, but in
cross-examination her story got badly mixed up,
and finally she acknowledged, also as usual, that
her tale of wrong was wholly imaginary. The
bench dismissed the case “without any com-
ments,” and nothing further has been heard about
it up to date. The blessed theory that it is the
privilege of any woman in a state of moral dis-
repair to bring any kind of accusation against a
man, and then, when her tale collapses, to own up
and go away without being arrested for perjury,
still seems to hold good in Australia.
* * * •
Hysterical Ethel Wright, the Gunnedah
(N.S.W.) girl who caused a sensation by accusing
a man of putting poison in the family flour-bin,
and who was sentenced to four years’ imprison-
ment for perjury, is shortly to become a mother,
and was married the other day in Tamworth Gaol
to the father of her child. As, therefore, she was
enceinte at the time of her offence, the case appears
more than ever one for medical enquiry.
Tasmania’s latest girl-horror has broken
up in the way such horrors usually do. The young
woman who cast herself head-first out of a railway-
carriage because the only other occupant, a man,
had taken out a bottle of cough-mixture, and
then turned up in a bruised state and gave him
into custody for attempting to chloroform and
outrage her, owned up in court that the said man
didn’t attempt to do either. He simply took the
cork out of a bottle, but he didn’t offer her the
bottle, or attempt to do anything whatever, and,
apart from the fact that there was a man and a
bottle, the story arose out of her own diseased
imagination. The Social Gimlet kind of uneasy
morality is responsible for a lot of incidents of
this sort. It gets hold of a nervous girl and
teaches her that she has to be always on the wild
high shriek to protect her modesty from imaginary
assaults, and some day, as a consequence, she
throws herself screaming out of a railway-carriage,
making a great exhibition of two open-work stock-
ings and a pair of calves as she goes but, and falls
on the line in a dishevelled state with her skirt
over her head, and the people in the rear carriages
view her from the windows as they go by.
Down South, the other day, a lot of New
Women shrieked and sobbed over the mournful
(inquest) case of a young lady who, poor innocent,
had been seduced and had a child at the early age
of 2G. The villainous seducer was a ruffian of 18
years. The same afternoon the same set of
humorists went and interviewed members of
Parliament on the congenial subject of the Age of
Consent—as regards women.
* * * *
Surgeon O’Hara, at Auckland Medical
Congress:
So long as scrofulous children were produced they must
expect tuberculosis as a disease to flourish. He had
known a flock-owner to give five hundred guineas for a
stud ram, and marry his daughter to a broken-down
strumous object.
The marvel is, not that O’Hara, who always has
the courage of his opinions, should have said this
thing, but that the S. M. Herald should have
published it in an un-Bowdlerised cablegram.

* * * *
There are only three English importations
amongst the entire Wesleyan clergy of Victoria
and Tasmania. A patriotic example to the
Anglicans—by which they are not likely to profit.
A common Australian parson is not good enough,
as a correspondence-clerk (in holy orders) between
God and themselves, for ever so many high-
toned, so-called Australian Church of England
congregations. In that very phrase Church of
England lies the root of the whole trouble. Church -
members of a muddily-logical turn of mind deduce
from it that a Church of England must necessarily,
wherever situated, be manned by English parsons.
Hence these importations, of which members of
the Australian - Anglican clergy complain so
bitterly. As well say a Holy Trinity of England.
* * * *
Two letters received by an employer from
a Melb. labor-agent:—
3/12 95. Dear Sir, —Your order for a cook at 20s. per
week to hand. I am sending you by the first train to-
morrow the only man cook I have at present on my books.
If he is not up to the mark please let me know, and I will
send another as soon as possible.
17 12 95. Dear Sir,—l hope the man I sent you on the
4th inst. is giving satisfaction. I have a splendid cook
now waiting engagement at 15s. per week. He is a smart,
sober man, and if you would like to have him, and save
ss. a iveek, I will be glad to send him on.
This isquite a common trick among certain registry -
keepers. They obtain a man a situation, charge
him a fee equal to at least a week’s wages, and
then cold-bloodedly try to get him dismissed for
their own benefit.
* * * ♦
At a recent meeting of the Queensland
Medical Society, a boy, born at sea, of English
parents, and 11 years of age, was introduced suffer-
ing from leprosy, although, so far as is known, he
had never come in contact with the disease. The
bacteriological examination is stated to have con-
firmed the truth of this diagnosis, and filarise
were found in the blood. Question : Whence the
leprosy ?

Out of 50 hands employed in the Salva-
tion Army’s Melb. printing works only two are
“saved.” So much for the morals (and intelli-
gence) of compositors. Two of the “ uncleansed ”
having taken a ticket in “ Tatt’s ” last sweep, were
lucky enough to win about A6OO each ; they were
promptly “ sacked though, not belonging to
the “ Harmy,” they were surely under no obliga-
tion to abide by the rules of the “ order.”

When will Melbourne cease to betray
a dread of Geelong waking up and asserting
itself ? The Age sneers again :—“ His Excellency
the Governor proceeded to Geelong yesterday in
the Sunbeam, for the purpose of paying an official
visit to the centre of the universe.” It isn’t as
though the holy Wesleyans, Baptists, and Church
of Englanders down at the peaceful “ Pivot ” were
in the habit of asserting themselves. All they
ask is to be left alone to live, say their prayers,
make money, and die.

easiest she knew, and even there in the fever-
season nurses were on duty continuously from 8.30
p.m. to 7.30 a.m. every night in the week.
Eleven hours’ night-work straight on end in a
fever ward, and never a night off. “And the
greatest of these is Charity.”

Dear Bulletin, —The prices charged at
some N.S.W. railway refreshment-rooms are scan-
dalous. Regular travellers know what to pay ;
strangers don’t, and are charged through the nose.
At one place, 1 paid Is. fid. for a lb. of grapes (in
Sydney, the price ranges from 3d, to 6d.); and at
another, 2s. (id. for two cups of tea, abread-and-
meat - sandwich, and some pickled onions. In
Maoriland, the refreshment-tariff is printed on the
back of the time-table, or used to be, and people
knew where they were.— Stranger.

In an Adelaide suburb, the Wesleyans
erected an imposing edifice, and dedicated it “to
the Glory of God.” Shortly afterwards, the Angli-
cans ran-up a small wooden mission-hall right
opposite the front door oQ the other place, and
inscribed over the door, “ Erected to the Greater
Glory of God.” Yet the unification of the
Churches is talked of.
At South Melb. police-court, the other day, one of the
magistrates (a medical man) remarked that a witness
who had kissed the much-diseased Bible was suffering
from cancer on the lip. The next witness appeared to
kiss his oath upon the very spot selected by the virulent
cancer case.

A BIBLE STORY.
A merchant in the prime of life
Came home one afternoon,
And thus addressed his little wife—■
“ I must be <join</ soon ;
I must be going soon, my dear—•
Alas ! you need not look,
I’ve not been drinking, as you fear;
I’ve merely kissed the Book.
Yes, merely mugged the Bible-0 !
The ancient Holy Bible-0 !
I took an oath,
To die I’m loth,
But, Jane, I’ve kissed the Book.
“That ledger clerk, whose summer suit
So charmed you with its style,
Went ‘ cronk ; ’ ’twas best to prosecute,
He ought to stand his trial.
And so, not thinking what it meant,
A warrant out I took.
They ‘ collared ’ him ; to court I went,
And there I kissed the Book.
They made me kiss the Bible-0 !
The microbe-laden Bible-0 !
I trust, my love,
I’m ‘ fix’t ’—above,
For, Jane, I’ve kissed the Book.
“ Please telephone to lawyer Snare,
He’s pretty wide-awake
And has instructions to prepare
The will I mean to make,
Which must be signed this very night
And witnessed by the cook.
At present I am feeling right—•
But, Jane, I’ve kissed the Book.
True bill ! I kissed the Bible-0 !
The comfort-bringing Bible-0 !
Don’t break your heart,
We’ve got to part
Because I kissed the Book.”
Poor Tompkins, raving, passed away
At three o’clock next mom.
He raved of romping in the hay
And chewing golden corn ;
And thought himself a boy again
Eel-fishing in the brook.
The cause of death was mildewed brain
Through kissing that there Book.
He died of kiss-the-Bible-0 !
The peaceful, greasefull Bible-0 !
At three a.m.
He went —ahem !
Through having kissed the Book.
The clerk (acquitted) shed a sneer
Of pity for his boss ;
The wealthy widow dropped a tear
To signify her loss
Of Tompkins, who had been a brute ;
Then sought a shady nook
Where Gustus, in the summer suit —
Well, didn’t kiss the Book.
Gus didn’t squeeze the Bible-0 !
The husband-killing Bible-0 !
’Twixt sobs of joy
Jane said, “ Dear boy,
I’m glad he kissed the Book ! ”

The Wild-Cat Column,
The Commercial Bank of Australia has decided
to appeal to its reconstructed depositors for a reduction
of interest, and probably an extension of time also. The
announcement was made by the same old James Service
who, less than three years ago, foresaw that the Com-
mercial’s preference shares would soon sell at £lO each,
and the deposit-receipts would be as good as gold, or
nearly so. Old James was brave to the last; even when
he announced that his big bank was once more in very
shallow water he pointed out that the institution had,
since ’93, paid off over £2,000,000 of its liabilities, and the
fact that it had done so largely by taking deposit-receipts
inpayment of overdrafts, &c., was judiciously left out.
Also, he added that the total liquid assets of the “ new
bank ” were equal to 60s. in the £ of the “ new bank’s ”
liabilities—which they are, only unhappily these assets
have also got to cover a lot of the liabilities of the old
bank, about which nothing was said at all.
The cause assigned for the proposed re-construc-
tion is the impossibility of paying 4J per cent, on fixed
deposits, with interest at its present low figure. As a
matter of fact, the Commercial of Australia has now only
£7,888,118 of old deposits on hand ; it pays 4J- per cent, on
£5,773,778 of these, and on the other £2,114,340 (converted
into preference shares) it pays nothing at all. Therefore,
it only pays about £260,000 a year in interest, which,
spread over the whole amount owing, means that the
rate is £3 6s. per cent. And when it can’t earn that,
the Commercial of Australia must be in a pretty bad way.
According to its own showing, it appears to have actually
realised last half-year only some £58,500 gross profit on
the £6,150,000 of the old bank’s unliquidated assets, equal
to less than 2 per cent, per annum. Assuming that this
£6,150,000 is supposed to be lent out at only 4* per cent.,
this would mean that about £3,500,000 is returning no
interest at all, and, if so, it seems probable that a good
deal of the principal is lost as well as the interest. The
£4,000,000 of paid-up capital, and the other £1,100,000
that has yet to come in should be more than sufficient to
provide for everything, if concessions can be obtained in
the meantime, but a big writing-down seems inevitable,
unless the Commercial can get very large concessions in-
deed from its creditors.
One way out of the difficulty would be to make
no more hard and fast agreements with the depositors.
A good form of compromise would be to provide that,
say, £50,000 a-year should be devoted out of the actually
realised profits to paying off depositors, to be selected by
ballot; that all the remaining profits should be devoted to
paying interest on deposits up to the agreed upon 41 per
cent, that of any profits realised over and above the 4-i
per cent., and the £50,000 paid in reduction of deposit
principal, one-half should go to a reserve fund to be in-
vested in Government securities, and the other half
should also go towards paying off deposits. Under this
scheme, if the bank gets no worse, the depositors would
probably receive 3£ per cent, in the immediate present,
and as the amount of their claims was reduced by the
acceptance of deposit-receipts in payment of debts and
by the repayment of £50,000 a year in cash, and by any
other repayments which might be possible, the interest
would probably rise again by degrees to the old 4J per
cent. Another device is the plain, crude idea of asking
for a reduction to 2£ or 3 per cent.—the same request
which the irate British shareholders sat upon so heavily
in the City of Melb. case. Another, and the best of ali,
would be for the Vic. Government to offer 2 or 2£ per
cent, debentures, redeemable at its own option any time
within, say, 20 years, in lieu of the reconstructed deposits,
to put the bank under the strict supervision of a State
auditor until all the guaranteed claims are paid in full,
and devote the £lOO,OOO or £130,000 a year thus saved to
writing off the bad assets, with the capital to fall back
upon for further writing-off purposes if absolutely neces-
sary. There is hardly the remotest possibility that the
State could lose by this process, and probably even the
shareholders would lose nothing in the end. And the
one thing to be kept in mind is that Victoria doesn’t want
to run even the most outside risk of any more big liquida-
tions just now under any circumstances.
* * * *
The Sydney and Suburban Hydraulic Power
Co. shows a steadily increasing business and pays a div.
for the half-year at the rate of 7J per cent, per annum,
and, after making liberal allowances for depreciation, &c.,
it ends the year £737 better off than it was at the begin-
ning. One remarkable feature of the balance-sheet, how-
ever, is that £1539 of the assets consist of an unsettled
law-case—£939 of a claim upon a guarantee co. which is
carrying the matter to appeal, and £6OO of law expenses
which will be recovered if the appeal goes the right way,
while if it goes wrong they won’t be, and the Hydraulio
Co. may also be struck with the other party’s law-bill.
Considering the awful uncertainty of law, this £1539 isn’t
much of an asset and should be regarded with grave
suspicion. Another asset is “ Parliamentary rights and
concessions, £15,000.” Probably they are worth that
money, but the co. did not pay £15,000 for them so
far as the public is aware, and yet the sheet would show a
serious deficiency if the amount wasn’t there, and, in any
case, the practice of squaring a balance-sheet by the in-
sertion of a more or less undefinable item for goodwill is
always questionable. Further, the Hydraulic Co., though
distinctly prosperous and with a most promising future
before it, seems to be declaring a £2812 dividend with
only £2l ss. cash in hand and a £123 overdraft to pay it
out of.
* * * *
The Ballarat Banking Co. has a net profit for
the half-year ended 31st December of £3463, against
£3449 last half, and after paying the usual div. of 5£ per
cent, per annum there is £1125 to add to profit and loss,
making the balance carried forward £3409. The Ballarat
Bank never attempts nowadays to get beyond its un-
obtrusive 5£ per cent., and if it is well-advised it will
remain at that and keep adding to its reserve-fund for
many years to come. The cash and Government deben-
tures amount to £80,300, equal to nearly 36 per cent, of
the liabilities to the public. It has no costly branches, so
the total expenses average only £2500 a year, no note
circulation, and no English deposits ; if it liked to call up
the remainder of its little capital it. could pay off its entire
liabilities in cash, even if all its investments went bad
together ; it has kept out of Melbourne, and consequently
out of the boom; it has only £3650 worth of modest
premises and furniture; and by its strict avoidance of the
policy of wanting to own the whole earth, it is now the
most distinctly prosperous of all the Victorian banks.

Scrap of evidence in the Mercadool land-cases :
“Jeremiah Roberts, of the Assets Company, was then
called and his examination continued. He said that the
company lent money on conditional purchases, and also
on conditional and homestead leases. The charge for
interest varied, according to the nature of the security,
sometimes the company charged as high as 20 per cent.,
and sometimes as low as 10 per cent,, their charge on an
average being about 15 per cent. In addition to this
they charged 2$ per cent, to 5 per cent, commission." An
average of 15 per cent, and 2J to 5 per cent, commission!
When is the State Land Bank coming along ? On this 15
per cent, average the interest on a £5OO advance would be
£75 a year. And with a decently-managed State Land
Bank, £85 a year would cover interest and also repay the
principal in about 25 years.
* * * *
The Metropolitan Mutual Permanent Building
and Investment Association shows a loss for ’95 of £3658,
whioh seems to be about its normal condition nowadays.
Some of the items in its revenue-account are calculated
to make the Association feel rather serious in its mind.
One property, which is valued at £12,060, seems to have
returned £272 in rents—equal to a little over 2 per cent.,
and £226,000 worth of property seems to have yielded
about 2£ per cent, all round. These properties are care-
fully moored down by £100,000 worth of mortgages, which
apparently swallow up all the rent-receipts, and perhaps
a little more. If the Metropolitan had been content to
own just as much property as it could pay for, the fall in
rents would simply have meant a reduction in the profits,
but it tried to own a lot more, and now the interest ap-
parently eats up the rent and a lot of the profits
of the ordinary business as well. The curse of building-
society business in Australia has been the desire to have
£100,000 capital and to borrow £100,000 more on deben-
tures, and then, with that £200,000, to buy a £500,000
property and owe £300,000 of the purchase-money on
mortgage, and carry on the rest of the business by getting
£80,000 of deposits.
* * * *
That good old Tory paper, the Australasian,
by way of pointing out the evils of a State Bank and a
State note-issue, and the beauties of the private busted-
reconstruct-15s.-in-the-£-boom-smash-revelations system,
remarks : “ While we in Victoria were passing through
our recent financial troubles not the slightest anxiety was
felt about the bank-notes, nor was there any rush to
change them into gold. Even the Federal Bank notes
were always worth par.” So they were, after the other
banks agreed to give 20s. in the £ for them and take the
risk themselves in the hope of averting a panic. But in
the very brief interval before that consummation Federal
notes were sold at 18s., if not less. And when the
Oriental Bank broke a large quantity of its notes changed
hands at 16s. And when the bank panic was in full blast
in Sydney the writer went to spend a Saturday afternoon
and Sunday in a decent-sized town 60 miles from the city,
and nearly had to camp out in the street because he had
no gold with him and the local Goths wouldn’t offer more
than 15s. in the £ for notes, and didn’t really want to
take them at all. Said notes, too, were those of a bank
which, after all, didn’t close or reconstruct.
* * * *
The very newest thing in small finance. Bal-
main (Sydney) New Ferry Co. calls for tenders for a new
steamer. Any persons proposing to tender must first send
in their names and a deposit of £5 5s., “which will be
returned on their submitting a bona fide tender, i.e.,
within 15 per cent, of the one accepted.” If it isn’t
within 15 per cent, of the one accepted, the money, pre-
sumably, will be confiscated by the co. And, as a suc-
cessful tenderer may come along who submits a pheno-
menally low offer, because he means to swindle the people
who supply the materials, or because he is going broke
anyhow, and is, therefore, reckless of consequences, or
because he intends to build on shoddy principles, or
through sheer blundering in his estimates, the result may
be that the £5 5s. of every honest tenderer will be for-
feited.
* * * *
To put an end to certain misapprehensions
which seem to be going round, Tiie Bulletin hereby
draws attention to the fact that it said “ Minister Peacock
has the misfortune to be manager” of the Rothschild
G.M. Co. —the Vic. concern with 10,000 real shares and
apparently 17,000 bogus ones—not that he had that mis-
fortune at the time the swindle was perpetrated. Mr.
Peacock wasn’t manager when the fraud was committed.
He has the misfortune to be the man who has to sort
things out, however, which is quite enough grief for any
one man.

* « * * *
Bendigo Electric Lighting Co., a local concern,
has made for the last year a profit equal to about 12 per
cent, on the capital. As it has to compete with the gas-
supply its prices are necessarily moderate, and the above
12 per cent, was made not by extortion but by good
management. The Sydney people who want to job away
their far bigger electric-light concession to a British co.
should think hard over this. The local corporation wants
the profit quite as badly as any British co. can do.

The directors of the City of Melbourne Bank
are to be examined before Judge Molesworth in Mel-
bourne :—
The examination of the City of Melb. Bank
directors, it appears, is to be a lengthy one, dealing with
the whole management of the bank for some years past.
The longer the better for everybody concerned except the
creditors. And a few more drops in the ocean of losses
won’t make much difference to them.

The N.S.W. tramway officials are at present
investigating an alleged case of ticket-forgery. It has
come to the knowledge of the Dept, that 20s. worth of
tickets could be purchased from a dealer in the eastern
suburbs for 16s.
* » * *
There is trouble ahead for the dealers in railway
and steamer tickets. It has been customary, when a
name was marked on a ticket, to alter the sex by adding
“a” to Mr. or scratching it out, as the case might be. A
scalper is now under committal in Melb. for this offence,
described as “ forgery.” Perhaps it technically is forgery,
but, seeing that the value of the ticket (which is a con-
tract to carry an adult) is not altered, the crime is a very
venial one. ' It is also hinted that the N.S.W. Railway
Commissioners may bring forward an action for con-
spiracy. But surely the commissioners and steamship-
owners can grasp the situation without going about it in
such a round-about and expensive way? They could
make the dealer shut-up shop at once by buying back,
less (say) 10 per cent, discount, unused halves of return-
tickets. Some steamship-owners do this, which is the
only equitable way out of the difficulty, and but a just
acknowledgment of the equitable rights of a ticket-holder.
* * * *
Melb. agents for British capital are eagerly
snapping up real estate mortgages. Last week one man
covered a big advance on several Q. properties at 2i per
cent, below what the loan cost five years previous.

Euroa (Vic.) Preserving Co. last year turned
out 490,000 rabbits, but although rates were slightly better
than in ’94, results still worked out the wrong way, the
debit (£458) showing a£2 increase for the year. Babbits
cost £2755, material, £2276; wages, £3780 ; and freight,£7B.
Sales receipts, £9557. Apart from employing a lot ot
labor, the tinned-rabbit trade is unprofitable. Even the
Camperdown factory, run by a Geelong firm, did not pay
in ’94, and it is doubtful if last season’s operations showed
a profit.

Brisbane municipality has frittered away a
capital chance of getting 1 control of the tramways. The
old co. has died of lingering paralysis, and its horses of
galloping consumption, and now a ’Ome crowd is going to
take the concern over and switch on electricity. Of
course, there is the usual flavor of M‘llwraith and
M‘Eacharn in the broth.
* * * *
The North Channel into Moreton Bay is silting
up, and the South passage, bringing Brisbane 40 knots
nearer Sydney, will sooner or later have to be opened.
Already a modest crew of syndicators is proposing to lay
the city under a perpetual obligation by cleaning up the
mud and charging tolls on the shipping for 99 years.
But it is too much for even Brisbane to swallow.
* » * *
Since the passing of the N.S.W. amended
Superannuation Act, enabling civil servants who feel that
way to cease being contributors to the fund —a concession
of which the lady-teachers have largely availed them-
selves—the Govt, offices and public-schools have been
simply rushed by life-assurance canvassers. What a big
chance the N.S.W. Govt, has missed by not inaugurating
a State Life-Assurance Dept, before amending the old
Act, and compelling civil servants to insure in the State
office ! By the way, it is not generally known that,
should a c.s. wish to resign the service, it is necessary for
him to give notice of withdrawal from the Superannua-
tion Fund before forwarding his resignation. Should he
resign first and then apply for his contributions to the
fund, he gets “ nix.”
* * * *
Melbourne is the paradise of the time-payment
system. The instalment payment at one time stopped at
furniture and sewing-machines; now it covers everything
—even teeth, groceries, funerals and tombstones. Life
and Death are alike gripped by the time-payment man.

As an indication of the state of the soft-goods
trade down South, one twine-maker in times past used to
draw regularly every month £BO to £9O for twine supplied
to and used by the warehouses. Nowadays, £2O monthly
is a good turnover.

The grain and milling trade of one Australian
province will shortly enlighten the public as to the extent
of the wheat-gamble in this and past seasons. The banks,
of course, are in it, and more than one of them is trying
its hardest not to squeal. The usual half-a-crown in the
£ will follow.

that he has been 30 years in business in Melb. and has
paid 20s. in the £ all the time. Why don’t they deport
him? He is a living outrage on the community. Yet
there ought to be money in him as a show, if run pro-
perly. But, perhaps, he is only a lying notoriety-hunter,
after all.

* * * *
Vic. Minister of Mines is being urged to set up
five or ten head State batteries in the remote mining
centres, for the encouragement of small reefing parties.
There is something in the idea. That stretch of Gipps-
land lying between Bairnsdale and Omeo is a network of
little patchy reefs, which might be worked profitably by
thousands of men were it possible to have the stone
crushed at reasonable rates. At present, even where
private batteries are available, dear cartage, high milling
rates, and phenomenal escapements in the interests of the
proprietor of the machine, who invariably treats the sand
“ on his own,” knock all the gilt off the gingerbread.

The gross weight received by Sydney Mint for coinage
during 95 was 760,1210 z.—the smallest record since’B9.
In ’93 and ’94 the gross value of gold topped the three
million mark, but for ’95 it is only £2,768,447. This raises
a query as to whether the policy of the Mint in making a
high coining-charge is justified. Gold raised in N.S.W.
is charged 6d. an ounce for coinage, in lots under
500 oz- a rebate of one penny an ounce being allowed
on “ foreign” gold. Vic. Mint charges 2d. an ounce
for similar lots to all comers. Sydney Mint misses
about 100,000 oz. of the annual N.S.W. production
but practically every oz. of Vic. gold is minted in Melb.’
The Sydney policy of penalising the local producers in
favor of the Bananalanders and Maorilanders is a
peculiarly ignoble method of financing. Melb. and Sydnev
mintage rates compare as follows
In the matter of silver contents of gold Svdnev
is nominally more liberal than Melbourne-Sydnev retain-
mg 2 per cent, and Melbourne 8 per cent. l'n the present
state of the silver-market, this amounts to practically
nothing. The coming charges should be overhauled, and
as the regulations are subject to the Governor-in-Council’s
abouHt ’ SOmethln§: can be done if the authorities care
’95 is the record year for Melb. Mint with
1,092,5200 z. gross mintage, the value being £4,288,356. Of
this amount it issued £4,165,869 in sovereign* nearly 30
tons. Sydney’s coin issue was £2,758,000.

Melb. speculators are fighting very shy of Gipps-
land’s new fields, despite excellent prospects. Too many
rue their Mt. Wills experience—though the Democrat and

and want deeper developments before venturing another

The history and possibilities of the various
Ballarat mines are succinctly narrated in the Indicator
series on Vic. mining, recently issued by “ Crush ’’Brad-
ford, of the local Courier. More of this kind of thing in
handy form will do much to give outside investors a
correct grip of where to play it up.
* * * *
New Prince of Wales, Bendigo, last half
divided £10,125—7610 loads for 40310 z. 19dwts. Average,
lldwts. 6gr. ; breaking and crushing 16s. sd. a load. Last
two months the weekly yields dropped badly—poorest,
306 tons for 320 z.— as the good 15ft. stone above the 300 ft.
level dipped underfoot, and the co. is now shaft-sinking
to pick up the run at a lower level. Meanwhile, better
stone in the 212 ft. south level has given a much-needed
fillip to crushings and shares.
* * * *
Melb. “on time ” scrip-gamblers have badly
tumbled in over Block Tens. They “ covered ” extensive
parcels for the “buck” expected when the new plant
operated, billed for six weeks back, but it is still dumb.
So they have either had to drop their “ cover ” or renew
at heavy interest.

Deep levels at Alexandra, one of Vic.’s old-time
and now neglected fields, are shortly to be tested. A
syndicate of returned lucky W.A. prospectors has bought
several township leases with the aforesaid object, and, if
the ground repeats at a depth its shallow-level record of
paying £lOO,OOO in divs. in a few years, they stand to
strike a tidy pile out of their spec.

Resuscitated Rokewood Junction (Vic.) has a

Ask a Bendigo shareholder in the Bendigo-
Coolgardie mine why he did not sell when the shares went

and now knows he is one himself.

Britishers are to be offered the Korumburra
(Vic.) coal-mine. 30,000 out of this co.’s 75,000 shares are
held by three persons.
* * * *
Dear Bulletin,—From the Blue Mountains to
the Q. border one hears the cry—“ Go to Cobar !” Labcr
is more than plentiful there and competition, for what
little work there is, keener than I’ve seen it anywhere else
in Australia. At the copper-mine numbers of men are
ready, every shift, to jump the place of some unfortunate
who’s ill or late. During the late heat a slag-pot handle
was no sooner vacated by its prostrated holder than it
was rushed and snatched up. It’s a gruesome fact, but
men are actually praying for someone to be struck dead,
and sleep near the works ready for the night-shifts.—
Flux.
* * * *
The Sydney Harbor Collieries people considei
that in refusing them a lease of wharfage accommodation
the N.S.W. Government is guilty of a distinct act of re
pudiation. The State, they point out, not only issued
coal-mining leases but actually subsidised the company’s
coal-boring-operations. The Government certainly has
wobbled over the matter.
* * * *
The New Bushman’s Hill (Parkes, N.S.W.) Co’s
mill has been running full time since midnight on Sunday,
26th Jan., and will clean up about the 22nd inst., after a
four weeks’ run. In the mine itself the reef, which is
about 16in. wide, is carrying gold for the whole distance
of 800 ft.

B.H. North announces that accounts received
for 777 tons of ore, sold to Block 14, show a return of
£1027 19s. 10d., equal to £1 6s. sd. per ton—which should
giy e > sa y> 10s. per ton profit. But it is hardly fair to put
this forward as an average sample of the ore in the mine;
the underground workings show that much pick-
ing,is done, for the discarded ore lies thick all round.
And, unfortunately, a very large proportion of the ore at
this end of the Broken Hill lode is intermixed with a
sandstone, whose particles are almost as heavy as lead, to
such an extent as to make payable concentration im-
possible.

Sing a rather ridiculous ditty
Of British depositors, mad
Since their cash in the ill-fated “ City ”
Appears to have gone to the bad.
Inspired by the ledger-inspectors,
Who furnished a written report,
They are burning to get the directors
Examined —examined in Court.
Those gentlemen, solemn and pompous,
Will state in a tone of regret
That losses now causing a rumpus
Are matters they almost forget.
The public will burst into laughter
When Molesworth is tempted to snort,
For nothing can happen thereafter
To bankers examined in Court.
We’ve heard the old Federal story,
We know that its assets have fled
To some place which is probably “ Glory,”
The final retreat of the dead.
These hair-raising yarns are intended
To furnish the lawyers with sport—
Let the “ City’s ” last cent be expended
In bringing directors to Court.
