A Boer view: “ Ring ’’-land, Roam and
Booty.
All the Labor members who sat in last
S.A. Assembly are to be nominated by the party
at the next elections.
Premier Seddon, it now appears, has not
appointed himself a salaried, but a purely gratis,
liquidator of the B.N.Z. Estates Co.
During ’95, not one Australian province
changed its Ministry. Not even South Australia,
the province of six-months’ Cabinets.
Byrnes’s chance for North Brisbane with
remainder to the Premiership has been to some
extern, scotched, or rather Irished, by the recent
controversy re sectarian appointments to the Civil
Service.
Donald ion is Opposition candidate for N.
Brisbane, chief stronghold of Property, and the
lair of the plural voter. But Queen-street prefers
a safe Tory numskull to an able man free from
the hide bound theories of the Ass. Here’s luck,
anyway !
Two ex-sneriffs are walking about Mel-
bourne hale and hearty (retired because they were
over 60). Another man is paid full salary to do
their work, but an economical Government thinks
these two too feeble to do aught but draw heavy
pensions.
Max Hirsch has been treating Melb. to his
views of a Federal Constitution for Australia.
But since Ernperor William took to tweaking the
Lion’s tail, Max has resumed his former appearance
of a “darned foreigner,” and his eloquence is
under a cloud.
Sangster, M.L.A. (Port Melbourne), stated
recently in Vic. Assembly that when people in
Australia wanted “a crawling thing” like a
clergyman they sent to Scotland for it, but when
they wanted fighting men they imported them
from England. Sangster may now say farewell
to the haggis-and-whisky vote.
BEWARE OF CHILLS.
BOURKE. —It is much cooler to-day. The thermometer is down to 117 in the
shade.— Weather report daring heat-wave.
Sister Jessie (to brother Bill, the bullock-puncher): “Bill, ain’t yer better take
yer overcoat an’mutfler? It might go down to one hundred this afternoon
an’ you’d be froze! ”
The Turner Gov. opposed the proposal to
establish a minimum wage of 6s. a-day for male
adults casually employed in Vic. Railway Depart-
ment. If carried, the amendment would force
the Gov. either to raise the necessary money by
increased taxation or sack the cheap old men and
get better ones and less of them, asid the Pre-
mier. Which implies that a great many of the
men in the employ of the department are not get-
ting 6s. a day:
Quite possible the Turner Ministry will be
reconstructed. The move is to retire Williams
from the railways, and give the brainy Peacock a
chance of knocking that octopus dept, into com-
mercial shape. Also, another Minister may be
got rid of—the man, too, the Vic. public nave
least thought of in this direction.
Bananaland is desperately police and
soldier ridden. It costs that sultry province
<£234,710 a year to pay police and military ex-
penses. Nearly 200 police are stationed in Bris-
bane alone, while Northern towns of from ten to
twenty thousand inhabitants are often stuffed
with from ten to a score of constables.
And here is S.M. Herald talking in a
leader of the United States as “a nation of
70,000,000 Anglo-Saxons.” Say 9,000,000 Ger-
mans, 10,000,000 Irish, 10,000,000 niggers, 1,000,000
or 2,000,000 each of Scandinavians and Italians—
besides Spaniards, Dutchmen, Russian and Polish
Jews, and all the rest. Anglo-Saxon !
Max Hirsch has been thrown over by
Melb. Freetraders since the discovery by the
A.rgus of one West, a Freetrade gabbler without
Single-tax trimmings. Freetrade has slumped
heavily in Vic. farming districts, the recent bad
season having convinced most wavering agricul-
turists of the advantages of a home market.
Again the Jap. The sugar-refining indus-
try of Hongkong is likely to receive a death-blow
now the Japs own Formosa. The Chinese Sugar-
refining Co. of Hongkong at first paid divs. of 8 to
12 p.ei, and in 1889, 25 p.c. Formosa grows plenty
of cane, and already the Jap. is busy raising re-
fineries. Another rival for the Bananaland planter.
If the Labor-members of Vic. Assembly
would like to reveal a very ancient scandal they
might call for ‘ ‘ papers ” re the Government sale
and purchase of sundry fine freehold properties
lying south of St. Kilda (Melb.) Cemetery. Old
inhabitants remember that the original cemetery
reserve included many acres of land which some-
how or other got diverted from burial purposes.
Legal bills go through Parliament without
the slightest discussion, as a rule, save by interested
legal members. And this although important
changes of principle may be involved. Such a
recent piece of legislation as the Married Woman’s
Property Act is surely not too recondite for the
ordinary member to master when changes are
being made. Vic. Assembly has just passed an
amendment of it, trusting to lawyer Duffy’s
assurance that there was “nothing in it.” Per-
haps there isn’t, but in 1884 Parliament thought it
advisable to confine the liability of women on
their contracts to cases where they actually had
property at the time of the contract. The new
bill alters this altogether, and greatly extends
their liability.

opponents of payment of members in Melbourne

According to the cable, the United States
Congress is solemnly passing a Bill for the
abolition of the blood-stained Sultanate of Turkey,
and the establishment on its ruins of a kind of
republic with a Christian president at its head.
And Abdul-Hamed, who even now can probably
muster 400,000 of the hardest-fighting soldiers in
Europe, will tell Uncle Sam to come along with his
army of 2(5,000 men and shift him out of the
Caliphate if he can.
MISS NEW WOMAN: “ Well?”
Johnson, the alleged perjurer, who was
sentenced to three years’ imprisonment over the
notorious Butler case, while the influential Rofe,
who was admittedly a worse offender in the same
matter, went free, had the remainder of his sen-
tence remitted, according to the S.M. Herald,
because of “ the peculiar nature of the surround-
ing circumstances of the case.” Now that the
Herald has admitted their existence, will it give
its opinion as to what these peculiar surroundings
consist of? The fact that the surroundings were
dreadfully peculiar has already been admitted by
almost everybody else.
Yic. Minister of Health recently referred
to directions issued to the police to set the con-
tagious diseases part of the Health Act in force in
special cases only. Is this constitutional ? Isn’t
it perilously near a “ high crime and misde-
meanor,” in fact the same “suspending” an Act
of Parliament for which James 11. was kicked out
of England ? Several instances of such repealing
of statutes by Ministers have lately occurred ;
notably in connection with the Lunacy Acts. It
will not be long before we have the interminable
discussion re “ blue laws ” raging here as it is in
America. But Ministers must not be allowed to
be our legislators.
The Yic. Homestead Bill appears to omit
the important provision of the Act from which,
in the main, it is copied, limiting the amount of
land to which an indefeasible title can be got to
=£lsoo worth. It is doubly important that this
should be inserted, seeing that the Bill is being
introduced by a decidedly Conservative section of
the House. Its omission would mean the securing
of enormous areas or values of untouchable property
to the magnates of the next boom after its collapse.
The Bill is wise in principle, but its details need
careful attention to prevent the possibility of the
long chain of evils that necessitated, in Ireland,
the passing of the Encumbered Estates Act.
The S.A. National Ass is most active in its
election work, and money is being spent hopefully
in the attempt to capsize Premier Kingston and
party. So far, Works-Minister Jenkins is the
only Minister threatened in his own seat, and
much disgust is felt by Ministerialists at the
action of the Central Labor Executive in urging
the local labor committee to run two direct nomi-
nees for the discrict (Sturt). Relations are much
strained between Chairman M'Pherson, of the
U.L.P., and Premier Kingston on this subject.
Australian Labor-parties in general seem en-
amoured of the examples of the Boy and the Nuts
and the Dog and the Shadow. Who in his pro-
vince has done, and is doing, so much for demo-
cracy as Kingston ?

The cable :
The British residents in South Africa consider that the
amalgamation of the two nationalities would be hastened
if Great Britain controlled the African seaboard.
True. If J. Bull manages to so block in the Boers
that they have no chance of getting an opening to
the sea by way of Delagoa Bay, then they will be
practically imprisoned in the paunch of and soak-
ing in the gastric juices of J. Bull aforesaid.
Great is the power of Bung and his XXX
At the last annual licensing meeting in Welling-
ton, M.L., the only hotel-licenses cancelled were
those of “ untied ” houses. The police apparently
dare not seriously prosecute a “ brewer’s house ”
for breaches of the Act; so, to justify his exist-
ence, Roberto must attack the other. This spells
ruin to a publican who refuses to be tied by XXX.
and Co.—unless he happens to be prominent in
secret-society or pious circles. Clearly the State
must brew its own beer.
The latest rumor about Boodlewraith is
that Nelson, who is unwell and tired of politics,
will take the Agent-Generalship, MTlwraitb’s
condition being too bad even for this “ soft snap.”
Byrnes aspires to leadership, and will stand for
North Brisbane if he thinks the seat is sure; he
has issued what may be considered a valedictory
address to his Chinese and other supporters at
Cairns, but is trying to keep the back-door open
in case he’s routed down South. And Tozer is
working up the Orange faction for the expected
wrangle for Nelson’s place.
The true inwardness of Jameson’s cam-
paign, or “outrage” as the press would term it
were the nationalities reversed, is to be read
between the lines, not of the reading matter, but
the advts. of the South African press.
burg Star in a recent issue, containing about 15
columns of fulsome adulation of Dr. “Jim,” has
among its “ Outlanders’ Arise ” advts. one giving
the subscription - list towards the Outlanders’
defence-fund. This list opens with Eckstein
<£lo,ooo, Barnato <£lo,ooo, Neumann =£lo,ooo, and
gradually diminishes until the item “ Johannes-
burg Hebrew congregation, £100,” is reached.
Also, the medium contributors are mostly named
Abraham, or Woolff, or Cohen, or words to that
effect. Who doubts now that the whole “ patrio-
tic ” action was nothing more than a cleverly-
planned job on the part of Barnato, Rhodes and
Co. to rig the London Stock Exchange ?

Personal Items.
Hori Grey has decided after all to risk a
sea-voyage and return to Maoriland.
A Coolgardie barmaid flashes a 300-guinea
bracelet, alleged by her to be a present from a live
English lord.
Ex-councillor Dooley, of Warrnambool :
‘ * The smallest bi in the room knows me for the
lasht twinty years. ”
Frank Mahony’s clever painting, “ The
Cry of the Mothers,” has been bought by the
N.S.W. National Gallery trustees.
Tremayne Lark, of Sydney, is doing a
lot of work at his Paris studio, where he has
recently had several Australian sitters.
Phillip Gidley King, M.L.C., grandson of
the third Governor of New South Wales, and a
native of Parramatta, has entered his 80th year.
It was Brodzky, of Melb. Table Talk,
who discovered “ Hansom Cab ” Fergus Hume,
then a sprouting law-clerk, in a Chancery Lane
office.
Daily pressmen frozen out of the eastern
provinces are doing remarkably well in West-
ralia just now. It is a wonderfully good country
for liars.
London publisher Methuen will shortly
issue, for Mr. J. A. Barry, author of “Steve
Brown’s Bunyip,” a volume of sea-stories, entitled
“All’s Well.”
“Jack” Longstaff has just completed
a fine portrait of ex-Mayor Snowden, and is
engaged on a painting of E. Pariss Nesbit, the
Adelaide Q.C.
Lady Forrest mostly tells the visitor what
“Jack” has done and is doing, and the visitor
generally admires her for her aggressive laudation
of her husband.
The wife of ex-Gov. Jervois left South
Australian property worth £45,000, including a
sheep-run near Murray Bridge, which is bequeathed
to her husband.
On Sunday, Feb. 2, Treas. Holder (S.A.)
took the trouble to travel from Adelaide to Broken
Hill, in order to preach anniversary sermons for
the Wesleyans there.
N.S.W. ex-Chief Justice Salomons would
like to be a lord of the Privy Council, and so would
Judge Windeyer. They are now, it is pointed
out, both in England.
That well - advertised individual, Mr.
Worthington, high priest of the Christchurch
(M.L.) “Students of Truth,” arrived in Sydney
from Hobart, the other day.
Phil Mennell, of the British Austra-
lasian, was to leave London Jan. 9 for Westralia,
on mining bent. Marriott Watson was to act as
Mennell’s editorial locum tenens.
Haddon Chambers, since he became so
intimate with Barney Barnato, has, it would
seem, almost forsaken the paths of literature for
finance—with what result the world is yet unable
to say.
Photo. by Talma.
EADY, the Tasmanian Cricketer,
Gerard Smith is a very poor success as
SV.A.’s alleged Governor, the real Governor being
Forrest. Smith says his greatest ambition is to
have people say of him, “Smith is an English
gentleman.”
An individual who figures as a shining
Geographical-Society light in Smellbourne, is the
hero of a story bearing on his knowledge of foreign
lands and peoples. A friend, speaking to him
about some white-haired, pink-eyed young ladies,
remarked that they were certainly albinoes.
“Now you mention it,” said the geographical
authority, “ I believe they do come from Albania.”
M. B. Stark, for 24 years clerk at Melb.
Police-court, who died the other day, was a re-
porter on Melbourne Heeald 35 years ago when
it was a morning paper owned by George Collins
Levey. He was visited in his last hours by
Father “Frank” Murphy, the Jesuit (aged 90),
who had taught him his A B C in Dublin 50 years
before.
A son of the late
H. C. E. Childers is
practising as a barrister
and peripatetic freetrader
in Victoria. He is joint
author (with R. K. Dee,
otherwise O’Hara Wood,
of “Mortgaged Years”)
of a work on “The
Stamps Acts.”
“GEOGRAPHY UP TO DATE.”
TEACHER: “How is Australia divided?”
SCHORLAR : “ Please, miss, I don’t quite know; but father says the banks have the best of it.”
Phil May, on hearing that a nugget closely
resembling the drawing over Sappho Smith’s
letter in The Bulletin, had been found at Cool-
gardie, and was to be presented to him, im-
mediately asked, “ Is it life-size ? ”
A prominent Pitt-street (Sydney) trader,
when banking his takings, daily entrusts the ple-
thoric bag to a messenger whom he follows closely
with a loaded revolver in each coat-pocket, his
hands firmly grasping the weapons.
Phil May’s modesty allows few people to
know that he has a most agreeable and sympa-
thetic tenor voice, which he uses very effectively.
Mrs. Phil May is an accomplished mandolin-
player, and now speaks French like a Parisian.
Circumstances alter cases. N.S.W. grog-
secretary Bevill now fights tooth-and-nail against
Woman-suffrage, because likely to interfere with
his paymaster, Bung. Yet he was once a foremost
champion of the Primrose League—really founded
on petticoat politics.
Westralian Gov.. Smith’s son, who at the
time of his father’s appointment was on an Argen-
tine cattle ranch, lately reached Perth and was
immediately appointed aide. He did not hold the
essential military copimission, but that difficulty
was got over by making him a second lieutenant
in the bloodthirsty Perth Volunteer Artillery.
Miss Morgan, daughter of Mr. W. Prit-
chard Morgan, M.P., who has lately been visiting
Coolgardie with her father, is his “right-hand
man ”in Welsh electioneering. W. P. M. himself
having come to Australia in his early youth, and
stayed here 25 years, has practically forgotten his
mother-tongue, but his children have been taught
Welsh, and Miss Morgan has more than once
appeared on the platform to deliver an oration in
the Cymric tongue, to the great delight of her
father’s constituents.
Old man Parkes’s furniture is once more
in the market, and attached to the auctioneer’s
advt. of the tables and chairs appears a letter
from the poor patriarch as follows :
Annandale,
January 25th, 1896.
Dear Sir, —Referring to our conversations concerning
the disposal of my effects, I hope you will do all you can
to give publicity to and make adequate arrangements for
the sale.
The things must be sold. I have no other means to
satisfy the claims against myself and to provide the neces-
saries of life for those dependent upon me.
Yours faithfully,
(Signed) HENRY PARKES.
When Westralian Gov. Gerard Smith
stood to represent Hull in the Commons, during
the 1892 elections, he was a virulent Unionist, and
at a mass meeting, on the eve of polling-day, he
unfurled a gigantic Union Jack, and waved it
amidst great anti-Home-Rule enthusiasm. But
suddenly the cheers changed to laughter; a com-
mittee-man snatched the flag out of his hand and
tore from its folds a large placard marked “For
Sale,” which the shopkeeper from whom the
bunting was bought had forgotten to remove.
Next day Smith was defeated, a fact which he
always ascribes to that careless shopkeeper.
Eady, the Tasmanian cricketer, has been
put into the Australian Eleven more for what he
promises than for what he has done. He is a 24-
year-old athlete —cricketer, footballer, swimmer—
and a six-months-old lawyer. He stands 6ft. 4in.,
and is an unassuming giant of good nerve and
stamina. He can begin bowling top pace at noon
and at six be still bowling it. Herein he differs
from Jonah Jones, whose first six overs come like
cannon-shot, and who gets slower and slower till
his best is the pace of the old-man game of bowls.
Still, Eady’s fastest is far from Jones’s best rock-
’em-in-uns, and is about the pace of Albert Trott’s
wind-jammer. Eady is a good field and bat as
well as bowler ; his big performances are two cen-
turies in Tas. v. Vic. second eleven last season and
156 against M.C.C. the other day. His pace
should make him a dangerous bowler on fiery
English wickets, but on good English wickets
Grace, Shewsbury, Gunn, Stoddart, Ward, Abel,
Reid, Jackson—used to the scientific fast bowling
of Richardson, Lockwood and Mold—will probably
smile if Eady’s side has the temerity to play him.
The relatives of Dr. Lang are of opinion
that the time has not yet arrived for the compila-
tion of his biography from his family papers. If
the family wait until the old warrior’s ‘ Life can
be issued without giving offence to some one or
other section of the community, it will wait till
Lang’s statue descends from its pedestal in Wyn-
yard Square.
A recen) sxd event up North recalls (by
contrast) a story related of the late Mr. Justice
Higinbotham. A young lady, a friend of the
family, once said to him at his residence late on a
wet night as he put on his hat to see her to the
train : “ Never mind, your Honor, the housemaid
will come.” “You forget, my dear,” said the
considerate old Judge, “that the housemaid is
a woman.”
Gov. Brassey had real bad luck on his
Tasmanian trip. Crossing the Straits he got a
bit of funnel in his eye, which had to be extracted
in Hobart. On landing, his daughter was thrown
from her bicycle. At Norfolk Bay, the sea was
so rough that he could not communicate with
Admiral Bridge, though he badly wanted to. The
Sunbeam had to be towed up the river like a
mud-lighter, instead of putting on style. He and
the Chief Justice got lost in a fog on the
“ploughed field,” Mt. Wellington. Finally, the
Sunbeam ran aground, and scraped copper off her
bottom.

Hickman Molesworth,
now Judge of Insolvency
(Vic.), once defended a man
for stealing a neighbor’s
cow. The case was as clear
as day against accused, but
he was popular, while pro-
secutor wasn’t. Verdict,
“Not guilty.” “How-
ever did you come to let that
fellow off ? ” the Crown-
Prosecutor afterwards
asked the foreman of the
jury. “Faith, didn’t he
have to pay Counsellor
Molesworth 20 guineas to
defend him, and isn t that
enough for any man’s cow ? ”
When last heard from, N.S.W. Attorney-
General Want, and his chaplain, attorney Murray
White, were en route to Manilla. On the return
of his lord and master, Jack Want, Premier Reid
will take “ a lengthened holiday ” —in England,
where the baubles' grow ?
The readers of the S.M. Herald’s leading
article on the Armenian question the other day
received an electric-shock. For once “ Granny ”
took a decided attitude with regard to a public
question, and came out with a leader strongly con-
demnatory of Salisbury’s policy. It certainly was
not from the pen of any of the usual contributors ;
that is proved by the style, the quality of the
matter, the decided view taken, and the distinctly
Gladstonian bias of the whole—moreover, it con-
tained an apt poetical quotation. Who wrote it
then?
H. C. E. Childers, ex-Victorian pensioner,
on every occasion when he was thrown out of
British Ministerial office, almost on the instant
notified the Vic. Agent-General that “ he desired
again to be pleasantly remembered by Victorians.”
This was his way of suggesting that he was once
more a parasite of the Cabbage-garden. One of
Childers’ claims, valid, but least recognised (his
position in the matter was championed by G. W.
Rusden, the historian, whose advocacy is enough to
damn any cause or any man) was to be the founder
of Melb. University.
Tagli Mahomet, the Afghan merchant
murdered at Coolgardie by a countryman whilst
at prayers, was a wealthy man. He and his
brother, Faiz (who came on to the funeral by
special train from Geraldton), owned about 1200
camels, and land in Bayley-street, Coolgardie, at
Hannan’s, Geraldton and other places. The
Mahomet Brothers did much good for the fields
generally, and were both very popular. At the
grave silver pieces were distributed to the
Europeans, who attended in large numbers.
Thus Rudyard Kipling on “ The Banjo ” :
“ The Travelling Postman ” is new and catching to me,
and I like it; and so do 1 like all the descriptions of
droving and shearing and tramping. I confess I am not
as fond of broken-down gentlemen (who generally deserve
all they get), or miraculous horses and men who can’t be
bricked off under any circumstances. ... I want Mr.
Paterson to write more about the man who is born and
ored in the land, and say what he does, and what he
thinks, and how he manages his affairs “ Till
I drink artesian water” is good, and real, and that is
the kind of thing most folks want to know about. . . .
There can’t be too many men in this world singing about
what they love, and want other people to know and love.

Sacred
To the Memory of
MR. THOMAS REIBY, 1811.
who departed this life on the 5th May,
Aged 36 years. 1823.
Also of
MARY, Widow of the above,
Mr. Thomas Reiby,
Born 12th May, 1777,
Died 30th May, 1855.

alleged Margaret Catchpole ?

O, Conk Inflamed ! that once invaded
The shores Cornstalkian that we tread,
And (though in manner mild and staid) did

If the Junction North had the Junction "Body
in its “ full strength,” the shares, instead of going- to
the £, which is predicted, would be dirt cheap at £5.
Not that the loae may not bulge again in the Junction
North ; but the chances are that the property will not
have length enough of it to allow of this. The B.H. lode
is a lenticular vein—“ making ” and narrowing, yielding
in one section the Block 10 kaolin bulge, and in another
the disappointments of the British ; in one section the
wealth of the Junction western development, and in
another the comparative sterility of the B.H. North at its
upper levels.
• * » •
B.H. Proprietary ceased to use the British Co.’s
furnaces on Jan. 27, after leasing and running them for
three years. This in consequence of the blowing-in of its
own southern nest of furnaces, thrown out of use when
the fire broke out. At one time cheaper work was
possible at the British, but general-manager Stewart has
made several sterling economies about the furnaces.
Apropos which Chief Metallurgist Kohler lately publicly
said (replying to the toast “The Americans,” at a B.H.
banquet) that Mr. Stewart, though not an American, was
the best manager the co. had yet had, by reason of his
extraordinary capacity for mastering detail.
* * * •
Capt. Joel Phillips, manager of the B.H. Junc-
tion, has been permitted to accept the superintendence of
the Junction North. The co. gets a first-class miner and
prospector.
* * * *
In anticipation of a big increase in the export of
sulphides when the Illawarra and Ashcroft (Sulphide Cor-
poration) people get to work, S.A. railways are adding
largely to their supply of trucks.
• * * •
Various parties are at work on the old Solferino
goldfield. The Band of Hope and Lombardy ground
has been taken up by John See and party, and three
men have begun operations in the old workings, with a
view of picking up the continuation of the rich reef which
started the Solferino boom 30 years ago. Over 200 men
manage to get a living in the neighborhood of Solferino.
The wash runs from Jdwt. per man a-day upwards. A
number of the diggers have left Yulgilbar freeholds, but
there are about 50 still at work ; sinking, 5 to Bft.; gold,
flaky and light, with occasional nuggets.
A small party is making tucker on the Cangie
Yulgilbar boundary. At the junction of the Mitchell and
Clarence there are a few men sluicing the sand in the bed
of the Clarence. At first they were getting 2or 3 dwts.
a-day, but it has now toned down. Along the Clarence
men are cradling the sand in the bed of the river. The
yield goes but little above tucker. 30 men are on good
gold, in a small gully running into Nogrigar Creek, the
continuation of Solferino. All the ground taken up and
not expected to last long. The old Tooloom diggers,
forced out for want of water, are all making back again
since the rain. Tooloom is described as “ a flash tucker
field.” The Gov. Geologist reports favorably on the cin-
nabar discovery near Solferino. Assays go up to 65 per
cent., with good, healthy lode.
* * * *
Tarrawingee Flux and Tram Co. paid during
last half-year 3s. per share (£11,310 18s.), and another
div. of Is. (£3770 65.) was declared at the half-yearly
meeting; £7039 14s. 6d. was carried forward. Of the
profit, £12,000 was on flux. The reserve fund of the co. is
now £22,200, or nearly 6s. per share. Owing to the
drought and the closing of the river a good deal of wool
came down by train that in ordinary seasons would go by
water ; and by this the co. profited during the half-year.
Though, as the directors’ report says, the fire in the B.H.
Proprietary somewhat reduced the flux demanded during
the half-year, yet it is doubtful if a much larger quantity
will be averaged in the future ; apart from the reduction
of smelting by the big mine, the obvious trend is towards
less local furnacing all round ; while the mining co’s are
now keeping their eyes open for all properties from which
they may draw part at least of their own flux supplies.
• * * •
At next meeting of B.H. Water Supply, Ltd.,
or on the first opportunity, the directors might be asked
whether the water-mains between the head works and the
town show any marked change from their original condi-
tion. Though it is only with difficulty that anything
relating to this co’s affairs can be ascertained, the action of
the water upon the pipes is said to be quite as serious a
matter as the at one time dreaded silting of the reservoir.
* • • *
The Eleanora, Hillgrove (N.S.W.), 492 tons, for
2750 z. gold, with concentrates estimated to contain
another lOOoz. After a long, weary struggle against
middling management and poor ore, this mine begins to
look like a div.-payer.
* * * *
Baker’B Creek, 532 tons, 4280 z. Tributors
working the old stopes crushed 92 tons for 109 oz. So, if
this crushing is a guide, the old and should-be-worked-out
stopes are producing a better average than the virgin
ground. Why is this thus ? By the way, what was the
reason for so much excitement over the recent election of
managing-director between the supporters of Smith and
Barnfield 7 Smith won.
* * • •
Sunlight, Hillgrove, 564 tons, 2570 z. A mine
that has paid away tens of thousands in expenses and
nothing in divs.—-a little, but important, fact never
chronicled by newspapers.
* • * *
“Tamworth. Deegan and party brought in
2500 z. of gold from the Lone Hand claim between Hanging
Bock and Nundle, obtained from lcwt, of stone, 1770 z.
being got from 251 b. of specimens.” News like this
appears in small type in the mining columns of the
Sydney dailies. But when it comes from W.A. it is put
in big type with sensational headings—“ Another rich
find, 25000 z. to the ton,” etc.
* * * * *
The Ashcroft sulphide co. has closed for a large
estate at Waratah, Newcastle, where its extensive
works will be erected. The Broken Hill co. has done, or
is on the point of doing, the same in the same district.
Which means a big improvement, if not a big boom, for
Newcastle.
* * * * *
Sensational newspaper references are made to
the rich crushings taken from the Dodger claim, Crow
Mountain, N.S.W. The owners have so far averaged 420 z.
to the ton, and they have taken out some thousands of
pounds. Nothing, however, is said as to the length of the
ore-chute, on which the value of all gold-mines depends.
Baker’s Creek, Mitchell’s Creek, and two or three others
in N.S.W. have long ore-chutes ; hence their permanency,
With most other ore-chutes, a 10-head battery would
exhaust a dozen of them in a year.
* * * * *
A forecast ? Apropos of the rumor re the Pro-
prietary moving its smelters from Pt. Pirie to Waratah,
director Muecke says: “ Perhaps some new sulphide
treatment may be discovered which may throw a different
complexion on the question of building works near the
coalfields.”
# * * *
Rockvale (N.S.W.) Freehold Co. emerges from
the gloom caused by mismanagement and internal dis-
ruptive, and is now “ The Phoenix.” No co., no nothing.
She is simply the Phoenix, and has been bought right out
bv a tough party of three-R. Amos. B. G. Watkins, and
George Smith (of Baker’s Creek fame, who takes the
management). The 30-head battery is a nearly new one,
and the new owners get for a song the benefit of the
immense amount of Adelaide money spent m making a
never-failing dam and providing a most complete plant.
Some frue-vanners have been attached, and crushing
beo-an again on 21st Jan. About 2000 tons have already
been mined a(id broken for the mill. Reef is now 3ft.
6in. wide, and 'is said to show gold all through. The ore
is highly mineralised, and it yet remains to be seen if con-
centration is the proper treatment, or whether chlorina-
tibn or cyanide will have to be resorted to.
Brief but accurate report on the Shoalhaven
River wash : “Plenty of gold but too much dirt.
• * *
Fred Wilson, erst of North Queensland and
other places, brought over Hicks’s mine at the Ninety-

Coolgardie —“ The Londonderry battery will
now resume crushing its own stone, running full time.
There is plenty of water.” Perhaps ; though that is hard
to believe; but is there plenty of payable stone ? A co.
with a capital of £700,000 crushing other people’s in pre-
ference to its own stone is a grim satire. The Plunder-
derry has been crushing for Burbank’s Birthday Gift,
situated about three miles away; 500 tons produced
nearly 2000 oz. If anything like this average can be kept
up it will be a div.-payer, notwithstanding its ponderous
capital. Burbank, a little man, found it on his birthday.
He was knocked out, fell asleep, dreamt of finding some-
thing rich, and when he awoke he found on an outcrop
some specimens. He thanked Heaven, and, as it was his
birthday, he so named the mine. That’s his own story,
anyhow. Burbank lived to see the mine floated for a big
sum in London, and the other day, in Perth —he got
married.
* * » *
The Great Boulder jumping-case has been with-
drawn. “ Counsel for the company made strong remarks
against Zollner, the jumper.” Zollner has had his joke,
anyhow. That is the quickest way to amend the law re-
lating to jumping. The curse of Australian mining is
the system giving the whole mine to the successful in-
former. The N.S.W. Mining Conference might consider
this matter.

To man all the leases within seven miles of Kal-
goorlie will require close on 20,000 men. But how many
lessees are prepared to exploit their leases ?
• * * •
Mr. Barclay, who has been appointed consult-
ing manager (at a salary of £5OO a year) to the Ernest
Giles W.A. Gold-Prospecting Co., reports from Coolgardie
that “ the prospects of the mine are still favorable, and
he hopes to be able to effect a sale of the property at an
early date.” The manager’s report consists of two para-
graphs. No. 1 shaft has been sunk to a depth of 18ft.
No. 2 shaft 38ft., and a windlass has been fixed. There
are about 20 tons at grass estimated to crush about 2oz.
to the ton. In the equipment of the expedition £1457
10s. 6d. was spent, and of the 10 camels costing £770 five
are dead, and one sold for £37. Salaries and wages on
the field, £607. Office expenses, £590. General expenses,
£712, including £313 for travelling and hotel charges. So
the finding of the Nellie Farren has cost this co. £3776.

Experienced Vic. miner: “To take the W.A.
diggings as a whole, I allow one in every 100 leases to get
patches of gold, one in every 1000 payable gold, and one
in every 10,000 above the average.” Looks near the
mark.
* * * *
In a leading article headed “ God send us a
fool-killer,” Coolgardie Mining Review trenchantly deals
with the W.A. Govt, water-supply to the goldfields :
“Premier Forrest might have allowed the experience of
others to be a guide to him. He might have looked at the
dams excavated on some of our mines and he would have
known that they had never held enough water to pay for
the tobacco smoked by the overseer of those works. How
much water has there ever been in the splendid and costly
dams on Bayley’s Reward ? How much will there ever be ?
Dams or tanks are next to useless here. Does he know
that to fill the 7,000,000 gallon dam at Niagara it will take
a rainfall of lin. over 24 miles of country and every drop
will have to be conserved ? The water policy of the
Premier is on a par with the rest of his actions. He knows
well enough that we have no rainfall to make the dams
valuable. If his best effort is a dry dam, then his best
effort is dam bad.”
* * * *
“Outis,” in Sydney D.T., says that Dr.
Schmeisser made no secret of his conviction that the
world’s gold-supply is going to be very appreciably added
to by the land of the Black Swan. The writer has heard
lots of stories put into Dr. Schmeisser’s mouth by people
interested in the W.A. boom. It will soon be known that
he condemned a vast number of well-advertised W.A.
mines and praised precious few.
“Moses,” in London Truth, referring to the
condemnatory report of Dr. Schmeisser on the Black Flag
mine, says the chairman of the meeting wisely tore it into
shreds as soon as he read it. Reference is then made to
the mining superiority of Mr. J. Gifford, who also reported
on this mine, and whose report, it is assumed, was much
more favorable. Schmeisser is now described as a “ mere
theorist.” In a little while we shall hear the Coolgardie
weaklings howling at the Professor. The question is, did
Gifford know when he reported that the Black Flag was
going to be loaded with a capital of £600,000? As it
happens, he did not.

Premier Forrest —“I hope we shall always be
loyal to the Great Mother Country. (Prolonged cheering
and waving of hands.) I hope that we shall never forget
that the Grand Old Country is the home of our fathers,
and I hope we shall be true to the traditions of our race.”
(More cheering.) The Grand Old Country will realise the
meaning of all this when the W.A. gold-boom is being
cleaned up. No more boom, no more lbyalty. “No
more blanky rum, no more blanky hallelujah.”

* * * *
The Tasmania, the boss gold-mine of an island
of that name, 4520 tons, 33060 z. Quantity and quality.
“ Bravo ” Jones having failed to sell the mine in London,
the directors have now decided to sink the present work-
ing shaft another 200 ft., and to start a new main and
pumping shaft, to be sunk 1000 ft before opening out.
This mine has a great water difficulty—too much water.
At one time it was thought that the Tamar River was the
source of it. It was mastered after the expenditure of
£50,000 in pumping-gear and after many failures. The
deeper they go the more water, and they are now prepar-
ing in the right way to meet it.

Alluvial-mining in Clermont (Q). for ’95 would
have been prosperous but for drought. “ Good work,” says
the warden, “ is being done on Black Ridge and vicinity,
where 800 men have found employment this last six
months. One party of four men have a shaft down to
180 ft., being now in ‘ tish.’ ” This is the deepest shaft in
alluvial sunk by private enterprise on Peak Downs.

» * * «
J. J. East, ex-Registrar of S.A. School of Mines,
and now manager of Hannan’s Central, explains the Kal-
goorlie gold formations : There are four occurrences of
gold here, which I classify as follows : 1. Slug gold— i.e,,
nuggets or vein gold. 2. Pepper gold— i.e., very fine
rounded seeds or pellets. 3. Leaf gold-i.e., thin plates
in the partings. 4. Mustard gold—i.e., an amorphous
powder looking like mustard. No. 2 appears to have been
the earliest and No. 4 the latest of the formations, and so
far as present developments have gone this theory fits in
with observed facts. I consider the origin of the gold in
the first place to have been in pyrites which occur in large
nests and bunches in sedimentary rock. Intrusive rocks
tilted the whole up nearly vertically, and then surface
waters percolating down decomposed the pyrites. Capil-
lary attraction returned the water to the surface, carrying
the iron with it and leaving the gold behind as pepper
gold ; subsequently cross Assures occurred into which hot
waters carried true quartz and the slug gold (these
are the ‘ leaders ’). The main lode is poor where
these leaders join. The leaf gold has the same
origin. The mustard gold is at present an obscure
subject, and is usually a shallow occurrence. It will
thus be seen that all the lodes here must be regarded as
contact deposits, and mining in them will be very' erratic
and at times unsatisfactory. The oxidation of iron in the
intrusive rocks also simulates lodes, and has in some cases
caused much disappointment. Especially near the lakes
has disappointment been met with, where the deposits of
swamp ironstone have been mistaken for lode formations
and Aoated into mining companies. From a miner’s
standpoint, the value of a mine is the softness of the
ground and the number of leaders workable. The whole
must be excavated, and the prosperity or otherwise of the
mine is dependent on the average value in gold of the
material raised. There are known to be 24 square miles of
formations as described in this Aeld, and its future is
established, but a dump must come , however, because
anticipations are at present so extravagant.”
* « * *
Kauri gum—which is unearthed only in two
districts, the M.L. northern peninsula, from Auckland to
Cape Maria Van Diemen ; and Coromandel peninsula,
from Thames to C. Colville—is one of the main causes of
Auckland being the most populous town in M.L. Since it
Arst became a marketable commodity, £8,000,000 worth
has been extracted. The return for ’95 was £412,830,
which is nearly double the value of Auckland’s gold ex-
export, and more than the value of the annual output of
coal for the whole countrv. The average price is £54 per
ton, and most of the fossil goes to New York to be made
into varnish. It is got from swamps and bare ranges,
from which the parent trees have for long ages disap-
peared ; also, among the roots and between the limbs of
living trees—but the latter sort is of an inferior quality.
To the broken man and the ex-prisoner the gumAelds
afford a means of pulling-up and making an independent
living, the tools needed being merely a spear and a
spade. The same industry also often supplies the poor
settler with a welcome Aver.
The Kauri Timber Co.—which holds about
27,000 a. of freehold on the Coromandel Peninsula, bought
from the Maoris for a song in the old free-trade-in-land
days - has sold out to an English co. for half-a-million, of
which £250,000 is to be spent in prospecting and
machinery. On the Whangapona and Mercury Bay
blocks good shows have been found, but the co. demanded
such high royalties that these tracts were practically
unworked. Formerly the land was purchased for the
sake of the timber, and prospectors were warned off on
the plea of danger to the bush from Ares. Now that the
timber is nearly all cut the co. is going in for gold-mining
—with J. Bull’s capital. In various other places mere
speculators hold blocks of auriferous ground which is left
unimproved, but if a prospector Ands anything good on
it the owner is down on him instantlv, and he has to
clear. Since ’7B the Govt, has reserved all mineral rights,
and may resume auriferous land on paying the holder
compensation for the agricultural or pastoral value.

* * * *
A Bendigo man holds that gold and quartz
grow through the generating inAuence of certain mineral
water constituents. He backs his opinion with a speci-
men, covered by loose mullock for several years, which
the mineral water running over it had cemented into a
solid lump and faced with quartz. If the argument isn’t
strong, the phenomenon’s singular.

* • « *
Proposed once more to agitate for the building
of a railway-line from Broken Hill to Menindie, on the
Darling, about 65 miles by an available route. The fall to
the river would be about 500 ft.; but no engineering diffi-
culties whatever stand in the way. This is in lieu of the
Cohar-Wilcannia-Broken Hill line, approved by N.S.W.
Public Works Committee, but scouted by Parliament as
quite impracticable in the present circumstances of the pro-
vince. The line to Menindie, while connecting the mines
and the river, would still serve as a possible section of a
line to Sydney, for it is in the track of the alternative
Lachlan Valley route. Around the Menindie Lakes is
much excellent land (of course requiring irrigation), and
near it good timber, of which Broken Hill is sorely in
need. Of course, Silverton and Tarrawingee tram interests
will oppose.

Latest Northern share trick. B. pawned a big
parcel of 3s. scrip with his banker for an advance a frac-
tion under market rates. Bank notiAed co. that it held
the stock. Few days after a call was made. Banker
refused to pay, mortgagee ditto. B. promptly got the
shares forfeited, and at auction bought his security back
for a few pence. But he gets no more loans from that
bank.

The well-boring activity of the Westralian
Govt. chieAy means that a number of very, very inAuen-
tial people have bought the local rights for the Calyx
drill, and that they are now Aoating their concession into
a huge company, out of which they draw a pile by way of
a start. Up to date, practical well-borers have little faith
in the artesian possibilities of those arid regions ; it looks
as if either the ocean-water must be brought to the Aelds
or ore taken from the Aeld to the ocean. The latter course
would be by far the cheaper and quicker, but it would in-
volve a railway to Esperance, and, if the Coolgardie Aelds
are going to stay, that railway will have to be built.

The secretary of the N.S.W. Society of
Artists writes, with reference to a recent Bulletin
paragraph in re the proposed withdrawal of the
State vote for art instruction from N.S.W. Art
-Society, that the-Society of Artists as such has
taken no action whatever in the matter: —
Those of its members who took part in the deputation
to the Minister for Education did so in their private
capacity, and not as representatives of this society.
No doubt. The position of the artists who were
on the deputation is that of Dr. Jameson. The
Society of Artists in this matter plays the part of
Rhodes and the Chartered Company. Not that
The Bulletin blames it.

To properly appreciate the cable-liar, a
man must read the foreign press. Cape Times of
11th January gravely prints a London wire to the
effect that an anti-German meeting had just
passed off with much enthusiasm in Melb.
Q. Attorney-Gen. Byrnes, returned from
his Honolulu trip, lays stress on the importance of
the Japanese factor in Hawaian politics. Hardy,
intelligent, and 2.>,000 strong, the Japs, now
demand the franchise, and promise eventually to
boss the islands. Byrnes has visions of a New
Japan within a fortnight’s steam of Australia,
and in the direct track of Canadian and American
trade; and he professes to be uneasy at the
neighborhood and its possible results. Yet,
though it is declared that Queensland will not
come into the Japanese treaty, the Q. Govt, has
thrown no obstacle beyond a mild diplomatic re-
monstrance in the way of Jap. sugar-laborers
and pearl-fishers, and it shirks Federation because
Federation means the abolition of colored labor.
“ Whether it is (writes Sydney D.T.’s Lon-
don correspondent) that Sydney air favors the
development of fictional talent or that Sydney
editors are specially perspicuous in discerning pro-
mising beginners, I don’t know ; but quite a dis-
proportionate number of literary Australians
seems to have made their mark first in your
colony. Ernest Favenc and Louis Becke are
recent examples. The latter has achieved a big
hit in England and America this last year.
Magazine editors will take all he can write, and
his two books sell exceedingly well. Mr. Clement
Shorter, of the Illustrated London News, is a
great admirer of Mr. Becke’s work, and this week
the author of ‘By Reef and Palm ’ commences a
serial story in Sir William Ingram’s paper.
He has also a grim tale of revenge called
‘A Boating Party of Two’ in the January
English Illustrated Magazine. Mr. Faveno
has not been quite as fortunate as Mr. Becke.
He fell into the hands of old-fashioned publishers,
who have neither the inclination nor facilities to
push a new man as Unwins and Hutchinsons do.
Nevertheless, all who stumbled across his ‘Tales
of the Austral Tropics’ wexe much struck with
them, and the boys’ book he published through
Blackie obtained good reviews.” Considering
that pretty nearly every Australian daily paper
makes it its business to suppress the fact, it may
be as well to remark that both Becke’s “By Reef
and Palm” and Favenc’s “ Tales of the Austral
Tropics ” were reprints from The Bulletin.
St. Kilda (Melb.) Salvation Army has been
troubled lately with internal dissensions, which
culminated in a free-fight outside the Salvation
barracks, one “ sister ” being badly slapped in
the melee, and a “hrother” severely bitten by
another “ brother.” A scrap of evidence :
Matilda Richardson gave evidence bearing out Mr.
Gaunson’s statement. Witness admitted having slapped
Collis first, when he sneered at her. He had ruined and
deserted her.
Mr. Gaunson : Did you consent to your ruin ?—No ;he
pulled me into the room and locked the door. A meetng
was going on in the barracks. The captain was reading
the Bible. The door in the partition was unlocked.
What was there to prevent you calling out ? —I could
have called out.
Was that the only act of impropriety you allege against
this young man ?—Yes. He had attempted to assault me
before. I know “ Captain ” Freeman. He was in the
habit of giving me a “ kiss of peace,” perhaps twice a
week.
Was this kissing public or private?—Well; he didn’t
kiss me before everybody.
The “ kiss of peace ” has burst-up more religious
movements than almost anything else on record.
When a church gets to the stage at which the
brothers begin to paw the countenances of the
younger and better-looking sisters, it is going
downhill.
One of Melbourne’s wealthiest and least-
obtrusive citizens is appearing just now in the
character of an alcoholic missionary, free from all
appearance of guile. This unique identity is
T. W. Stanford, brother of the late Senator
Leland Stanford, erstwhile Governor of Cali-
fornia. He preaches the gospel of Vina brandy,
on behalf of a noble cause. The late millionaire
Leland Stanford and his wife (still living) built a
University at Palo Alto, California, in memory of
their only son, who died, and gifted an estate of
50,000 acres for maintenance of the same. It is a
solid educational establishment, where “mechani-
cal and other arts, requisite to fit out young
women and men for the business affairs of life, are
theoretically and practically taught, as well as the
higher subjects generally associated with a univer-
sity career.” Already 1000 students, including
300 girls, are domiciled on the premises. They
pay about £1 a week for board and lodging, all
other expenses, amounting to some .£45,000 per
annum, being met by the Endowment Fund. The
Vina Ranche, nestled in the heart of the Sacramento
Valley, 200 miles north of San Francisco, is a
choice estate of 59,000 acres, and of this philan-
thropic territory a vineyard, the largest in the
world, covers six square miles. The rest of the
soil is mainly grazing land for 30,000 sheep and
cattle, whilst alongside the irrigating ditches 30
miles of fruit-trees are dedicated to the advance-
ment of human knowledge. The entire income
from the estate flows into the University Fund.
Leland Stanford’s widow has control of the
business, which, at her decease, will be managed
by trustees. T. W. Stanford, a trustee in
prospective, undertook to introduce Vina brandy
to the Australian public, not from any personal
commercial motive, but for the benefit of the
university that honors the name he bears. It
seems that wine brandy, rather than wine simple,
is the great product of the Sacramento vineyard.
The Government has a bonded warehouse on the
ground, where enormous quantities of spirit are
released, upon payment of duty, and sent along to
New York. As far as genuineness can be guaran-
teed, the Vina (why not call it “University”?)
brandy is above suspicion. The shrewd philan-
thropist who created the industry forbade any
sort of adulteration or misstatement of the liquor’s
age. To preserve the character of this cognac un-
spotted before the world is a duty to the dead.
“ The public may fairly assume,” says brother
T. W. Stanford, in his interesting circular, “ that
the industry is being carried on under auspices,
and for a purpose calculated to give the
utmost confidence that the quality and purity
of the brandy will be honorably maintained,
and, if possible, improved upon.” Without doubt,
the Vina brandy—whether circumstances permit
it to “ catch on ” in Australia or no—is a valuable
subject for local consideration. Its first value, the
present writer fancies, is in the absence of fusel
oils. The Californian cognac has not, thus far,
a quite convincing aroma or flavor, but it displays
the true “feeling” of maturity, and makes the
consumer happy on arriving at its destination.
And the vines that yield this tonic are rooted in
good ground. Even a conscientious teetotaller
might lower a bumper of Vini brandy (and water)
to the health of Sweetness and Light, coupled
with the name of Senator Stanford, deceased.

Answers to Correspondents.
No notice will be taken of communications insufficiently
stamped. Name and address must appear on MS.
No liability is undertaken re contributions volun-
tarily submitted, and in no case will MS. be
returned unless stamps (of any province) accompany.

The first issue of this paper was printed and published at
107 Castlereagh-street, Sydney. The Bulletin was never
published at Melbourne. On observing The Bulletin’s
success some Melb. people got out a paper called The
Melbo&rne Bulletin, which had,, and deserved, a short
and inglorious career, and was finally buried in the
stomach of Melb. Punch, “ with which is incorporated the
Melbourne Bulletin.” M.C. : Yes, there are six prac-
tising N.S.W. Q.O.’s. Mr. Rogers’s name was omitted
from The Bulletin’s list L.M.D. (Vic.): Will be illus-
trated Veracity (S.) : One unsavory, the other unin-
teresting A.K. : (1) S.M. Herald. (2) There is now
little difference L. 0.0. : The Argus only reviewed

But, The Bulletin’s troubles ! “ Banjo ” was famous
long before the Argus noticed him H.L. (St. Kilila) :
But we couldn’t print it 8.0. : Rolf Boldrewood wrote
only the slush part of “ A Modern Buccaneer,” though
it was published by him under his own name ; the inter-
esting portion was from the pen of Louis Becke. Boldre-
wood bought Becke’s work when Becke was unknown and
hard-up ; Becke’s matter thus become Boldrewood’s in the
sense that one woman’s hair often becomes another’s at
the wig-makers Correspondent : Tiie Bulletin hears
from Normanton that station-manager Ferguson, mur-
dered by the aboriginal Cunijum, was not a son of, nor
was he in anyway related to, the late Mr. David Ferg'uson,
M.L.A. for Wellington, N.S.W. But a son (George Fer-
guson) of the latter gentleman is at present on Delta
Station in that region, and is very much alive. The coin-
cidence of name and place naturally led to enquiry

With an ordinary tape measure determine the width
round the joints AA, round the instep 88, round heel CC,
round the ankle DD ; next ascertain the length and width
of tread by placing the foot on a sheet of paper, and
running a lead pencil round the heel and toes. Cut the
outline thus made with a scissors, and enclose with the
other measurements, stating what size you generally take,
what are your peculiarities— a weak ankle, perhaps a pet
corn, ingrowing nails, or a blooming bunion. All these
little matters attended to. If you have a long thin foot, or
a short thick one, with very high instep, send to ELLIS’S,
where you are sure to get a fit. The proprietor, with a
view of introducing his new and simple system into the
country districts where practical boolmaking is unknown,
has decided to supply his patrons at the lowest cash price,

The New Photography.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
London, Jan. 30.
Much interest has been aroused by a recent remarkable
discovery in photography and its application to medical
science. The medical profession in London is utilising
Routgen’s process of photographing through opaque sub-
stances with astounding results. By means of this pro-
cess it is_ claimed that gallstones, stones in the bladder,
and injuries to bones in the body can easily be seen.
There are certain possibilities the future has in
store
For the photographic snapper with his swift
snap-shot.
With his perky little camera he hopes soon to
explore
The “ innards ” of the citizens he may chance to
spot.
If your liver’s out of order he will photograph the
thing,
At your corrugated stomach he will have his
merry fling ;
He will show a startled public, by his dark and
gruesome fad,
The tracks of all the whisky and the tanglefoot
you’ve had.
You may wear a double overcoat to bar his little
game,
But he’ll photograph you through it and will get
there just the same.
And even if you’re in a house the task will not
appal
The photographic pirate—he can get you through
a wall.
He will picture all the lesions in your liquor-
joggled nerves,
He will show degeneration in your cerebellum’s
curves ;
When your bile is biling over, or your ribs are on
a spree,
He’ll serve ’em up per camera and let the public
see.
Or, if you are a gorger and your teeth their duty
shirk,
The portrait-fiend will show your sweetbread doing
double work.
There is a pleasant vista though, in which the
fancy sports,
Perhaps some day they’ll find a way to photo-
graph our thoughts.
And when they catch ideas in the sources where
they’re lodged,
The Reign of Truth will come at last —because it
can’t be dodged.
The humbugs of all kinds and sorts will see their
saddest day
When once the mind-photographer gets fairly
under way.
No more in court all truth and law the perjurer
will chaff:
They will not ask him then to swear—they’ll take
his photograph.
The parsonry and legalry to dodge the thing will
try
(But journalists will welcome it— they never tell a
lie).
In that day when the light of truth shall glare on
mental smutches,
The best photographer will be the one who best
retouches.
And when men see the mischief that the photo-
graphs will make,
They’ll sadly mourn the happy time when they
were all opaque.
And many a trusty citizen will get his gun and
pot
The photographic pirate with his swift snap-shot.
ZEF.
“Banjo” Paterson, after a spell on and
about the Gulf watershed, has return d to Sydney.
“The Bulletin, of Syd
ney, the most popular journal
under the Southern Cross.”—
Saturday Review (London),
Dec. 28, ’95.
Johns, the wicket-keeper of
the Aus. Eleven, is a fair-haired
athlete 28 years old ; scaling list.
41b. and measuring sft. llJsin. He
is a Melb. native and Melb. solici-
tor ; he takes the present trip
because a man gets only one great
holiday in his life, and Johns’s
turn has come early. When Stod-
dart and his Englishmen were here
they said Johns was the first
Australian wicketkeeper ; and, in-
deed, he did keep wicket against
them very brilliantly. Johns keeps
wicket for University in Mel-
bourne, and altogether he has been
“keeping” for 12 years, six in
junior and six in senior cricket.
He has never received any serious
injury while wicket-keeping—but
has had three knuckles knocked up,
and in Vic. v. N.S.W. last time he
got a thumb disjointed. In that
match, too, he received an injury
which upset him altogether - the
insults and hoots of the Sydney
mob which thought that Iredale
was out stumped instead of caught.
Johns thinks that Trumble is the
hardest bowler to take —for he
bumps them down so. He is not
the least concerned about taking
Eady and Jones —“straight fast
bowling is as easy as any ; I can
take A. E. Troct’s fast ones as
easily as I could wish; they are
just as fast as Eady’s or Jones’s.
The hardest bowler to take would
be Englishman Richardson; you
never know when his fast one is
coming straight on or going to
break back.”
Nathan Herman, of the Misfit Parlor,
9 Wynyard-street, Sydney, publishes in this issue
a few letters from enthusiastic customers testify-
ing to the wonderful value of the famous £2 bale
of clothing and other articles, and sceptical per-
sons who feel inclined to do business are invited
to remove their doubts, in the first place, by in-
specting the originals of these and many other
testimonials at Mr. Herman’s premises. In addi-
tion to the £2 bale of men’s clothing Mr. Herman
now offers for 20s. a parcel containing all material
for three dresses and linings complete ; also several
other 20s. parcels of such magnitude that only the
advt. can do full justice to their contents. The
watob, clock, tobacco, furniture, carpet and lino-
leum, crockery, and other departments are all in
good working order, and everything, from the
7s. fid. watch and chain upwards, is selling at the
same extra ordinarily low prices as heretofore. The
man who u ndertakes to supply suit of clothes, four
shirts, six pairs socks, six handkerchiefs, two
jieckties, two collars, studs ? hat, braces and boots
for 405., and pay freight into the bargain, natur-
ally reckons that he can beat creation at almost
anything.*
SPORTING NOTIONS.
“ Gundagai ” Purves wrote to the Age suggest-
ing the advisableness of selecting the future Australian
Elevens by means of the referendum, and proposing that
two extra men be so selected, each voter sending along Is.
for the man he fancies—all the money so collected to go
to the elect. The idea failed to catch on—shillings are
scarce in Melbourne.
* * * *
Since the early days of Australian elevens, when
a well-known stonewaller was paid a lump sum before an
Australian eleven began its tour, no one else was similarly
treated until a certain Melbourneite asked for, and re-
ceived, a guarantee of £4OO ere he would sign the articles
of the last team. Yet he is now indignant at members of
the ’O6 Eleven wishing to take Lyons on the ground that,
as a big hitter, he would attract gate-money.
* * * *
“ Lightning ” Jones, who has always been
cracked up by his club-mates as a batsman, on Saturday
week against North Adelaide made 87 runs in 50min.
“Jonah” will be far from bottom in the Aus. Eleven
batting-list if he shows his true form in England.
* * * »
Eerris (now a clerk in the Commercial Union
Insurance office, Adelaide) played for Norwood against
Adelaide Feb. 1, and bowled nearly all day, taking two
wickets for 49 run's. He has filled out a little since he
left Australia, and seems to have deteriorated in .bowling
and marvellously improved as a batsman.
* * * *
Melb. Sports Co. displays a photo, of the
lustrous Australian cricket-team of ’B2 for England :
Murdoch, Spoiforth, Black ham, Giffen, Palmer, M‘l>onnell,
Massie, Bonuor, Horan, Bannerman, Boyle, Garrett,
Jones. Giffen has not held out quite so long as Black-
ham, but as the survivor of this lot he should be proud.
* * » «
“Jack” Worrall, playing for Carlton against
Melb. University last week, carried out his bat for 415
runs, an Australian record (as is also Carlton’s innings of
924). Two Saturdays were occupied in compiling the big
score. In 1892 Melbourne scored 82S against Essendon,
and last Saturday E. Melb. knocked up 876 against Rich-
mond, and Melb. scored 633 against St. Kilda.
»—* « *
Worrall went ’Ome in ’BB, but shaped indiffer-
ently. On his record he should have been picked in the
last Vic. team that beat N.S.W. His omission was, in the
light of former and subsequent events, a glaring mistake.
A big number turned up on Saturday to see
Turner bowl on Sydney Domain. Rain came along as his
side was taking the field, and the crowd went home dis-
appointed.
• * * *
Will the Australian Eleven team really play an
eleven picked from those left out? It had better be
careful. Defeat would utterly ruin its ’Ome prospects
financially.
* * » *
Already the Great Australian Thirst is dogging
one of the Australian representative eleven.
« * * •
A Williamstown junior cricketer, “ Yorkey ”
Dyson, took four wickets with four consecutive balls,
playing with the A.N. A. team of his suburb against the
Hamilton branch last Saturday.
* * * *
The much-mashed female who worships the racy
cyclist was more in evidence than ever at the recent
Melb. L.V.W. carnival, some of her even going to the
length of pelting the particular hero with flowers. A
couple of bunches of the gaudy vegetables placed well on
the track would provide material for a thrilling tragedy.
The ground officials should keep an eye on these young
women, and let them restrain their impetuousity until
the evening stroll with the demigod of their fancy.
♦ » * *
The statement that Zimmerman means to con-
fine himself henceforth to the making of records is very
amusing. A man who can make records might as well
race against men, as well as against time, whilst he is
about it. But Zim. will break no more watches, anyhow.
* * * *
Small boy MTnnes, said to be only 18 years old,
looks like and rides with the judgment of a veteran
jockey. In the L.V.W. Cup he was an easy second in his
heat, and though the heat was the slowest of the five, yet
he got off his machine with such a 16in.-collar smile that
the writer would not be surprised if in the final he had a
bit up his sleeve.
Photo, by Kerry.
Wicket-keeper JOHNS, of the Australian Representative Eleven.
How much muscle ! how little head ! The
writer heard Parsons say a few days ago—“ Most of us
can sprint one round of the M.C.C. Ground (a quarter of
a mile) at our best, but when Porta starts to go the
second lap from home it tears the bowels out of us.” And
yet in the final of the International Scratch Race (one
mile, run in 2min. 51 2-ssec.) Parsons, who had won his
heat in 2min. 31sec., allowed the first two laps to be a
crawl, and was not amongst the first three.
* » * *
Martin wanted Porta to “ join the party ” for
Maoriland. Porta’s reply was characteristic : “ Porta big
baby—Martin know everytink—me no go, Martin.”
* * * J*t
Scharp, the veteran Vic. cyclist, rode home
a winner of £lOO in L.V.W. Cup last Saturday on a
Melbourne-built machine. Scharp weighs 12st. 71b., and
his mount scaled under 201 b.—a record which it would be
difficult for the foreign firms to surpass.
* * * *
The Bulletin’s prediction that the winner of
the L.V.W. Cup would spring from the four bikists placed
in the fifth heat was quite borne out. One of the
quartette, G. Irish, came to grief in a scrimmage. Two
out of the remaining three, L. B. Scharp and Hill,
finished first and third respectively. A protest by Hill
against Scharp was dismissed.
Another dismissed protest came from Porta,
who fInLL-d a wheel behind M‘Lean for the 10-Mile
Championship race. The Italian complained that M Lean
and Ken. Lewis, the third man, had attempted to bring
him down by wobbling their wheels. In the opinion
good many onlookers, he (often blocked . , ?
journey) was unlucky to get beaten, to sa J’ ) ~ d t
it, and any ardent Victorian who might feel inclinwf to
match M’Lean against Porta over 10 miles would easily
find somebody to put up money for the foreigner.

* * * *
Two ladies in Melb. train. No. 1 : “ Yes, my
dear, I assure you they are a very clever family. No. * ■
“In what way clever? ” No. 1: “ Well, the eldest son is
one of the leading cricketers in Yarraberg, and Will, tne
next one, got a prize at a bicycle-match the other day,
and ” The train stopped here and the listener had
to get out. But what he had heard was enough to show
him that the Australian young man who thinks he will
become the rage amongst Australian young women oy
going into business as a poet, a painter, or piano-pounder,
has come to a goat’s-house to buy wool.
Be it known to all men that Thomas William
Garrett, veteran cricketer of N.S. Wales, is Registrar of
Probates in the said province, and has no connection witn
the Dept. for the Registration of Births, Deaths, and
Marriages.
• * * *
A London cycle co. is turning out machines
with handles, tubing, and forks of bamboo, and steel
joints. They are light, durable and cheap.
Give the cash-cyclists plenty of rope and they’ll
hang themselves, just as surely as did the over-petted
cronk pedestrians. One utterly evil influence is the Pro-
fessional trainer, whcTbids his man win or lose as he (the
trainer) thinks fit. t
They whisper in Melbourne that the joke of the
O’Halloran-Ohainpion case is an opinion expressed (and
overheard by strangers) by Brewer to “ Carslake Jones,
re the running of Dowry. But, then, uniortunately for
one of the parties to the libel-case, what Brewer allegedly
said to Carslake isn’t evidence.
Champion’s cross-examination of O’Halloran a,t
Melb. City Court seemed very weak, but since it couldn't
make any difference to the result (committal for trial
being a foregone conclusion), Champion wasn't likely to
show his hand —presuming that he has some cards worth
keeping dark.
Yet Champion’s conduct of his case struck
people as fiabh.y, and came as a disappointment. Purves,
who prosecuted, made a most offensive and personal
speech, ca.ling the defendant all sorts of things; but
Champion, who can be sharp-tongued when he chooses,
took it all quietly. To the average man the temptation
to pour salt into one or two of Purves’smany open wounds
would have been irresistible. Champion had better not
be his own lawyer at the higher tribunal. His best
possible move would be to hire David Gaunson, who is
just spoiling for a go at Purves, and let Nature take its
course.
In opening for O’Halloran, Purves said there
was nothing but legal redress for slanderous attacks, and
that physical chastisement was not practicable. Which
unfortunate remark at once recalled the “O’Hara’’in-
cident.
» * * *
Another jockey has been done to death on
Moree course, and a second badly injured. The regularity
with which these fatal accidents occur on country courses
is painful, but not surprising. The country undertaker
now attends races as a matter of business.
* * » •
A Sydney jockey : “ Yes, I rode at a backblocks
meeting once, never again. You could swim in the hol-
lows on a wet day, and the going would shake the front
teeth out of a lion.”
* » * *
Member Gaunson’s turf-reform proposals were
hopelessly rejected by a V.R.O. special meeting. Not
surprising, seeing that Melb. papers, always anxious for
reform, whooped against Gaunson because he wasn’t an
experienced, practical racing-man. The paid-steward pro-
posal, advocated by all the papers, wasn’t even seconded,
though 100 were present. A conservative club that works
on a minimum expenditure, and ties string on to press
complimentary tickets in. place of the boom-time blue
ribbon, couldn’t be expected to go back on its honorary
stewards and appoint paid officials in their places. Gaun-
son should modify his proposals, and get some special
meetings called to show that Vic. racing is mainly a
swindle and not a sport.
* * % *
After all, the reputation of a racehorse-owner
for straight-going depends chiefly upon his trainer’s
reputation. If the trainer is a highly-respected trainer,
then no doubt the owner’s horses run honestly. They
wouldn’t be in an honest trainer’s stable if they ran
“cronk.” On the other hand, if a trainer has a fishy
character as a horse-manager, his employer would, rightly
or wrongly, be spoken of as “ fishy ” also. Men are judged
by the company they keep.
*.* « *
The Foliage stable is apparently making no
mistake about its flying three-year-old, all the Adelaide
and Vic. money up to 100 to 4 being snapped for the
Newmarket.

Jockev Stevenson’s disqualification for spread-
eagling his fingers at a noisy crowd bothered the owner
of Auraria not a little. The V.A.T.C. refused Mr. James s

Now that proprietary racecourses are springing
up like mushrooms, some one should urge the authorities,
in the interests of humanity, to test the safety of the
tracks. Every week jockey-boys are mangled to make a
gambler’s holiday. » * *
An infatuated public is always ready to back
good cattle, whether they’re under a cloud or not. The
acceptance of 2 to 1 about Auraria for the Champion,
though lame, is a case in point, and shows what Adelaide
folk think of the mare. She pulls up dotty after her
long-distance exercises; but, as owner Jones says it’s
merely soreness, she may see the post after all.
* * * *
Whakawatea hasn’t won a race since his unex-
pected Oakleigh Plate win with 9.13 up, a year ago. A
great weight-carrier, he has only once won with under Bst.
up out of 17 successes. Were he prime he would shake
the Newmarket badly, though Bungebah is the only horse
that has ever got home with his weight, 9.3.
* * * *
The oldest horse in Aus. Cup is the light-
weighted, not-up-to-much nine-year-old Taurus, who has
raced in all sorts of company and has scored 15 times
among those of his own set.
Idolator, third in last year’s Newmarket, has
risen 31b., and Laundress, the winner, 151 b. The chest-
nut, now six years old, has but two races to his credit,
and it's time he brought off something good.
Clever people are keen on the Ballarat double,
Idolator and Elswick. The latter has never travelled a
distance, but there seems no reason why he shouldn’t.
If he can stay a fast 2j miles a big coup will be landed,
according to all accounts.
* * * *
Of the 15 three-year olds left in the New-
market, Challenger, with 8.13, is adjudged the best of the
bunch, according to the handicapper. It’s a big weight
for a youngster in an assorted field of seasoned and light-
weighted sprinters. If Wilson's horse is 101 b. better
than Thunder or Foliage, he must be a good ’un. The first
named, as a two-year old, gave Auraria 231 b. and a beat-
ing. Challenger has a stone less in Oakleigh Plate, and
may spin for that.
* * * *
The best three-year old that ever ran the
V.R.C.’s big dash was undoubtedly Cranbrook, who
carried 8.12, and put up 1.144 record, which still stands.
* * * *
Three-year-olds have won Aus. Cup 10 times in
33 years ; aged horses eight, four-year-olds seven, and
five and six-year-olds four each. This year, the field will
likely be small, but, so far, nine four-year-olds, seven
three-year-olds, four five-year-olds, and four six-year-olds
grace the bill. The only aged horses are Admiral and
Taurus, who may not -tart.
* * * ♦
Tour and five-year-olds appear to be most suc-
cessful in Newmarket Handicap, eight of the former and
nine of the latter having annexed the stake. Three-year-
olds have won on four occasions, and a six-year-old once.
* * * *
Havoc is thought in some quarters to be quite
equal to repeating last year’s performance in Aus. Cup,
though he has a stone more. Malua won with 9.9, but he
was an exceptional horse. Woodman was the only animal
that ever won the event twice.
* * * *
Lists of Melb. Cup and other winners invari-
ably omit the sex of the animals. How is a new-chum to
know whether such as Pearl, Haricot, Wollomai, and
Calamia were colts or fillies? Many lists of handicap
winners forget to state their ages.
* * * *
Of the four best three-year-olds in the Spring—
Auraria, Wallace, Osculator, and Waterfall (written out
of order)—one has been lame, the second has got rheu-
matics, the third has broken down, leaving Waterfall, who
gallops lustily and revels in his work. Oxenham’s colt
may yet prove to be the best handicap horse of the crowd.
*** * *
If the V.R.C. abolished the assumed-name
regulation as proposed, a number of bankers, &c., who
race contrary to regulations, would have to give up the
little game, or let their trainers assume ownership.
Disguised Sydney trotters are being bowled-out
all over M.L. Ringing-in is prevalent in N.S.W., but it’s
expected that when Crawford’s gum-tattooing patent is
adopted a check will be given to the industry. The patent
includes a system of dots which represent numbers up to
a thousand or so. The description of every horse tattooed
will be officially registered.
« * * *
Sam Hordern has a knack of giving his gees
“ shop ” names, such as Collarette. When his stud turns
out a probable Carbine, “ Universal Provider” would not
be a bad monniker.
» * * *
Memo.: No horse-reporter should be allowed to
act as a stipendiary official of any race-club. No man
can serve two masters.
* * * *
The M.L. Rowing Association is putting in
train arrangements for the inauguration of an Austra-
lasian four-oared amateur championship, and will send a
strong crew to Australia to row in best and best boats.
Somehow or other four-oar rowing was always more
interesting than eight.
* * * *
The Sydney police, in closing the tote-shops
about four months ago, would seem to have exhausted
themselves. Several of the shops, which have remained
empty ever since, are now being reopened by their old
proprietors. The tote-keepers know they are carrying on
an illegal business through sheer cheek and bluff ; but
they also know the police are slow in moving, and that
money can be coined meanwhile.
* * * *

One of the pleasantest, coolest, most
central and best-appointed restaurants in Sydney
is the Manhattan Dining Room, ‘situated in the
basement of the great Equitable Building in G eorge-
street, Sydney. It is in the middle of the busiest
portion of Sydney, and is in every respect a high-
class establishment with good cooking, good at-
tendance, a lavish bill of fare, and luxurious
fittings and furnishings.*
Messrs. Hordern Brothers, of Sydney,
have secured the contract for carrying mails be-
tween Sydney, Norfolk Island and Lord Howe
Island during 1896. Their schooner, the Alice
May, will make the round trip every six weeks,
carrying freight and passengers, and the latter
will have a week or a fortnight, as the case may
be, on shore at the end of the trip, consequently
the new arrangement makes these solitary islets
Eossible holiday resorts, which has not previously
een the case. Board and lodging in these lone-
some regions cost only 15s. or £l a-week, and as
the islands are the most peaceful, secluded and
absolutely restful spots within reach of Sydney—
spots where the daily-paper-horror ceases from
troubling, and the canvasser is at rest, and where
the dun and the mother-in-law, and the New
Woman, and the sex-problem drama, and the Salva-
tion band are all shut out—they should be the best
place imaginable for the man whose nerves are
shattered and his constitution run down.*
Scrubb’s Cloudy Ammonia is an article
which serves more good purposes than almost any
other fluid on the market. A few drops will
soften the hardest water; therefore, Scrubb’s
Cloudy Ammonia should invariably be added to
the bath and toilet basin, not alone for its soften-
ing influence, but for the invigorating, beneficial
effect on the skin, producing freshness and
buoyancy in lieu of languor and weariness. If a
couple of tablespoonfuls are added to a hot bath
the effect will be almost as beneficial as a Turkish
bath, while the cost is something under a penny.
In small quantities, Scrubb’s Cloudy Ammonia also
forms a good wash for the hair. Its antiseptic
properties commend it to the sickroom, while as
an ordinary cleanser it is perfect, brightening
plate, jewellery, oilcloth, &c., and saving no end
of trouble in the laundry. It renovates dress
fabrics, and even grease-spots yield to its influence.
Also, it is cheap and has a pleasant fragrance, and
various other advantages.*
A GRAND LITERARY INSTITUTION.
Cole’s Book Arcade now runs from the centre
of the block in Bourke-street to the centre of the
block in Collins-street, Melb., a distance of 660 ft.,
and contains the following 17 departments, mostly
literary and artistic: 1, New Books ; 2, Second-
hand Books ; 3, Stationery ; 4, Music ; 5, Fancy
Goods; 6, Toys; 7, Perfumery; 8, Pictures ; 9,
Artists’ Materials ; 10, an Ornamental Exhibition ;
11, a Japanese Exhibition ; 12, a Wonderland ;
13, a Smiling Gallery; 14, a Child’s Picture
Gallery ; 15, a Bargain Room ; 16, a Tea Room ;
17, a splendidly arranged new Wholesale Book
Department, containing half-a-million books. The
main walks of the establishment are half-a-mile
long.*
At the last sitting of the Land Court at
Darlinghurst, John Moonan appealed against the
appraised value of his conditional purchase, No.
93-26, Burrowa. Mr. James Carroll, land agent,
appeared for the appellant. The land in question
has been part of a special area, and, under the Act
of ’95, the appellant had it re-appraised, when ttie
local board reduced the price from .£3 to £2 10s.,
and part of it to £2 15s. per acre. Mr. Carroll now
argued that the valuation was still too high, and
that it should be reduced to about <£l per acre,
as the appellant found he could not make a
living out of it owing to the inferiority of the soil
and the absence of water. He selected the land
before seeing it. He had expended .£250 on the
farm, and would inevitably continue to lose. The
Court reserved its decision.*

Pinaud’s Sapho Soap may fairly be set
down as the crowning effort of this great and
world-famous perfumery firm. Pinaud’s firm has
been in the business for many years, and what it
doesn’t know about toilet luxuries can hardly be
worth mentioning ; and as the Sapho Soap is the
latest result of its long experience and study—is,
to some extent, the fulfilment of its destiny—the
good qualities of that article may be safely reckoned
as beyond all question. Pinaud’s perfumes are
already familiar all over Australia, and their ex-
cellence is too well-known to require mentioning.*
