and was commencing to be a smart boxer, whilst he was
A 1 in the salle-d’iscrime. He had to take his turn of
military service, but it was soon seen that he was physi-
cally unfitted for a soldier’s life. He got leave of absence
on the ground of sickness, and immediately the Radical
papers declared he had bribed the surgeons who gave the
certificate. Each time he got a short spell of leave
attacks were made on his commanding-officer for granting
it. All the while he was surely dying of consumption,
and eventually, when symptoms of typhoid unmistake-
abiy showed themselves, he was taken to a miserable, so-
called military hospital, where he breathed his last. In
any civilised country but France he would tune at once
obtained his discharge from the army —as indeed he
would, even there, had he been a poor man—but because
he was very wealthy tan unpardonable offence), and as
such a butt for the radical press, the military authorities
were pusillanimous enough to shut their ears to the
claims of humanitv.

Maher declined to accept the championship
from Corbett—he preferred earning it. “If he beats
Fitzsimmons he’ll fight any man in the world, and no
humbug.” But he will meet a tough opponent in Fitz.,
who lives carefully, has not begun to go downhill, and is a
tiger to fight.
♦ * * *
The recently-issued annual report of the N.S- W.
Police Department allu les to the powerlessness of the
law to cope with the Sunday-selling publican and the
tote-promoter, but omits to state that some of the most
successful practitioners in these lines are ex-policemen.
For instance, an ex-“cop” lately had to shut up his tote-
shop, but carries on operations all the same in a city
lane. The crowd became so troublesome that he soon
employed a bruiser to keep them. It would never
do to call in the aid of the police and the d’s. It
was quite sufficient if they saw and heard not. But the
tote-man has applied for permission to swear the bruiser
in as a special constable—presumably to “stoush” dis-
satisfied tote-patrons.
* * * *
Simms, the crack black American jockey, is
riding in England. His stirrups are short as a Cossack’s,
and he almost hugs the horse’s neck.
* b * • •
Corbett’s real reason for not desiring to meet
Fitzsimmons is now known. He had good reason for
quitting the ring, being physically unable to stand train-
ing. His friends assert that if he re-entered the arena,
any first-class heavy-weight would make a mark of him
after the first few rounds. His mode of living—well,
Corbett is now a Has-been. All ring champions go the
one way.
* • * *
Some turfmen assert that, from an owner’s
standpoint, bookies are better than the tote. Yet Gollan,
O’Brien, and other Maorilanders have tried Australia and
returned convinced to their old home. Experience favors
the mechanical system of gambling.
A Vic. sporting writer, in conversation with a
well-to-do casual acquaintance once, was asked the tip for
a certain race. “ Oh, So-and-so can win, but is being kept
for the handicap, run in a fortnight. I got it from
the stable.” “Indeed! Well, that’s worth knowing,
anyhow.” But the horse won that day, and the scribe
averred that they were a cunning crowd, and must have
changed their minds. At every subsequent meeting
when that particular neddy was running the scribe’s
friend asked for the tip and got it, and it wasn’t until six
months afterwards that the pressman accidentally dis-
covered that the backer was the owner of the horse.
* * * *
The number of women-backers is a feature of
the irrepressible Australian gambling mania. They send
along their money to the silver bookies, back their pretty
fancies like seasoned punters, and dread “ a bad day ” as
they do the weekly wash.
* * * »
Jockey John Watts will retire from business, it
Is said, after he has fulfilled next season’s engagement to
royal “ Wales,” who stands a reasonable chance of
winning the Derby. Watts has enjoyed a good long
innings, and will come out of it with a lot of money and
a pretty strong constitution. He was a heavy-weight (as
jockeys go) from the first. About 20 years ago Lord
Dupplin, in the hands of the Jews, was somehow or other
unable to start his horse Petrarch for the Two Thousand
Guineas in a style worthy of the ’orse. Petrarch, instead
of being a warm first favorite, started at an outside price,
and a well-grown stable-boy, instead of a crack jock, had
the mount. Petrarch was considered a dead ’un. How-
ever, the stable-boy brought him home an easy winner,
establishing himself as a first-class rider in one act. Watts
was the boy.

In 1895, 3087 horses ran in England for prize-
money aggregating £487,888 Is. Bd.
* * * *
At Trentham, Vic. the other day, a party of
three shot 300 rabbits, a hare, a fox, and a big black snake
in three hours
* * * *
A one-time Vic. editor, whose horse-reporter
was away “ on his own,” found that tips were required for
some forthcoming races, and, knowing nothing about the
game, drew the names out of a hat, assisted by the office-
devil. Three came off, and the boss was delighted.
Subsequently, when more races were on, and the horse-
reporter was again away, the editor decided to repeat
the process; but this time the devil went back on his
chief. The imp cut a list of runners out of an old paper,
and the' boss next day confidently tipped three that were
never entered, two that had been dead six months, and
one outlawed for life a year previously.
* * * *
Up North, the other day, a man owned the only
two starters in a race. He was clever, and told the
favorite's rider to run off the track. He did so, but
the other horse ran off too, and an uproar followed the
palpable- swindle. The neddies turned back, and the
favorite had to win. The owner was glad to get off the
course with his skin —and the stake. But the stewards
were clever also. They declared the race void, and dis-
qualified owner, jockeys, horses, saddles, everything.
* * * *
“Delaware,” of Sydney Truth, doesn’t think
much of the Musket strain. “We read,” he says, “about
the glorious Musket blood, but we are not likely to hear
of the hundreds of rotten-legged Muskets that are
manuring Maoriland. Brutes with big, ungainly bodies
too big for their weak, soft pasterns to support. Musket
got a few good horses, but he got them out of the
grandest lot of mares it was possible to get together.
With the exception of Carbine, all his good horses came
from proved good Australian stock. His sons who have
so far been successful at the stud came from hardy old
stock whose ancestors made the Australian horse famous
for its stamina. Instead of improving I hold that the
Musket has weakened them by introducing bad feet,
weak, soft pasterns, and a general softness all round.
Who ever heard of a good (bar Carbine) Musket after he
was three years old ? Trenton raced on a bit, but he was
all to.pieces at four years. The time will come when the
Muskets Will be dubbed rotten instead of incompaiable.
The most remarkable feature about Musket was his
potency, his ability to get twins and bad-legged, tender
horses. Among all the beautiful, high-priced English
mares imported by the Stud Co., he got nothing really
first-class except Carbine, who takes after his mother’s
family. Carbine’s grand-dam, Clemence, is by Newmin-
ster from Imperieuse’s dam Eulogy, a daughter of Martha
Lynn, the dam of Voltigeur. Such a bred mare ought to
throw a racehorse to a Berkshire boar. Locket, Pulchra,
Pearl Ash, Madcap, Sweet Cicely, Miss Laura, Rangi,
Petroleuse and Atlantis, all produced foals to Musket, but
I doubt if one of the said foals eould win a pony-race
nowadays, especially if Bulleraria was in the field. All
the above mares were selected regardless of cost, their
tabulated pedigrees make one’s mouth water, yet the
‘ incomparable ’ Musket couldn’t knock a real good horse
out of the lot; but other stallions could when they got the
chance. Musket’s fame rests solely on Carbine and his sons
and daughters whose dams descended from Yattendon or
Fisherman, or Yattendon’s sire, Sir Hercules, and hardy
old Tradueer. The latter has done far more good for the
M.L. thoroughbred than ever Musket did. He laid the
foundation of a hardy, sound breed, but unfortunately
Musket came along and poisoned it. Manton was once
thought to be better than Carbine. He is out of a
Tradueer mare, named Gitana, who was by Sir Hercules.
Raupo, a grand-daughter of Sir Hercules, could throw a
horse like Mata to Dead Shot, but I never heard of her
producing a winner to Musket in the three seasons she
visited him. Atalanta threw winners to all sorts of sires,
except Musket; and Atlantis threw King Ban, Happy-Go-
Lucky, Pride of the Ocean, Henry 11., Bower of Bliss, and
St. Leger to different sires, but her three visits to Musket
only produced Happy Land, who would never have been
heard of had she not been mated with stout Ingomar, and
produced Hova.

Mrs. Andrews, the handsome little Mel-
bourne lady who has constituted herself champion
of the Old Woman as contrasted with the New,
declares : “ Women defame their own sex from
motives of jealousy, man defames women from no
motive whatever.” Wrong again. Men defame
women from motives of vanity.
Mow Sang, of Melbourne, was tendered a
bogus £5-note by a visitor to his “store,’’and, being
doubtful of its genuineness, asked the customer to
wait whilst he went “to see.” The heathen went
to see a policeman, who came back with him and
arrested the note-holder. The note-holder (re-
manded for a week pending enquiries) states that
he received the worthless thing in good faith from
a bookmaker, and offered it to the Chow in pay-
ment for “ a lottery ticket, which he had taken out
and marked.” Interesting to see if anything
“ legal ” will happen to the alert Mow Sang.
Melb. Cyclist : Riding from Violet Town
to Melbourne the other day I encountered 20
“ travellers,” of whom two were women, and one
of these barefooted. The latter told me she was
going to Sydney ! She had just arrived in Mel-
bourne from Ballarat, and was looking for work
when she was arrested “under the vag,” and let
off conditionally on leaving the city at once.
What she will do on reaching Sydney is not
known ; probably the magistrate will give her 12
hours to start back to Melbourne—and so on ad
infinitum. The swaggy is the only true profes-
sional pedestrian. He lives by walking and
nothing else.
THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, is the largest buyer
of opal in Australia, and opal miners will find it
to their advantage to send their opal direct to
him. The firm has a reputation for straight deal-
ing extending over a period of 40 years, which is
sufficient guarantee to consignors that they will
be honorably treated. H. Newman is also a
buyer of all other Australian gems.*
Dear Bulletin, —A stupid criticism of the
Australian Girl was published a while ago in the
Sketch, a London journal mainly occupied just
now by full-page portraits of third-rate actresses —
the first and second-class ditto having been pic-
torially played out—and untruthful puffy para-
graphs relating thereto. Comment on any subject
emanating from the illustrated jam-factory in
question is not worth making, perhaps. But
since this particular article has been reprinted
in one or more Australian newspapers, at the
instance, no doubt, of some local simpleton who
sent it ’Ome, a protest seems permissible. Here,
then, are a few selections from the Sketch’s
inaccurate summing up of the Australian girl
It is in the exquisite contour of her figure, in the small-
ness of her hands and feet, and in the perfect moulding of
her features that the daughter of the Sunny South is able
to hold her own. . . . The innate shyness of the
English girl has no place in her temperament, nor does
she pay much regard to the culture, the self-contained
dignity, the gracefulness which sit so easily—[As though
gracefulness could sit uneasily!]—on the girls of
the West. . . . To her, art, literature, drama, and
politics are unexplored regions. Dress is the dominant
note of her character. ... In her domestic qualifica-
tions she is unapproachable. Hard-working, industrious,
economical, capable with her needle, good-tempered, and,
above all, generous, the Australian girl makes an ideal
wife. . . . The life of the average Australian girl
seems to he made up of around of social functions—balls,
“ at homes,” parties.
These statements the two last should be
bracketed together for their absurd inconsistency
—are apparently the work of an English-born new
chum, resident in Melbourne, regarding himself
as an irresistible devil among the women, for he
says in the course of his judgment—
In her affections, she dearly loves an Englishman. She
doesn’t stop to enquire about his character ; if he comes
from the old land, he has the key to her heart.
The critic’s Melbourne localism is suggested by
his reference to dress ag the dominant note of
Australian girl-character, and his nationality
obtrudes itself without apology. The Australian
firl, as I find her, is very apt to be Scotch, or
rish, or Jewish, rather than English at heart, in
either of which cases her lack of ‘ * innate shy-
ness ” is not attributable to her Australian
education. Her disposition to extravagance in
dress, as compared with her “ English ” sisters,
is not strongly marked outside of Melbourne.
“The exquisite contour of her figure” is falling
below the British standard for girls in easy cir-
cumstances, and the average London milliner
(how she makes Max O’Rell’s mouth water !) is a
more trimly and comely-built creature than her
Australian-born cousin, who begins to look rather
narrow at the shoulders and hips. Art, litera-
ture, drama, and politics are more explored by the
ordinary Australian girl than by the same class of
damsel at Home. She reads the news of the day
more persistently, she has more ambition to sing,
play the piano, or paint; in fact she is more
artistic. If she doesn’t read as soberly it is
because she hasn’t any long British winter
evenings to while away. Her chief domestic
qualification is her vivacity. As the childless wife
of a man who doesn’t lay too much stress upon his
meals the Australian girl is a treasure. But she is
neither hard-working, industrious, economical
(except in the way of not putting much to eat
before her friends), nor capable with her needle—
not as a rule.— Northerner.

Sold Everywhere
Two small farmers at Wangaratta (Vic.)
broke up a long friendship recently through a
fowl getting over a fence and some other small
matters, and went to law. The first man claimed
,£lOO damages for trespass by the second man’s
horse and cow, and the second man claimed £ 100
for damages done by the first man to some wattles
and gum-trees. Each was full of the old illusion
that when you win a law-suit you come away with
a lot of damages in a bag, while the other man
pays everything, and so thejr went at the matter
gaily with two firms of solicitors, three barristers,
and'an immense quantity of blue paper. When
the case got fairly started after much preliminary
legal expens 3, it boomed along all day and most
of the next day, by which time the lawyers
evidently felt that they had about used up both
agriculturists in costs. So one of them suggested
that apparently the damage done was about equal,
and amounted to something like 40s. on each side,
and nothing was likely to come of it except
an order that each man should pay his own costs,
and he proposed that plaintiff and defendant
should drink beer together at the adjacent pub.
and talk things over. They did so, and agreed to
pay the costs between them, and say no more
about the cow. Then they went home to put in four
years’ extra hard work to cover the expense of two
days’ law, and peace supervened once more.

Dear Sir, —It gives me great pleasure to inform
you that I have derived great benefit from your
celebrated Alfaline Remedies during my explor-
ing expeditions throughout Australia, and I con-
sider them invaluable to all travellers in the bush.
—Yours very truly, B. BUTTON,
(Formerly a Councillor, Brighton, England).
Witness : A. F. Beker, Manager, Grand Hotel,
Spring-street, Melbourne.
Mr. Button writes later:—“ The Pills I had
from you were of great benefit to me and others.
In one case, when three hunters and trappers
were travelling about the ranges near my place on
the Rose River, one was taken very ill ; his mates
intended taking him in a waggon to Wangaratta
(about 60 miles) next morning to see a doctor. I
gave him a few of your Pills. lam pleased to
inform you that having to pass their camp next
day, I found him up and pegging-out his skins
and looking well. This speaks volumes for your
valuable Remedies.”

Thursday before Good Friday I took the first of
your Pills ; since then I have used 4 boxes Special

Powerful Alfaline Rheumatic Pills, with Liver

There is an extraordinary similarity between
the face of old Francis Longmore, M.L.A., the Vic.
squatter-baiting radical, and the portraits of
lhomas Paine, the author of the Rights of
Man, about whom much is being said in London
just now apropos of an exhibition of relics. It
may be that Longmore is a re-incarnated and
christianised Tom Paine, for the old man is very
pious. In both men the sharp jaw, prominent
nose and deep eyes are the predominant character-
istics. I heir intellectual bent is also comparable :
though Longmore would never have intervened
to save the lives of aristocrats, as Paine did.
Pum em ! ” Longmore would have said.
Robert Howard (“Nosey Bob ”), the N.S.
Wales Govt, man-butcher, was previously a cab-
K'fT’ ® n d became official strangler because,
though he was always neat and clean and pro-
prietor of a good turn-out,” ladies didn’t like being
driven by a noseless man. Howard, who is now
well over 60 years of age, lost his proboscis through
an accident, nature unspecified. “Nosey,” who
has hanged about 80 persons (he “has never
reckoned the total ”), in 20 years has made as
many corpses as would reach to the top of Sydney
Post-office tower and half-way down the other
+ e ‘j.' , ? ls has very long arms and sub-
stantial feet, and as, strap-in-hand, he presents
himself m the cell to tie up and un-iron the
cowering victim who has never seen him before,
ne must look like the most hideous fiend in all
lophet arrived in his capacity as drummer for the
Devil to grab the soul of the condemned—for there
surely can be on earth no more blood-icing sight
than that of the big, brawny, noseless man-choker
who has come for you.” The whole world
shudders at the noseless—it has been reserved for
the fiendish ingenuity of Botany Bay justice to
make the condemned’s last glimpse of earth a
view, as it were, of the chief janitor of Gehenna.
Une evening a year or two ago, in an Australian
theatre, while the lights were up between the acts,
on a vice-regal command night, and people were
taking and looking round, a tall, large, fashion-
ably-dressed young man came in and stood for
haif-a-minute at the top of the dress-circle’s centre
aisle, the most conspicuous place in the house.
His sinister presence was accentuated by his size
and by the obviousness with which his monstrous
deformity preyed upon his mind, for, as a hush
fell upon the crowded assemblage, and all eyes
were fixed upon him, the poor human chimpanzee
passed his open right hand over the spot where
hl u . nose . should have been as if to feel
whether it was not all a horrid nightmare—
whether a new nose had not begun to sprout. In
a recent interview a Sunday Times man says that
hangman Howard “ lacks nasal adornment, but
you very soon get used to that as you talk to the
quiet, kindly owner.” The spider’s flies, how-
ever, don t get more than three minutes to get
used to him. When Botany Bay appoints anew man-
choker, it might be decent enough to pick one
whose appearance appeals less luridly to those of
the dying who believe in Hell, and so on. “ Nosey ”
hangs his prey with 3in. manilla, and doesn’t go
in a bullying, bouncing way, but performs his
task as kindly and tenderly as circumstances will
permit. “What sense is there,” he asks, “in
being harsh with the poor unfortunates ? A young
man as an assistant is no good ; he may boast about
his nerve, but unless he has had some experience
of life ’’—Nosey, of course, meant Death—“ or has
naturally a strong nerve, he fails at the critical
moment.” The hangman lives by the sea, at
Bondi—which should put up Bondi allotments by
at least £2 per foot as long as he stays there—and
has a flow T er-garden of which he is proud. The
Bulletin remembers that when it interviewed
him, years ago, he said : “ Next time you come
bring the missus, and I’ll give her a nice bokay.”

A Tree that Grew Awry.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Long ago, in the dark dawn of our history,
a man and woman met and married in Sydney
settlement. They had been convicts, but specious
liberty, in the shape of a ticket-of-leave, was
granted them on their union. They built a little
shanty on the Western road, just outside the
settlement, and there they made a home.
A few months after there began in them a moral
revolution. Perhaps it was due to some far-ott
Puritan forbears, with white blood and starved
desires, that these people suddenly recognised
that life for them was a foul and loathly thing.
A twelvemonth afterward a girl-child was born.
On the night of its birth, the father came to
the mother, some seeds in his hand.
“Wife,” he said, “these are from the crooked
gum-tree on the hill. Shall I plant them as we
agreed ? ”
“ Plant them,” she answered, “ I will abide.”
He kissed the mother and the child, and took a
spade and went away. Where the soil was soft
he dug a hole, put in the seeds and covered them
up. Then he came back.
“Wife,” he said, “ it is done. I have given
them a fairer chance than the parent, and as the
tree grows we will do.”
The wife said nothing, but clasped her hands
across her eyes, and perhaps prayed.
This man and woman were said to be half-
witted. It was held that a long life of crime had
sapped their reason. They were called the ‘ ‘ Crazy
Couple.” And yet some there have been of whom
the same was said who saw great truths and
gathered them for their maligners.
A year after, the seeds had sent up a sprig to
the height of a foot, and the girl could just walk.
She was a pale little creature with a few pearls
under her lips.
Every evening the parents took her hands and
led her to the young tree. There they would
stand, with beating hearts and bowed heads,
watching it for an hour at a time. Sometimes
they smiled and were happy, but once the wife
drew a curved line in the air with her finger, and
went away weeping.
Up and down the road in front of the house
went a good deal of traffic. Once the Governor
rode by, in his naval uniform and cocked hat, and
with a sword at his side. By him rode his lady,
and the man, the woman, and the child came to
the door and looked at her. She was a pale,
sweet being, and had done many good deeds.
“ If the tree grows straight! ” said the woman,
and sighed.
Another time, as they stood outside, they heard
shouts and songs, and up the long road came a
party of cursing men and a drunken woman. The
husband and wife looked at the woman, but let
the men pass unnoticed. She was a grand crea-
ture, rich with spoilt beauty, delirious and song-
ful. As she passed, she turned and laughed at
the couple for a curious pair, and went round a
bend in the road with her arm about a convict’s
neck.
“ Wife, if it should grow crooked ? ” said the
husband.
* * * *
Three years passed, and the youngster had
grown a strange child. Children of four are
usually full of prattle. She was silent. Those
who met her described her as uncanny. It seemed
as though she knew that Fate, in the semblance
of Nature, was shaping her destiny. She had an
old soul in a'young body, they said. Perhaps
they were right. Children of this description are
rare. They are beings of the twilight, and live in
a grey world. When they cry it is as if an old
anguish deepens their sobs. When they smile a
wintry white gleam shines from a distance. Yet
it may be the child’s unchildishness was due to her
environment. On one side was the great grey
forest where the shadows moved with the sun, and
the silent ants crept to and fro in long files. On
the other was the bloated human world, scarred
with crime and malice, with which she must not
mix. How should the young life shape between
these ?
At length came a day in the summer when the
husband said to the wife —“ The young tree grows
awry. ”
The wife answered, “ Nay, it is no time to
judge. Let it strengthen—it may grow straight.”
But the man replied : “Put on your cloak and
come. There is something I would show you.”
Then they walked through the gum-trees, the
child between them. As they neared the settle-
ment others joined them. The man, the woman,
and the child were very silent; but of those that
joined them many danced and more sang.
They looked so merry as they journeyed on that
the woman, turning to her husband, asked what
festival was being celebrated.
“Wait and see,” replied the man, and said no
more. But a convict, hearing the woman, shouted,
“A festival, aye a festival, and Old Merriment
dances her last dance.”
The man and woman, slackening their pace,
endeavoured to draw apart from the crowd. But
a brazen woman with thick blue lips came and
kissed the ex-convict’s wife and held rum to her
mouth. She thrust the creature aside and spat in
the dust.
“Hush!’’she said, covering the child’s ears
with her hands, “for the little one’s sake be
quiet! ”
When the woman had gone the husband and
wife looked into each other’s eyes, and a heaviness
was there.
“ Husband, it is an ill world.”
‘ ‘ Aye, wife, and shame to keep it going. ”
Towards noon they came to a camp crowded
with soldiers and convicts. On a scaffold stood a
big woman with yellow hair and round, red lips.
It was the same, that some time before had passed
the ex-convict’s home.
“Who is that ? ” said the ex-convict. (And to
his wife, “ Listen.”)
“The Wife of the Regiment,” answered a
soldier.
“ Old Merriment, we call her,” added a convict.
“Hold your tongue, Swiper, or I’ll slice it,”
said the soldier.
“Why do they hang her?” asked the ex-convict.
The convict made a coarse joke and ducked his
head to avoid the soldier’s musket. But the mili-
tary man laughed, nevertheless, and called the
convict “a damned scurvy nag.” Everyone
seemed happy except the man, the woman and the
child.
“ She struck the sergeant with his bayonet.”
And now all eyes were turned on the grand
animal standing on the scaffold and the pale
chaplain beside her. As they fixed the rope
about her neck, the chaplain whispered in her ear.
She paid no heed to his words, but threw back her
golden head, swept the crowd with her large eyes,
and sang a song of the “ Currency Lass.” In this
wise they shoved her off the scaffold. They did
things rudely in those days. But they do the
same things now. It is only in the polish they
have added that the difference lies.
When the crowd had broken up and gone the
husband said :
“ She was once a girl.”
“ And perhaps innocent,” said the wife.
“ Nay, never that.”
“ Who knows ? ” the wife answered—“ some are
born white.”
“Yet, whether or no, it is better she hangs
there,” replied the husband.
“ Had fairer things happened, husband, she
might have been a good woman. ”
“They did not,” said the man; “but instead
the wind hailed always from the one quarter, and
blew the tree awry.”
Then they looked at the child, and a chill
blackness swam before them. The woman clenched
her hands.
A strong nor’-easter blew, and the sky was
marbled with a stretch of cloud. They turned
about and faced homeward.
That night, as they sat by the open door, the
man and woman talked.
“ Wife, are you satisfied ?”
“ Is there no escape ? ” she asked.
“None.”
“ No God ? ”
“ Nay, I do not know,” said the man, “ I have
never seen Him, nor felt His hand.”
The sky had grown black as ink. One level
leaden cloud roofed the earth. As they paused,
staring at each other through the dark, they felt
the nor’-east wind was dying. It was soughing
like a lost wind with a low, dreadful plaining that
hinted of unquiet waters and dead men on frothy
seas.
The man rose.
“ Wife, cursed be the hour that made the thing
necessary ! ”
“ Husband,” the woman answered, “ we were
thoughtless. ”
“ It is no crime we do, they cannot call it crime.
The crime would be in not doing it.”
“ Husband, perhaps we are wrong.”
But the husband took two steps towards the
little room where the child slept.
“ Wife,” he said, “shall I ? ”
“ I love her,” the woman answered.
“ And I,” said the husband, “ else there would
be no need for this.”
The wind was dead now, and no leaf stirred.
The man and woman heard their own breathing,
and it was quick and loud.
Again the husband said :
“ Wife, shall I ? ”
The wife answered :
“ She is young and pretty.”
“Remember the Wife of the Regiment,” said
the man.
“ Do it,” said the woman shortly.
* * * *
An hour later they came to the settlement.
“We have killed our child,” they said.
“Why? ” asked an officer.
“ Because a tree grew crooked.”

Makes the very best sunbonnet

THE DIPSOMANIAC.
Dear Bulletin, — Re N.S.W. Govt.’s des-
patch of the unemployed to “ thin forests ” on the
Murrumbidgee. The men worked on the butty-
gang system, and were not educated up to it, each
individual being frightened to do more than his
share. Much valuable time was lost in arguing
the question of equity. Fifty per cent, of the
members of many gangs bad never been in the
bush before, being mostly bootmakers, building-
laborers, smallgoods-men, ex-breadcart-drivers, and
clerks. Except the clerks, these men were a curse
to the gangs they belonged to—always growling
and whining ; they didn’t know how to use an
axe, and wouldn’t be shown. Any rat of a man
can, if he uses his axe properly, bring a three or
four-inch pine sapling down with two or three
cuts, but these fellows had to make five and seven
cuts. The gang said to have made most money
only cleared 15s. 2d. per week per man, after pay-
ing for railway fares, food, and tools ; had they
worked properly, they could have made nearer
255. The Lands Dept, intended that the men
should earn 7s. per day, but, at the prices fixed,
none save expert bushmen (in many cases, not
even they) could have done it.— Hermes.

At the Wheel.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
He was sick of Wellington and hard luck, so he
shipped on the old Celestia for the run to Dunedin.
He would go rabbiting in Central Otago, or gold-
washing on the river beaches. All he had was the
clothes he stood in, and his blankets; but he
knew it was only a three days’ run with decent
weather.
The Celestia slid out through the Heads on a
mild winter evening, but in the Straits she struck
a wet sou’-wester, and had to thrash against
lumpy seas and savage squalls. He was wet to
the skin from the first hour on board, and things
were bad all round. The cook, in a drunken fit,
threw all the fresh meat overboard, swearing that
it was poisoned. The two casks of salt-horse,
kept for such an emergency, were found to be
putrid. His watch-mate Bill was a born loafer,
the mate was as sullen as a Norseman can be, and
the skipper was in a state of bitter exasperation.
It was “bout ship and bout ship” continually,
and the rain drove with every squall.
He was not dry even in his bunk, for the
fo’castle head leaked like a sieve, and dirty water
dropped on his face while he slept; and when he
awoke it was to jump into wet clothes and to go
out to labor in wind and rain. He cursed some-
times in the night-watch, but he was not an
ordinary growler, and Chantler, the only fellow
he chummed with on board, had to swear for him
occasionally.
Under the seaward Kaikouras the clouds broke,
and they saw giant mountains rising blackly from
the water to dazzling heights of snow. The
vessel rolled about there through a dead calm
winter’s day, but towards night a North-east wind
sprang up, and with it came a cold sea drizzle.
With the wind on the quarter and all sails set
the Celestia drove southward —past the Amuri
Bluff, past Banks Peninsula, down by the Ninety-
mile Beach. The old craft, coal-laden and water-
sodden, went stiffly before the wet breeze. The
patched sails were set like carved boards, the
grimy shrouds and stays were white with the
clinging mists, and as she rolled over the black
sea her hungry crew blessed her as if she were a
clipper.
The wind fell light, the mists were split with
sunshine, and through these splits the glaciers of
the Southern Alps were seen, and further south
the green downs about Oamaru and the white
stone houses of that tax-bound city.
He and the mate were taking a pull on the
maintopsail halliards : they had the handy billy-
tackle on the fall of the halliards, when ‘ ‘ crack ! ”
and the big four-sheave block whizzed past him
and a fathom of rusty chain grazed his head and
knocked him senseless to the deck, the chain
cutting a gap from the bone of his temple to the
nape of las neck. The mate washed the wound
with salt water, cut off a furrow of hairs, and he
went to work again, but slept in his cap that night
for fear the fluff off his blanket should get into the
wound.
The three days’ voyage had extended to a week
when Bill the loafer saw the loom of Port Chalmers
Heads and swore he would have a drink ashore
that night; but almost as he spoke there arose
before them over land and sea a dome-shaped
cloud, black-green, fringed with white streamers ;
and a gale from the Antarctic smote the vessel.
Savage captain, sullen mate, loafer Bill, drunken
cook, and Scotty worked like devils to get in the
sails. lie was at the wheel, and smiled with the
wrong side of his mouth as he saw the entrance
of the harbour close behind a wall of rushing rain
and foam.
The two staysails blew away, the mainsail and
foresail were split to rags before they could be
clewed up; only the topsails held good. They
lowered the main topsail, but before they could
lower the fore it split with a great noise and rib-
boned out to sea.
All hands went up to reef the main topsail,
loafer Bill leading; and the skipper following
hard. He at the helm watched them lay out on
the yard and struggle with the stiff, wet canvas.
To enable them to manage this sail he must steer
up into the wind, so that the canvas flapped
loosely. He shifted the wheel a spoke or two,
holding his eyes open against the driving wind,
when to his horror he saw that the lee braces were
loose, and that a single inch too much in steering
would bring the wind abaft the sail and send the
yard back with a shock that would hurl his mates
into the sea.
The captain, with his yellow beard blown over
his mouth, looked downward, shouted a useless
oath, and motioned the order “Bring her up in
the wind ! ” but the steerer, knowing what such an
order meant, shook his head and pointed to the
braces. The captain heeded him not, for at that
moment a fearful squall with hailstones big as
marbles burst on the ship. He who steered must
hold up his face to this and keep his eyes open to
see that even in that mad rolling and rushing,
that mad dash of wind and ice, he steered just in
the teeth of the wind and no more.
The squall tore off his cap and left the gaping
new wound open to the stinging hail and spray.
Hailstones smote to the depths of the rawest cuts
—struck eyes and cheeks agonising blows ; still he
looked up, for he must steer by the wind.
With a delicate reel of the helm he brought the
sail so near the direction of the wind that the men
were able to haul it on the yard and reef it; and
then they hurried down on deck, the skipper lead-
ing this time. He came on the poop with a gleam
of fury in his china-blue eyes. “ Damn you !” he
said, “ why could you not keep her shaking, you
skulking ? ”
“ The lee-braces weren’t fast,” was the answer—
“an inch too much helm and you and the rest
would have gone to blazes. ”
The skipper looked at the speaker’s face, pricked
with a hundred tiny wounds, saw the blood trick-
ling from that open gash over the temple. Then
he said, quite softly, “Go forrard and turn into
your bunk for awhile, and I’ll send you a glass of
grog.”
Maoriland.
F. ROLLETT.
A Melbourne bride sued her Romeo for
maintenance, the other week, under circumstances
of considerable interest to the “Wild-Cat Column. ”
She and her adorer got married on the day of
Lord Brassey’s arrival, since when they have lived
unhappily. The wife started by pawning her
wedding-ring for 125., probably because her hus-
band, on his own admission, can only allow her
ss. a week, a small amount considering that he
hasn’t provided a home for her at present. He
is floating a mining company, and has to “spend
money to induce people to take shares. ” If they
don’t take shares the mine will not float. There-
fore, it will be doing the lady a kindness to take
shares and place the would-be floater in a state of
affluence. Meanwhile, the wife of his bosom
exhorts him to “ leave the Stock Exchange and
work on the sewerage.” Shares can be obtained
in the street on application.
“But Lost Labor.”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The sharp rap of the hammer resounded
through the burning, breathless atmosphere, and
to one man the noise was as joyous as Ins marriage
bells. He paused in his work, kneeling. on the
roof and balancing a strip of corrugated iron, as
he glanced at the place where he had lived so long.
The fruit colony is full of quaint dwellings, but,
in this one, the ridiculous was lost in the pathetic.
On a rude wooden framework were stretched
pieces of torn, weather-stained sacking, often all
too small for the gaps they were intended to nil.
These, where the winter winds had perhaps swept
in too cruelly, were patched with pieces of old
kerosene-tins, or wood from packing-cases, so that
one read, “ ’s Lager Beer,” upside down ;
above, “ ’s Scotch Cream Whisky.”
All available money and labor had apparently
been extended on the fruit-drying house—a neat
little wooden erection over which a passion-vine
climbed. The block was clean and free from
weeds, and the little channels for the irrigating
stream had just been dug out, close by the rows of
healthy trees. The man’s look, as he pushed back
his slouch hat from his damp, sun-scorched face,
was full of contentment. The discomfort, the
alternate extremes of heat and cold in his miserable
apology for a tent, had never troubled him. He
had always had this present moment before his
eyes, and now, as he looked with pride on his
almost com [lie ted three-room cottage, his eyes
grew dim with the thought of swiftly-arnvmg
bliss. The toil of clearing and ploughing, the
agony of disappointment when the one expected
year s exile from wife and baby slowly lengthened
into four, the bitter rage when he discovered how
the nursery man had cheated him in his plants,
the despair when the workmen struck and there
was no one to work the pumps, the loneliness and
the starvation, all were forgotten now. His navel
oranges had carried off a prize, and the Melbourne
market had demanded more of his own brand of
dried apricots, and now his loneliness would end.
He wrote home, as he began to build, and for the
first time his letter spoke the whole truth. It
would be a lonely life and a hard-working life.
There would be struggles and uncertainties, but —
and to him that but redeemed all—they would be
together. “Then come,” he wrote, “I am already
counting the hours before your dear feet can touch
Australian soil.”
To-day’s mail might tell him when she would be
starting. Perhaps she was already on her way.
When he heard the five o’clock whistle, he would
knock off work and ride into town for his letters.
* * * *
Down the river bank on the way to the
old homestead was the only place where he oould
have solitude for thought. The water was whirl-
ing down mid-stream, and nearer the banks were
little eddies and whirlpools and counter currents,
where the white foam swirled round stalwart
eucalyptus trees. A treacherous river and a deso-
late, and the man needed that dread desolation to
help him to think. In his ears was a surging
noise that was louder than the river. Before his
eyes all was vague and uncertain, for his sight was
thrown inwards on his brain, and there he saw—
chaos ! He took out the crushed letter again and
tried to read it. There must be some mistake.
What was it ? What did it mean ? “I am afraid
to face such a life. . . . The child is not very
strong. . . . Could you not come home and try
for some employment here ? ” These sentences
stood out black and cruel, but he could read no
more.
* * * *
There were voices behind him as some
men ran down the bank.
“ Come and have a swim,” said one who came
close. “ Hullo, old man, are you ill ? You look
ghastly. ”
“ I’m not bad,” said the man. “ A dip will set
me up.”
It was hard work battling against the strong
current. All his life he had done nothing else. He
was tired of it —very, very tired. So he gave up
the fight.
* * * *
Next mail brought another letter for him.
“You had better read it, doctor,” said the post-
master, “as you are settling up the poor chap’s
affairs. ” So the doctor read :
“I had a dreadful dream about you last night.
It frightened me so much, and made me think
how selfish and cruel my letter must have seemed.
Please forgive me. I was anxious at the time
about the child, but now I have decided to come
to you, and, if necessary, to leave her in Eng-
land. I shall be on my way when this reaches
you.”
The doctor tore the letter in pieces, for now he
understood.
Bananaland.
M.A.P.
Solicitor Sam Lyons, Premier Turner’s
police-court partner, told Prahran bench, the
other day, that he knows a man who for several
years has habitually taken three dozen of lager
beer daily to wash down his lunch, and is all the
better for it. Three dozen !at three glasses to the
bottle —108 glasses ! His lunch must have taken
all day.

truly, SAMUEL STOW.
NATHAM HERMAN, Esq., 9 Wynyard-street, Sydney.
